THE RED PAGE.
Somebody (a lady, of course) pays the Rec
Page a severe compliment. She says quite a lot
of good original verse has been printed the
lately .. . written by T. B. Aldrich. Well,
must be content to go slowly. After all, Ameri
has only one Aldrioh, and America has sixty-fi
millions of people. Who will wager that wh
Australia has so many she will not count h
Aldriches by the score ? Man for man, oui
poetical output is even now greater than that o
the United States. Dead-alive rhymers the;
have over there by the million; but of bare
touched with the divine fire, writhing in t
divine frenzy, you can name very few. If geni
■till exists in America it is orushed under t
weight of culture; and the Muse doesn’t care fo;
pancakes. With Whitman died the second o
America’s two great poets: the first wa
Poe. The remainder are mostly clever me
like Lowell, who write forcible verses
or cultured men like Longfellow, who wrx
tolerable verses with a popular and rathe
crude sentimental appeal; or pleasant rhyme
like Aldrich, who weave phrases tenderly ant
gracefully, and now and then strike a stron
ohord of humor or pathos. Two or three lik
Lanier and Miller display something of the tru
poetio energy, without making a sufficiently abic
mg impression to be called great. And for th
rest, there are dexterous versers like Scollard o
Gilder, or homely singers like Wilcox or Rile;
and thousands of others who among them con
trive to turn out millions of lines holding muo
that is interesting and felicitous and nothing at a
that is new or memorable.
* * * *
Yet Aldrich can only be depreciated by com
parison with the greatest. In virtue of pas
work, his is probably the name highest on the list
of living American poets ; though for the last te
years he haß written very little verse. To b
sore, he is sixty-one years old now ; and th
fount of fanoy cannot play for ever. There i
probably a good deal of truth in his history o
“The Flight of the Goddess,” recently printec
here. When he was young and struggling, h
Sublisbed book after book of poems—exquisitely
nished and tingling with emotion. Then a mi
lionaire became Aldrich’s patron, provided hi
income, and .finally died leaving the bulk of th
million to his children, and
... the Goddess had somehow flown.
Flown, and I fear she will never return ;
I am much too sleek and happy for her,
Whose lovers must hunger, and waste, and burn,
Ere the beautiful heathen heart will stir!
Some one has suggested that this poem gives the
lie to the Great Beefsteak Theory, which declares
that if you want an engine to work you must feec
the furnace, and if you want a poet to work you
must feed his brain with good rich blood. Bu
there is no opposition of ideas. The sweet uses
of Aldrioh’s ‘garret aloof ’ were not to starve him
but i;o isolate him, to concentrate him, and to
stimulate him with the knowledge that if hedidn’
work he wouldn’t get any beefsteak. It is pointed
out that Zola laid the foundation of his fame when
be was trapping sparrows for supper on the win-
dow-ledge of his garret. Precisely—thanks to
the vitality he got out of the sparrows.
* » * •
Aldrioh’s ‘ In an Atelier ’ has been often quoted
but not too much, perhaps, to hinder Außtraliai
reproduction—
IN AN ATELIER.
I pray you, do not turn your head ;
And let your hands lie folded, aa
It was a dress like this, wine-red,
That Dante liked so, long ago.
You don’t know Dante T Never mind.
He loved a lady wondrous fair—
HiS model ? Something of the kind.
I wonder if she had your hair!
I wonder if she looked so meek.
And wae no-t meek at all (my dear,
I want that side-light on your cheek).
He loved her, it is very clear,
And painted her, as I paint you.
But rather better, on the whole
(Depress your chin ; yes, that will do):
He was a painter of the soul!
(And painted portraits, too, I think,
In the Inferno - devilish good !
I’d make some certain critics blink
If I’d his method and his mood.)
Her name was (Fanny, let your glmce
Rest there, by that majolica tray)—
Wae Beatrice; they met by ohance—
They met by ohanoe, the usual way.
(As you and I met, months ago,
Do you remember? How your feet
Went crinkle-crinkle on the snow
Along the bleak gas-lighted street 1
An instant in the drug-store’s glare
You stood as in a golden frame,
And then I swore it, then and there,
To hand your sweetness down to fame.)
They met, and loved, and never wed
(All this was long before our time),
. And though they died, they are not dead—
Such endless youth gives mortal rhyme 1
Still walks the earth, with haughty mien,
Great Dante, in his soul’s distress ;
And still the lovely Florentine
Goes lovely in her wine-red dress.
You do not understand at all ?
He was a poet; on his page
He drew her ; and, though kingdoms fall;
This lady lives from age to age :
A poet—that means painter too,
For words are colors, rightly laid ;
And they outlast our brightest hue,
For varnish oraoks and orimsons fade.
The poets—they are lucky ones 1
When we are thrust upon the shelves^
Our works turn into skeletons
Almost as quickly as ourselves ;
For our poor canvas peels at length.
At length is prized—when all is bare!
** What grace ! ” the critics cry, “ what strength I
When neither strength nor grace is there.
Ah, Fanny, I am sick at heart,
It is so little one can do;
We talk our jargon—live for Art l
I’d much prefer to live for you.
How dull and lifeless odors are I
You smile, and all my picture lies I
I wish that I could crush a star
To make a pigment for your eyes,
Yes, child, I know I’m out of tune;
The light is bad ; the sky is gray:
I paint no more this afternoon,
So lay your royal gear away.
Besides, you’re moody—chin on hand—
I know not what —not in the vein—
Not like Anne Bulien, sweet and bland 1
You sit there smiling in disdain.
Not like Bluff Harry’s radiant Queen,
Unconscious of the coming woe.
But rather as she might have been,
Preparing for the headsman’s blow.
I see 1 I’ve put you in a miff—
Sitting bolt-upright, wrist on wrist.
How should you look ? Why, dear, as if—
Somehow—as if you’d just been kissed 1
• • • *
H. 0. Bunner, whose death was noticed nol
long ago, perhaps comes as close to Aldrich as
anyone, and falls below hiir. less in art than in
energy. Bunner was a good type of the clever,
nervous man without quite enough vitality to dc
anything really great. Yet ‘ The Appeal to
Harold ’ is worthy to stand beside Aldrich’s besl
work. The poem is based on the tradition oi
a popular right of appeal to Harold of Normandy,
made by crying ‘Haro! Haro! Haro!’ In a
modified form, the practice is said to still survive
in some of the Channel Islands.
THE APPEAL TO HAROLD.
Haro I Haro !
Judge now betwixt this woman and me,
Haro !
She leaves me bound, who found me free.
.Of love and hope she hath drained me dry—
Yea, barren as a drought-struck sky ;
She hath not left me tears for weeping,
Nor will my eyelids close in sleeping.
I have gathered all my life’s-blood up—
Haro!
She hath drunk and thrown aside the cup
Shall she not give me back my days ?
Haro!
I made them perfect for her praise.
There was no flower in all the brake
I found not fairer for her sake ;
There was no sweet thought I did not fashion
For aid and servant to my passion.
L ibor and learning worthless were,
Haro!
Save that I made them gifts to her.
Shall she not give me back my nights?
Haro! , -
Give me sweet sleep for brief delights?
Lo, in the night’s wan mid I lie,
And ghosts of hours that are dead go by:
Hours of a love that died unshriven;
Of a love in change for my manhood given :
She caressed and slew my soul’s white truth,
Haro !
Shall she not give me baok my youth?
Haro ! Haro !
Tell thou me not of a greater judge,
Haro !
It is He who hath my soul in grudge.
Yea, from God I appeal to thee ;
God hath not part or place for me.
Thou who hast sinned, judge thou my sinning:
I have staked my life for a woman’s winning ;
She hath stripped me of all save remembering—
Haro I
Right thou me, right thou me, Harold the King !

By the side of this Kipling’s recent ‘Vampire ’
verse is very feeble. (Judging by the reproduc-
tion in ‘ Blaok and White,’ by the way, Burne-
Jones’s picture is feeble too—a forceless oopy of
idealess models.)
* * * *
Bunner is at his best in serious mood, yet his
lighter vein is delightful. E.g. —
FORFEITS.
They sent him round the circle fair,
To bow before the prettiest there.
I’m bound to say the choice he made
A creditable taste displayed ;
Although—l can’t say what it meant—
The little maid looked ill-content
His task was then anew begun—
To kneel before the wittiest one.
Once more that little maid sought he,
And went him down upon his knee.
She bent her eyes upon the floor—
I think she thought the game a bore.
He circled then—his sweet behest
To kiss the one he loved the best.
For all she frowned, for all she chid,
He kissed that little maid, he did.
And then—though why I can’t decide—
The little maid looked satisfied.
* * * *
R. K. Munkittrick, one of the best-known con-
tributors to ‘ Puck ’ during Bunner’s editorship,
recently published a book of verse from which two
quaint items were the other day thankfully bor-
rowed. ‘ R.K.M. ’is never serious : he is always
gambolling in the manner of a diluted Calverley,
and playing all sorts of pranks with phrase and
fancy. The method of ‘ Unsatisfied Yearning ’is
Very like that of the quoted ‘Put to Sleep.’
UNSATISFIED YEARNING.
Down in the silent hallway
Scampers the dog about.
And whines, and barks, and scratches
In order to get out.
Once in the glittering starlight,
He straightway doth begin
To set up a doleful howling
In order to get in.
* * * •
And here is another ‘ R.K.M. ’ ballade, with a
full N’York flavour—
BALLADE OF TRIUMPHANT TIME.
Oh, time is ever upon the wing,
It flies like a gull o’er the shining sea;
It gathers the white bloom of the spring,
And the immature apple upon the tree ;
It gathers all matter from A to Z.—
From the trouser’s seat to the lamb of May ;
Oh, time is fleeting in ruthless glee—
To-morrow, to-day will be yesterday.
Where is last Summer’s engagement ring?
Where is last Summer itself, and she?
Mosquito, mosquito, where is thy sting?
New Jersey, oh where is thy victoree ?
The plumber must crumble, ah me, ah me t
Like snow he must fade from the earth away I
No purse can imprison the green-winged V:
To-morrow, to-day will be yesterday.
Not long to the skull can the front hair cling—
The reaper is swinging his scythe care-free,
One day doth the bird in the garden sing,
Then akimbo on Annabel's hat is he ;
To time all subjects must bend the knee ;
All beauty must dwindle in slow decay ;
All flesh is grass and some grass is tea :
To-morrow, to-day will be yesterday.
ENVOI.
Prinoe, even the Presidential Bee,
A frost benumbs in the sunny ray;
Our idols fall and our shekels flee—
To-morrow, to-day will be yesterday.
* * * •
Col. John Hay, the new American Minister at
London, is best known by ‘ Jim Bludao,’ ‘ Little
Breeches,’and similar ballads. To anon-American,
these seem rather crude and stagey, and the
cheap-sentimental appeal is repeated over and
over again, as in ‘ Golyer ’ —
Ef the way a man lights out of this world
Helps fix his heft for the other sp’ere,
I reckon my old friend Golyer’s Ben
Will lay over lots of likelier men
For one thing he done down here.
You didn't know Ben ? Hedrivastage
On the line they called the Old Sou’-west:
He wa’n’t the best man that ever you seen,
And he wa’n’t so ungodly pizen mean, —
No better nor worse than the rest.
He was hard on women and rough on his friends,
And he didn't have many, I’ll let you know ;
He hated a dog and disgusted a cat,
But he’d run off his legs for a motherless brat,
And I guess there’s many jess so.
I've seed my sheer of the run of things,
I’ve hoofed it a many and many a miled,
But I never seed nothing that could or oan
Jest get all the good from the heart of a man
Like the hands of a little child . . .
* * * •
Of course, familiarity with scene and people and
dialect counts for a great deal in appreciation of
suoh work. A Canadian to whom a copy of
Lawson’s verse was sent declared that he could
see nothing at all in the swag-ballads. And he
was quite sure ‘ the form is disgustingly rough.’
Yet to an Australian these seem often full of life
and color—just because he is Australian ; while
the blemishes may become beauties by dint of
ardent contemplation. There is a pregnant story
of a man who was annoyed into green and yellow
melancholy by a mole on his wife’s lip. But, willy-
nilly, he saw it every day; he became used to
it; he became enamoured of it ; and at last he
would kiss nowhere else.
* * » •
But Hay has another vein—the Bunner-and-
Aldrich vein—with rarely enough skill to mine it
properly. ‘ The White Flag ’is his best result in
this kind—
THE WHITE FLAG.
I sent my love two roses—one
As white as driven snow,
And one a blushing royal red,
A flaming Jacqueminot.
I meant to touch and test my fata;
That night I should divine, i
The moment I should see my love, l
If her true heart were mine.
For if she holds me dear, I said,
She’ll wear my blushing rose ;
If not, she’ll wear my cold Lamarquo
As white as winter’s snows.
My heart sank when I met her : sura
I had been over bold,
For on her brea&t my pale rose lay
In virgin whiteness cold.
Yet with low words she greeted me.
With smiles divinely tender ;
Upon her cheek the red rose dawned,—
The white rose meant surrender.
* • * •
Someone recently quoted for The Bulletin one
of Hay’s often-quoted distiches, slightly altered—
As the meek beasts in the Qarden came orawliug for Adam
to name them,
So men and women to-day orawl to the feet of a Queen.
Here are others—
DISTICHEjS.
Wisely a woman prefers to a lover a man who neglects
her.
This one may love her some day, some day the lover will
not.
There are three species of oreatures who when they seem
coming are going :
When they seem going they come; diplomates, women
and crabs.
What is a first love worth, except to prepare for a second?
What dots the second love bring? Only regret for the
first.
Wine is like rain: when it falls on the mire it makes it
the fouler,
But when it strikes the good soil wakes it to beauty and
bloom.
Maidens! why should you worry in choosing whom you
shall marry ?
Choose whom you may, you will.find you have got some-
body else.
Unto each man comes a day when his favorite sins all
forsake him,
And he complacently thinks he has forsaken his sins.
True luck consists not in holding the best of the cards at
the table ;
Luckiest he who knows just when to rise and go home.
Pleasant enough it is to hear the world speak of your
virtues ;
But in your secret heart ’tis of your faults you are proud.
Try not to beat back the current, yet be not drowned in
its waters;
Speak with the speech of the world, think with the
thoughts of the few.
Make all good men your well-wishers, and then, in the
years’ steady sifting,
Some of them turn into friends. Friends are the sunshine
of life.
* * * *
To the making of a poet go brain, energy, and
education—corresponding with seed, soil, and
tillage. (Education would refer chiefly to sense of
form, taste, premeditated art, &c.) And an ex-
aminer might mark thus the exercises of the poets
quoted—Shakespeare and Poe being included for
the sake of a relative standard—
* UNIVERSE OF POETRY.
Time—Life.
Maximum Marks—Brain 100; Energy, 100 ; Education.
50 ; Total, 250.

brain energy Education total

Poe 85 b*o 40 185 *
Aldrich 45 40 35 120 t
Bunner 40 25 35 100
Hay 25 35 20 80
Munkittrick 10 20 30 60

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
EUCHRE.
What death could Antonine’s surpass,
Who, to the soldier at his side,
Gave the word “ Aequanimitas,”
And turned unto the wall, and died ?
Perhaps he said it, though I fear
Too oft some writer’s facile pen
And sense of fitness make appear
The apt remarks of dying men.
I hate to think that in a screed
A man should fable, for witli me
The truth is precious—as indeed
So rare a thing deserves to be.
But men there are who undergo
The last reprieveless sentence grim
With even spirit; this I know
By best of proof—for there was Jim.
For Jim when dying did not raise
Regrets, or build up hopes ornate :
He summed it all up in a phrase :
“ Ah, life’s a game of euchre, mate.”
The last grains sank within his glass,
And, while his breath came hard and slow,
He murmured faintly, “ Mate, I pass,”
And I responded, “ It’s a go.”
Of course, to nicely round the tale,
Jim ought to “ gag ” with every groan,
And murmur ere his soul set sail
(Soft music, please), “ I’ll go alone.”
But I’ve for Truth a love absurd,
And so I tell the yarn, you see,
Exactly as the thing occurred —
Yes, just as it occurred to me.
Sydney.
ZEF.
CONSOLATION.
Came a man to Mary Casey,
In her hut at Malden Camp,
Saying, “ Mary—now, be aisy—
But poor Casey’s gone on tramp. ”
“ Och? go plumb,” said Mary, scolding,
With a glitter in her eye,
“ To the place where they’ll be holding
Yez on griddles when yez die;
Yah ! go aisy wid yer lyin’—Micky gone on tramp,
you say ?
Shure, it’s me that knows he hasn’t, for he cud n’t
get away.”
Then the man who brought the tidings
Simply stood and gasped for breath ;
Stricken dumb by Mary’s chidings,
Feared to tell of Michael’s death.
“ But, say, Mary,” said he, crying,
When at length he found his voice—
“ Michael’s dead. There’s no denying :
’Twas a case of Hobson’s choice ;
He was loading in the cutting, and was just agoin’
to ‘ tamp ’
When he dropped dead of a suddent. Yes, poor
Michael’s gone on tramp.”
“ Wirra ! Wirra ! ” moaned the mourner,
(“ Ah ! Poor Michael! ” sighed the man)
‘ ‘ That’s his best suit in the corner ”
And her tears to flow began—
“ And he’s left me, och ! the villin,’
And he niver said ‘ Good-bye ’ —
. To forget him I’d be willin’ —
Sure I’ve half a mind to thry.
You’re his size ? Go aisy, sonny—sure ye’re foolin,
nothin’ more ?
Ye’re in earnest? Come in, darlint—hang yer
hat behind the door. ”
P. R. MACLEOD.
WHEN MOTHER CALLS TO DINNER.
We’re on a farm not far from town—
There’s just a dozen acres ;
Our neighbors range from atheists
And infidels to Quakers ;
We’ve got the good old pious sort
’Long-side the hardened sinner—
But that won’t spoil our appetite
When Mother calls to dinner.
When, years ago, we started first
And did the pioneering—
The fencing and the breaking-up,
The stumping and the clearing—
If stuck at some old ironbark
Which looked a likely winner,
We always got our courage up
When Mother called to dinner.
We’ve had some floods, when weeks of rain
Have given us a notion
We’d wake some day and find the place
Adrift towards the ocean ;
And then such droughts and failing crops
As daunt the green beginner !
But still we fought and struggled on,
And Mother called to dinner.
So though the droughts may scourge the land,
Or floods roar like a river,
We’ll hope that better times ’ll come—
The bad can’t last for ever !
And though the worry and the care
Are making Dad grow thinner,
There’s always hope of winning yet
While Mother calls to dinner.
N.S.W.
ULOOLA.
THE BICYCLE’S GONE TO THE BUSH.
It’s all up a tree with the swagmen,
It’s all over now with the tramp,
And the horsemen can dally with bagmen,
For they’re wanted no more on the camp.
They’re trooping in droves from the west-track,
With tidings of woe to their push,
For the inside, they say, is the best track,
Since the bicycle’s gone to the bush.
In old times we went to the city,
With a tidy old cheque to knock down ;
We’d muse on the beauties of Kitty
As we lit from the lights of the town ;
We had only to steer for the back-blocks
Again in a while to be flush—
Now it’s ai! for our youth and our black-locks,
Since the bicycle’s gone to the bush.
On the stations out back they are riding
’Long bound’ry and rabbit-proof fence ;
And rouseabouts swiftly are gliding
With shearers and tank-sinkers hence ;
They’re rounding-up sheep and scrub-cattle,
They stem the most desperate rush —
E’en the myalls will pedal to battle
On the bikes that have gone to the bush.
E. S. SORENSON.

SATURDAY, JULY 10, 1897.
Five Tears of Socialism.
Maoriland, unlike the other provinces of
Australasia, takes its census every five years,
and the one just completed practically covers
the 1 era of its semi-socialistic State policy.
It extends over most of the period since the
Ballance-Seddon combination began to run
riot, and to do things in the name of Liberal-
ism which caused the aggregate hair of the
aggregate Fat Man of all the seven Aus-
tralias to stand up in horror. During that
period Maoriland has been, in the eyes of
the orthodox capitalist, the shocking example
of Australasia. The heavy feet of Seddon
and M‘Kenzie have stepped, not lightly
either, on almost all the idols of Respecta-
bility. “ Freedom of contract ” has been so
badly wrecked that even the fragments of it
are hardly to be found, and that other fetish,
the "‘liberty of the subject,” has not been
dealt with at all kindly. Also “private
enterprise” has been knocked out in one
round through the Government taking a
hand in all manner of things with which,
according to the opinion of solid financial
magnates, it had no possible right to inter-
fere. Maoriland, in short, has followed a
policy which has been one long outrage on
that kind of orthodoxy which consists
mostly in reconstructing and issuing inter-
minable “ B” debentures. It is strongly and
rigidly Protectionist, which it shouldn’t be
for the obvious reason that Britain isn’t
Protectionist, and all pure commercial mora-
lity is made in the land of J. Bull. The
province has been run for six years by what
is practically a Labor Ministry, and it has
been proved over and over again that such a
thing is a mere rushing down a steep place
into the sea. And its tendency to “ dabble ”
in Socialism is the crowning iniquity and the
last stage on the road to ruin.
* * *
It was understood any time within the
last six years that these accumulated evils
were' fast “driving capital out of the
country.” Also, by reason of the inevitable
driving of capital out of the country it was
held that industry must necessarily become
paralysed, and through the paralysis of in-
dustry that the population must begin to
shift bodily to more promising fields. So
far, however, as the information goes, none
of these events has happened up to date.
In five years the population increased by
76,709, which means, roughly speaking, an
increase of 60,000 by the preponderance of
births over deaths, and 16,000 by the
influx of strangers escaping from coun-
tries where freedom of contract is still
in a good state of preservation. In
proportion to population, this increase is
a shade better than the Australian average,
even with the great Westralian boom
reckoned in. And apparently this popula-
tion has still some visible means of support.
The value of manufactured goods turned out
in 1896 was millions as compared with 8f
millions in 1891 ; the number of factory em-
ployes increased in five years from 25,663 to
27,389, and this during a period when both
New South Wales and Victoria showed a
serious decrease ; and out of 69 leading in-
dustries only 16 are not distinctly progres-
sive. Manufacturing industries, as a rule,
are evil-smelling and unpoetic affairs, and
their tall brick chimneys are a blot on the
landscape ; still their increase is a sure sign
of material prosperity. No community ever
came to much by merely tearing raw pro-
ducts out of the ground and sending them
abroad, and by humping foreign - manu-
factured goods ashore from foreign vessels in
exchange.
• « * *
The last five years have been years of ex-
cessive cheapness. The cause of this is
variously set down as mono-metallism, over-
competition, the introduction of new me-
chanical processes, the opening up of new
countries, the want of orthodox piety and a
neglect to put the usual threepence in the
plate, a godless system of education, and
many other things. But, while causes are doubt-
ful, the result is absolutely certain. Prices
in 1896 were so much lower than they were
even in 1891 that it was quite possible to
turn out a great deal more produce, and yet
the aggregate value to be a great deal less.
An increase of £750,000 in the value of local
manufactures meant a notable expansion of
Maoriland industries, and, considering that
this result was arrived at in the disastrous
period when two out of the country’s three
banks came to awful disaster, and its other
biggest financial institution did likewise, and
when the Australasian boom-bubble definitely
burst, it shows that there is much vitality in
the land of Labor-Ministries and State-
Socialism, and other “ reckless experiments.”
And, notwithstanding the general cheapness,
wages in Maoriland shared very little
in the downward tendency, which has
been so marked in Australia. Out of 26
industries, of which full details are given, 12
show a distinct reduction in the average rate
of wages, one is about stationary, and 13 ex-
hibit a marked increase. One good old fiction
which is badly exploded by the census-returns
is that the male worker is being ousted from
a large number of industries by the inrush
of female competitors. In one solitary Maori-
land industry out of 26—the printing trade—
this has been the case to some extent; that
is to say, there has been a small increase in
the number of female employes, accom-
panied by a considerable decrease in the male
compositor. In two other trades women
have dropped out altogether during the last
five years, and in three more the male workers
have increased, while the female ones have
grown fewer, while in the rest the workers
of both sexes have increased or decreased
simultaneously.
* • » *
On the whole, however, though they have
not begun to drive men out, the female
workers of the factories and workshops are
increasing faster than the males in Maori-
land as well as in all the other provinces.
This result is inevitable, and no fiscal policy
that it is possible to devise seems likely to
alter it. The female population, which was
at one time a very small affair, is getting to
be practically an even half of the community.
While women are increasing the proportion
of marriages is diminishing, not only in these
provinces but almost all over the world,
therefore the proportion of women who have
no male support, and who have to hustle for
a livelihood in whatever occupations will
yield the necessary crust, is constantly
becoming greater, and seems as if it might
increase almost indefinitely. In the Maori-
land factories during the last five years the
male workers have increased by 322 and the
females by 1434, which is a striking dispro-
portion. Yet, in defiance of all accepted
theories, the male factory operative is a good
deal better paid on the whole than he was
five years ago, while the female one on the
Avhole is paid a good deal worse. Taking
the two classes together, however, the rate
of wages has remained almost exactly the
same. Apparently, therefore, the province
of socialistic experiments has made greater
advances, so far as its industries are con-
cerned, during the last five years than any of
the other Australias ; it is the only one
where wages have not diminished; capital
has apparently been accumulating instead of
being driven out; and the inrush of the
enfranchised woman into all manner of
industries has not produced any disastrous
results so far, except, perhaps, to the
enfranchised woman herself.
♦ * • *
Maoriland and Westralia are the only pro-
vinces out of the seven which are not bur-
dened with a huge accumulated deficiency.
Maoriland is the only one which, for eight
consecutive years, has shown a sufficient
surplus of exports over imports to cover
interest on its foreign debt, and it is the only
province which, for a number of years, has
contrived to pay a material part of its public
works expenditure out of revenue, instead of
shunting it all on posterity. Also, its pas-
toral, agricultural, and mining industries all
show a material improvement as compared
with their condition at the beginning of the
present decade. Certainly, there has been
nothing great or gorgeous about the im-
provement, and Maoriland, like many other
places, has been a bad country to live in dur-
ing the past series of lean years. But, so far
as all the available evidence goes, it has been
the best of seven lean provinces, whereas,
according to that financial orthodoxy which
consists so largely in going broke and issuing
“ B” debentures, it ought to have been the
very worst. It is now over six years since
the Democratic party became dominant, and
the large land-owner, and the proprietor of
innumerable homed cattle, and the whole-
sale draper, and the company-monger and
bank-wrecker ceased to run the political
machine, and all that time the air has been
full of jeremiads from these persons about the
irreparabledisasterwhichmustensue. If there
was anything in that dismal tale, Maoriland
should be the most insolvent province of the
seven. Yet, though its finance has not been
wholly honest, or its Government wholly in-
telligent, it is in the main a shade more pros-
perous than the best of its neighbors.
Every one of the Australias has been a
reasonably good place to be away from during
the last seven years, but no one of them has
stood the strain so well as Maoriland.
State Insurance in Victoria.
Probably the most unsatisfactory act in the
career of Isaac Isaacs, Victorian Attorney-
General and Acting-Premier, so far as that
astute lawyer has yet gone, is his decision to
appoint a commission “ to inquire into the prac-
ticability of State or municipal fire insurance.”
Commissions in general are an expedient for
passing on the responsibility, or devising com-
promises and half-measures, or smothering the
subject under an avalanche of verbiage. They
consist generallyof politicians who are instructed
to inquire into something they know nothing,
or less than nothing about; therefore the com-
mission has to educate itself as it goes along.
It educates itself by getting evidence from
people who know nothing about the subject,
from people who know a little about it, from
people who know all about it, but whose inte-
rest it is to obscure the issue and lead the com-
mission astray, and from other people. The
commission, as a rule, doesn’t know enough to
tell one of these varieties of people from another,
and the results, in most cases, are delay and
expense, and a vast amount of print, and very
little else.
* • f *
The commission which is to consider the State
fire insurance idea can’t begin with any practical
knowledge of the subject, for there is no com-
prehensive system of State fire insurance at
present in existence anywhere from which
knowledge can be gathered. Therefore it starts
with no special fitness for its work. It will pro-
bably take great masses of evidence, but as there
is no comprehensive State insurance system
for its witnesses to supply information
about, they can’t tell it anything in particular
of a practical character. The majority of them
will probably be experts who will talk about
fire insurance in general, and, as an insurance
expert, in 99 cases out of 100, is a business
gentleman in a bell-topper who earns his crust
by bossing a private insurance office, and whose
business and salary will be destroyed when the
State takes to the work in real earnest, they
will naturally be almost unanimous in condemn-
ing the idea. The only expert who doesn’t con-
demn the idea will probably be the one who
expects to get a billet as superintendent of the
new department. If the experts are friendly,
all they can tell the Commission is that the way
to run a fire-insurance office is to charge enough
premiums to cover losses, and to keep a sharp
look-out on customers whose noses are hooked,
and whose eyes are too close together, and
whose burnt premises smell too strongly of
kerosene. None of them could tell what per-
centage of the premiums it would cost to run a
universal system of compulsory insurance, or
anything else about it, for none of them ever
did run anything of the kind. If they could
tell it is tolerablv certain that they wouldn’t.
• ' * * *
What the Commission already knows is that
insurance at present Victorian rates would pay
if expenses were largely reduced; if expenses are
not reduced then insurance at present rates
won’t pay. They can’t be reduced while about
40 companies are trying to live on the game,
and have to keep up all their offices and staffs
and managers, and give heavy commissions,
and pay 40 different rents and advertising bills,
out of the business to be done in a community
of a million people. If State insurance was
made compulsory there would be one office and
one set of branch offices and agencies instead
of 40, and the saving to be effected thereby is
too apparent to require demonstration in 240
pages of printed evidence. If State insurance
was made compulsory there would be no need
to pay heavy commissions to agents as an in-
ducement to tout for business, and the fact that
not paving commission would be cheaper than
paying" it is also sufficiently obvious. The
State wouldn’t want 10 per cent, dividends on
capital, and that saving is visible to the naked
eye. Further, it could start with no capital
save the amount required to set the scheme
going; if it struck some heavy losses at the
outset before a sufficient amount of premiums
had accumulated to meet them, the Treasurer’s
guarantee that the needful sum would
be advanced would be quite as good
as a paid-up capital of five millions. The
State is the one insurer that could start
the business without a large amount of ready
money, and it is hardly necessary to demon-
strate the economy to be thus effected. And
as the capital of a Victorian Insurance Depart-
ment would be about .£350,000,000 —in other
words, it would be the entire assets and credit
of the province—it could obviously undertake
single-handed the biggest risk in the province,
as it would be about ten times as strong as all
the 40 private offices in Victoria put together.
No evidence is likely to throw additional light on
any one of these essential points.
• * » *
The questions about which information is
really wanted are: Whether the proposed fire
insurance system should be run by the State or
the municipalities ; whether it should be com-
pulsory or not, which simply means whether
the private offices should be entirely shut up
and abolished or allowed to compete with the
State department; whether, in the event of
abolition, they have any claim to be compen-
sated ; to what extent insurance should be done
—whether the maximum should be three-fourths
of nine-tenths the value of the property, or
someother percentage; how the premiums should
be collected, and what rates of premium should
be charged. Also, whether it is advisable to
have any State insurance system at all. With re-
gard to the question of sweeping out the private
institutions : if State insurance is not compul-
sory it obviously means that the State will
have to hustle for business in the same fashion
as private companies and in opposition to them.
It will be simply one more institution added
to a list that is already too long, and the com-
petition, which is already bad, will be made
worse, and the expenses, which are ruinously
heavy by reason of too much competition,
will be made heavier by more competition.
If the State does not propose to run the indus-
try as a monopoly, then the proposed saving
mostly vanishes into air, and there is no reason,
or very little, for it going into the business at
all. If the Turner Government decides to go
into the business as a monopoly, then it and
the Victorian Parliament must decide the ques-
tion of compensation or no compensation. No
commission can relieve them of that responsi-
bility, and no evidence that any commission
can gather from experts —seeing that the ex-
perts are interested parties and are bound to
assert their claim to compensation in every
case—will make the problem any clearer.
Most of the Victorian companies, at present rates,
have lately been losing money on their insur-
ance business, and the idea of compensating
anybody for preventing him losing any more
money, seems grotesque. By combining to put
up rates, as they propose doing, they could
doubtlessmake money, buttheideaof compensat-
ing anybody for loss of the power to needlessly
fleece the public also seems grotesque. Most
of the companies are foreign institutions which
paid nothing for the right of access to Victoria,
and, under these circumstances, there is some-
thing weird about the idea of paying them to
go away. Anyhow, the responsibility of this
problem rests on the Government and the
Legislature, and they must puzzle it out for
themselves quite apart from any commission.
* *
As regards the other questions, the State can
command the services of the best insurance
experts in the country to manage its depart-
ment, and they are likely to throw more light
on details than any gathering of political ama-
teurs can do. The State can certainly afford to
insure all the property in Victoria at the rates
now charged by the private offices, and to
insure to the same extent, which, in general, is
probably about nine-tenths of the value of the
property. As regards the question of State or
municipal management, it hardly requires con-
sideration. State management means one cen-
tral office controlled by the best expert
obtainable, with branches in the principal cen-
tres ; w’ith one strong reserve fund ; with the
Treasury as a guarantee of security; with all
the State and municipal tax-gathering ma-
chinery at command for the collection of pre-
miums, and all the police and detective
and Crown Law r Offices to investigate
the case of the claimant whose ruins
smell toe strongly of kerosene. Municipal
control means 207 small insurance departments,
too poor to pay for expert management, bossed
in an amateur fashion by the butcher-aldermen,
some of them prosperous, some bankrupt, and
most of them in a chronic state of muddle and
incapacity. As regards the most important
issue—whether it is advisable to have any State
insurance system at all—that is another point
on which Isaacs and his colleagues must ulti-
mately struggle to a decision on their own
responsibility. The fact that it is advisable to
have a cheaper and better system of insurance
is admitted. The fact that it is undesirable for
Isaacs to lose his billet, either by rashly trying
to establish a new system or by rashly leaving
it alone, is also, no doubt, plain—to Isaacs. A
small, apologetic inquiry made at the door of
the Upper House would throw more light on
this point than a dozen royal commissions.

prices. lam prepared to prove that Protection in rela-

such commodities by the amount of the tax, and which

material used in biscuit-making has been removed. We
have been honest with the general public and reduced the
prioe of our biscuits considerably, and, as a natural con-
sequence, our factory has ever since been working full
time, the staff increased, besides a considerable improve-
ment in the individual wages of our hands. We not only
believe in Freetrade for the other fellow, but for every
other fellow, ourselves included.
The Bulletin replies, with all due respect for
a manufacturer of excellent biscuits, that Mr.
Aulsebrook throws no light, on anything. (1)
He says he is “prepared to prove” that Pro-
tection, under certain conditions, raises prices,
but as he doesn’t attempt to prove it that
matter may be passed. (2) In the speech
alluded to in the issue of 19th June he said
that Protection in Maoriland compelled him to
reduce prices ; he also said that the reduction
of the N.S.W. protective duty from 2d.
to Id. per lb. led to him reducing prices,
and this paper asked him whether he
meant that Freetrade and Protection both
reduced prices. He doesn’t say with any
certainty, but he now alleges that Pro-
tection leads to the manufacture of cheap,
common, local goods, and the public gladly pays
more for the superior imported article. If the
public is willing to pay more for a superior
article, what prevents the local man making
that superior article ? (3) The Bulletin did
not say that Mr. Aulsebrook came to
Sydney at the time when the duty was 2d.
per lb., for it didn’t know the exact date of his
arrival. It now appears that lie came to Sydney
when the duty was Id. per lb., equal to 20 or
25 per cent. Protection. He stayed, and seem-
ingly prospered, when the duties were raised to
40 or 50 per cent.—the heaviest rate in Aus-
tralasia. He is still here and prospering when
things have gone back to the old level and
he is again protected to the extent of 20 or
25 per cent., which looks a pretty liberal
rate. (4) Mr. Aulsebrook alleges that he
is “ experiencing the effect of Freetrade on
his industry with much satisfaction,” because,
though the manufactured article he makes is
heavily protected, the raw material he uses
comes in free. The Bulletin flatly contradicts
him. It is not he but the man who turns out the
raw material who is “experiencing the effect of
Freetrade.”, Mr. Aulsebrook himself is ex-
periencing the effect of Protection ; he always
has been doing so since he came to N.S.W. ;
and as he doesn’t clamor, either in his original
speech or his letter, for the immediate removal
of the Protection he still enjoys, it would seen),
after all, that it is Freetrade for the other
fellow that he wants—as The Bulletin sug-
gested. Three years hence—in 1900 —he will
get Freetrade for himself, which is quite a dif-
ferent thing. If he is in the same mood then
The Bulletin will be pleased to hear from him.

Vanished in Smoke.
From the commercial column of Sydney Daily
Telegraph of June 29 :—
This is the final day for collecting the present N.S.W.
import duties on the following articles :
This unpretending batch of figures gives the lie
absolutely to the biggest part of the Tele-
graph’s case against Protection. The case in
question is that protective duties are a waste-
ful and ruinous mode of raising revenue, because
the importer passes the duty on with a large
profit added, and the middleman and the re-
tailer do likewise, so that finally where the
State gets >£1000 in duties the consumer pays
.£2000 or so. About six months ago, in fact,
the Telegraph engaged someone (alleged to be
Max Hirsch) to prove that where the Vic.
Government realised £900,000 in duties the
people paid £4,900,000 in extra cost of goods.
Then it issued a list of alleged prices in Sydney
under Freetrado and in Melbourne under Pro-
tection, and, by its own showing, a duty of 3d.
instead of increasing the price of the taxed
article from 6d. to 2s., generally increased it
about the result being that the Treasury,
by the paper’s own showing, received 3d. while
the local consumer paid only Therefore,
protective duties, instead of being the dearest,
were apparently the very cheapest form of
taxation. In the above table the Telegraph
drops into the same hole. It has alleged for
years that duties levied by Government are
passed on to the consumer with 50 or more per
cent, added for middleman’s profits. There-
fore, a duty of 3d. adds at least 4|d. to the
price, and the removal of a 3d. duty would
naturally take 4Jd. or more off the price. Yet,
in the list quoted, it announces that the price
will be reduced by exactly the amount of the
duty, and no more. Therefore, it seems that
the State loses £63,000 a year revenue, and the
consumer saves just £63,000 a year, and he must
pay £63,000 a year in some other form of taxa-
tion to fill the gap, and there is no removal of
any “ burden,” after all. Even if Protection
didn’t help along any local industry it is still,
by the Freetrade paper’s own showing, as cheap
a means of raising revenue as any other, and
the story about £1000 of duties costing the
consumer £2000 or so in additional prices ap-
pears to have vanished in smoke.
No Australian Need Apply.
dear Bulletin,— The N.S.W. Bar is indeed
a happy hunting - ground for English bar-
risters who take up their residence here.
How is it that even Australians in big posi-
tions, who, on proper occasions, thank God
they are not as other men are—foreigners—
always appoint the adventurers — I do not
necessarily use this word in its disrespectful
sense—to snug billets ? Almost every billet
open to members of the N.S. W. Bar is promptly
filled by a barrister whose main qualification is
that lie is “ English, you know.” Jack Want,
for instance, who is very keen in a popular
cause, and denounces the C.S. Board for ap-
pointing a solicitor to the Tax-Commissioners,
and so poaching on his preserves, puts in “ on his
own” a pushing young Irishman, M r. Pollock, as
his secretary. No applications were invited for
the position, though the Public Service Act pro-
vides that that shall be done when a billet has
to be filled from outside the ranks of the Civil
Service. Jack’s patronage is now, more or less,
worked by his secretary—who has lately put
in a pal (Mr. Salisbury) as Master in Equity
during the absence of Master Barton. The
pal is, of course, English, and though he has
most satisfactorily acted as Registrar in Bank-
ruptcy there is no evidence before the public
that he has any particular knowledge of Equity.
The pal expected to get Mr. Pollock’s billet ;
but, having failed, Mr. Pollock, it would
appear, now consoles him. This is only a speci-
men of how the imported article is used to fill
up every vacancy in the legal part of the Govt,
service. Take the Quarter Sessions Crown-
Prosecutors : how many of them are N.S. W.
men ? Who get the nice fat briefs to prosecute
on Circuit? Who are the barristers who
receive all the Crown patronage on the civil
side of the Courts ? English to a man. Per-
haps this is only the effect of the Freetrade
proclivities of the Government. In this con-
nection it is interesting to observe that Mr.
Heydon, Q.C., Consolidator of Statutes, has
given to Australians a very fair show on the
Statutes Consolidation Commission, over which
he presides. When Mr. Heydon offered to
undertake the work he stipulated that he
should himself choose the men who were to
assist him. He picked a fair number of N.S.
Wales men—can this have any connection with
Mr. Hkydon’s fiscal belief? The only excuse
for giving such preference to the imported bar-
rister would be that the home-grown article is
inferior to him ; but is he ? Most emphatically
not. The usual English appointee of the
Government is one of whose existence the
public only becomes aware when his appoint-
ment is published, and one who could hardly
earn his salt in open competition with his
Australian-grown confreres ; recognising this, he
uses his “influence,” or that of his friends or
relations, with the Government—unfortunately
with only too large a measure of success.—R.R.

The Sunday Beer Problem.
Parson Boyce, of Sydney, has broken out in a
new direction. A week or two ago he was re-
ported as alleging that the Sunday closing of
hotels, in 1882, reduced the N.S. W. convictions
for drunkenness by 50 per cent.—which it cer-
tainly did not. He now explains as follows in
the columns of S.M. Herald :
As I observe that my statement is impugned that when
the present Sunday-closing law for public-houses was
enacted the convictions for drunkenness decreased by
about 50 per cent., I desire to say that my authority is the
return placed before Parliament by the Minister for
Justice, and ordered by the House, on Bth September,
18S5, to be printed. The exact figures are : Convictions
for Sunday drunkenness in 1881, the last year of the old
Act, were 2462 ; and in the next year, under the new,
1268. The great improvement has been more than main-
tained, as seen by the figures given by Mr. Gould as to
1895. He said the convictions for the Sundays of that
year were 1196, while those for Saturday were 5860. From
these striking figures it is clear that if the bars were re-
opened on Sundays by law, a large increase in the con-
victions might be reasonablj- expected.
Parson Boyce here speaks of “my statement ”
that “ the convictions for drunkenness de-
creased by about 50 per cent.” being impugned
—it was The Bulletin which impugned it—
and seeing that the said convictions increased
for several years after 1882 instead of diminish-
ing, it shows a considerable amount of gall on
the rev. gentleman’s part to mention that the
thing in question is his statement. Then,
instead of proving his assertion that “convic-
tions for drunkenness ” decreased, he quotes
figures to prove that convictions for Sunday
drunkenness decreased, which is quite a dif-
ferent matter. Possibly they did decrease, but
the point at issue is that the convictions for
drunkenness in general started to increase
immediately after 1882—the first Sunday-clos-
ing year. What does it signify if people were
50 per cent, less drunk on Sunday when
they were 75 per cent, drunker on some other
day to make up for it ? The point which
Parson Boyce mildly shuffles away from
every time is that in 1883—the first complete
year under the Sunday-closing system—the
number of drunks convicted was 200 more than
in 1881—the last complete Sunday-drunk year.
Also, in 1884, there were 1200 more ; in 1885,
3000 more ; and the same in 1886. Seeing that
Parson Boyce said “the convictions for drunken-
ness decreased about 50 per cent.” when Sun-
day-closing came into force, and seeing that he
again quotes this as “my statement,” and
further, seeing that the said statement is a
weak and boshy tarradiddle, the dear old
reverend ought to explain his dreadful inac-
curacy.

Cow-Merchant Brunker.
“ Machaon ”to The Bulletin Dear Bul-
letin, —Your estimate of N.S.W. Acting-Pre-
mier Brunker will be acceded to by all who
have intelligently watched the gentleman’s
career. A cattle-salesman by profession,
Brunker is a splendid example of a man of less
than mediocre ability pushed to the front rank
of politics by newspaper assistance. Had Mr.
Brunker’s brother-in-law, the Sydney Morn-
ing Herald, not boomed Brunker, it is hard
to believe that he would ever by his own
ability have risen above the position of “ cow-
merchant” at Maitland. The S.M. Herald
consistently boomed Brunker until it placed
him on a pedestal as a straightforward, God-
fearing, able politician. And Brunker,
on his side, played his part well by
seldom opening his mouth, except to
give forth safe utterances on God and
Brunker’s integrity. “ There are two ways,”
once said Washington Irving, “ in which a
man can gain a reputation for wisdom. One is
by talking a great deal and thinking very little ;
the other by shutting his mouth and not think-
ing at all.” Immediately Brunker had to act,
he gave the S.M. Herald’s estimate of him
right away. But the Herald sticks to its
fetish. The other day in Parliament Lyne hit
Brunker hard on his alleged nepotism. The
Daily Telegraph reported the incident, but
the Herald suppressed all mention. At the
time of the Federal election the D.T. put
Brunker aside as an incompetent; the Herald
boomed him, and Brunker was elected a
delegate. At Adelaide Brunker was the
miserable failure of the Convention.
The official report of the Adelaide Con-
vention gives Brunker away badly. There
was evidently no friendly reporter on hand
to write or correct speeches—Brunker made
three—two personal wails and one gaunt,
four-column peroration. Here is a slab of
Brunker’s best “ cow-merchant ” style. Con-
vention Report, p. 365 :
It matters not where we are located, what position we
occupy, or what is our nationality, it as true colonists we
do our duty well, the future is fuil of promise, not only to
the present generation but to those who are to come after
us ; and, unless as time advances and the circumstances
and conditions of the colonies change most materially, our
future is assured beyond all peradventure ; and this fair
land, dedicated by our ancestors to liberty and freedom,
will become so firmly affixed to the soil that, it shall live
for all time, and continue to shine as a beacon-light to
the whole world, showing that an intelligent people are
capable of self-government.
“Assured beyond all peradventure . . .
unless,” is not too bad, but “a fair land”
becoming “affixed to the soil” and “shiningas
a beacon-light ” is just the kind of blatherskite
one might expect from a “ cow-merchant ” of
Brunker’s pretensions. Reid, P.C., showed
marvellous perspicacity when lie selected the
bullocky-minded Brunker to be Acting-
Premier. The cute Reid has made Brunker
prick his own bubble reputation. The empty
Brunker may now be thrown overboard as soon
as it suits Reid to do so.

Colonial Clergy.
A medical man, advising his dyspeptic patient
to find food for laughter, couldn’t do better
than refer the sufferer to the daily press for a
course of “ religious discussion ” reading. The
whole conduct of church business is so keen a
satire upon Christianity that no one—priest,
pew-renter, politician, or journalist—can speak
of it in a serious spirit without becoming an
unconscious humorist, and the newspaper com-
mentator professing to treat the subject from a
sober, eommonsense point of view is the drollest
creature in the crowd. A case in point. Last
week the Anglican church party of Victoria
celebrated the fiftieth anniversary of their
first bishop’s arrival in Melbourne. The
professional pietiets, having thanked the
Lord for His goodness in permitting them
to enjoy a Jubilee luncheon, proceeded to
congratulate Him upon the healthy state
of His favorite ( i.e . Anglican) church, after
which lawyer Best, M.L.A., chipped in the
suggestion that parsons of local manufacture
should now begin to fill the fat billets hitherto
reserved for imported articles. Mr. Best com-
plained, in effect, if not in words, that the
Australian-born clergyman was not getting a
fair cut at the loaves and fishes. Then a
bishop (from Hingland) pointed out to the
worldly lawyer-man a very good church reason
why billets requiring deportment {and culchaw
in the occupant are at present monopolised (or
nearly so) by new-chuins. In England the
“ established ” Church is a fashionable profes-
sion. Wealthy’ fathers, with lots of—haw !
style and breeding, have a son or two
prepared for—haw ! —Holy Ordahs, and the
result is a scholarly, dignified class of parsons
such as Australia has not yet attempted to
raise. Our “Nicest people” do not supply
recruits for the Lord’s crack regiment (the
Anglican Blues, we will call it), and not until
bloated squatters and capitalists in general
follow the example of Hinglish sassiety, can
the A.N.A. hope to supply this country with—
haw !—high-class bishops. The explanation is
thoroughly sound from an episcopal point of
view. Also, it is screamingly funny, yet not
more funny than the sordid protest of the law-
yer who called it forth, or the comments of
Melb. Age thereon. The Age says :
It seems never to have occurred to the good prelate
that he was here giving utterance to two facts that stand
in the relation of cause and effect. The clergy in the
colonies have none of the highest prizes in their pro-
tession to look forward to, and the best men are unwilling
fo enter the Church. To law, medicine, literature, and
politics Australians give of their best, because all of them
offer careers to the men who engage in them.
In short, it is useless to expect a* highly intel-
ligent preaching of Christ’s doctrines from men
who “ have none of the highest prizes in their
profession to look forward to.” If you want
first-class apostles nowadays you must promise
them a thumping salary and a pair of gaiters in
the not-distant future. No brainy Christian
cares to serve Cod on the ordinary colonial
terms. Religion for the native-born clergy-
man is too much like the religion of Christ, in
which, as everybody knows, there is r.o money,
but merely board and lodging and second-hand
clothes.

Where people marry without any intention of
living together—merely, as Justice Simpson
euphemistically phrases it, “to save the girl’s
character”—N.S.W. Divorce Law does not
accept an application for divorce on the ground
of desertion. This is not good humanity,
neither is it good morality, and it is regrettable
if it be found to be good law. Was it the in-
tention of N.S.W. legislators when framing the
law to legislate on the intention with which
persons contract matrimony ? The intention to
not live together would, in certain ecclesiasti-
cal senses, render a marriage null. How does
it come that this same intention in the civil
sense is refused the relief of a dissolution 1 On
the face of it, the subject needs more than a
Judge’s dictum to produce a unanimous opinion.
N.S.W. Just-ice Minister Gag-Ghould lets it
be known that the Botany Bay back-door is
open for Thomas Suffield’s release if he likes
to crawl through on petition— i.e., waive his
right to an open enquiry, and practically admit
his guilt by suing for mercy. If Suffield’s
health is good enough after six years of living
death, his manhood should do nothing of the
sort. Guilty or innocent, it is a public scandal
that a man condemned on such contradictory
and uncorroborated evidence should be denied
a re-hearing of his case—just as it is a public
scandal that four affidavits containing much
fresh evidence should have been suppressed (as
The Bulletin is credibly informed by the
signatories) at the hands of the Crown Law
Dept. And Suffield’s case will be re heard.
Eighteen months ago a Melbourne man was
sent to gaol for a long term for abducting a
year-old girl. The keeper of a house where he
sought admission for himself and the “innocent
child ” gave information to the police, and the
child’s modest demeanor in court appeared to
justify a stiff sentence for her would be “be-
trayer.” Last week the young heroine, just
arrived at the age of consent, was discovered,
in the nude, in a Chow’s den, and Constable
Appleby testified that her father has been
addicted to sending her out to get money for his
horse-racing “punts.” The case of the man
who was gaoled 18 months back for abducting
this artless infant seems to need reconsideration
in the light of recent disclosures. Probably
she was at that time already engaged in busi-
ness for her delectable parent —seeking whom
she might be devoured by. If the police-story
is correct, the felon has probably paid in full
by now for all the damage he, in this case,
did to social purity.
E. Denman, Mackay delegate to the Agricultu-
ral Conference held at Gatton (Q.), put the pro-
kanaka argument thus—
At Homebush —which, in former years, was worked as
a cattle-station by two whites and a few aboriginals—
there are now 170 white farmers settled on the land, who
constantly employ 100 white laborers. In addition to
these, there are 120 extra laborers during the six months’
crushing, 120 more in the mill, besides white eane-cutters
earning, amongst them, from ±'2ooo to £3OOO per season ;
besides 7000 tons of freight for steamers, for the team-
sters and railway, and for the wharf-laborers to handle.
Homebush also employs nearly 1000 horses, consuming
Southern maize, and finds work for not le-s than 700 white
men and 400 kanakas, all of whom consume Southern
produce. Surely this is a desirable change from the posi-
tion of the same land when held as a cattle-station ; yet,
were the 400 kanakas taken away, the whole gigantic co-
operative plantation would collapse.
Would it? 1 here is the begsed question. Also,
Allora (Q„) black-soil plains in former years
were not worked at all; now they support a
thriving white agricultural population—without
kanakas. Also, as whites can and do cut cane
(it used to be alleged that they couldn’t), why
employ kanakas ? Because they are cheap,
and mean more money for the greedy
white boss. The white man cuts cane, because
he is allowed to take that work by contract;
he won’t trash cane, because the planters only
offer him kanaka wages for it.

Premier Turner comes back to Melbourne a
new and nobbled man, too full of Jubilee
memories to be his old self ever again. Tur-
ner’s recollections of Hingland will, in the
ordinary course of human nature, prove much
too strong for his democratic principles. Will
the lawyer ivho (in a cocked hat and silk
stockings) waved his hand to the London
mob preserve much respect for the opinions of
Sam Lyons’s partner ? Not much. An Aus-
tralian, pitchforked into the forefront of one
of the “ biggest” pageants the world has wit-
nessed up to date, becomes a Tory in 24 hours.
The Right Honorable Turner, Knight, had
about a fortnight of it sights, splendors,
luxuries such as he had never dreamed of. He
returns to little Melbourne feeling like an ex-
Atheist who has been enjoying % trip to the
Kingdom of Heaven.
Melb. Australasian even pasted its little
moan about the advisability of selling the rail-
ways to a shindykit on Commissioner Eddy’s
tomb :
Mr. Eddy had much more to do than manage in the
sense in which that term is understood in England. There
the railway manager has to be principally on his guard
against the rivalry of intelligence, enterprise, and cash
resources as great as any which he can himself command.
But the rivalry is carried on in a big way, and each
manager has at his back a powerful, staunch, and con-
fiding directory. He gets cheering support. Very differ-
ent is the lot of the railway manager under Government
m Australia. In each province the railway system is a
State monopoly. There is no rivalry to fear except along
the borders, and what little there is of that causes small
anxiety. But the very fact that the system is a monopoly
makes management onerous, exacting, heartbreaking. The
railways are constructed and worked to serve political,
party, and class ends. Political influence may be excluded
from the appointment of employes, but it is present
almost everywhere else, and the manager who aims at
serving the public by making the lines useful and re-
munerative finds that all the attack he has to contend
against comes from or through the authorities at his back.
The support he gets from the Ministry of the day is
seldom genuine, never thorough, and at best short-lived.
So we can realise how- Mr. Eddy, striving to manage the
railways in the light of English and American experience,
found the task heart-straining as well as brain-straining.
The masters he was laboring to serve—the people in the
mass—were unappreciative, while there were constantly
arrayed against him forces as potent, as ceaseless,
as malignant as any which human imagination has
credited to the evil magician. Railway managing in
Australia, where it aims at being efficient, is work only
for men of unconquerable will, exalted principle, and in-
vincible physique. Their reward, if any ever comes to
them besides their salary, comes after they are dead.
Well, it is something for your reward to come
at all, even after death. Two managers of big
private concerns, who recently died worn-out
and covered with vituperation— Drury, of the
Queensland National, and Colin Longmuir,
the Man that’s Dead of the defunct City of
Melbourne Bank—didn’t get their reward even
then.

Magistrate Panton, of Melbourne, “ dis-
courages the proposal to grant monetary pen-
sions to the aged poor on the ground that the
large majority of them would promptly go out
and get drunk.” Panton, by the nature of his
business, comes in contact almost exclusively
with the sort of aged poor who do get drunk,
and, being a narrow-minded man of routine, he
assumes that all the rest must be like the aged
poor he associates with. But why all this
anxiety about the aged poor keeping sober ?
If the very old pensioner does drink his weekly
dole of 10s., then the State immediately gets
Bs. of it back in liquor-duty, and nothing else
that the aged poor could do would pay it neatly
so well. Next it would run in the aged poor
for being intoxicated, and either make another
ss. out of the fine or else save a week’s money
while the aged one was doing his seven days’ .
hard. Finally, the apocryphal patriarch, who
weighs so heavily on Panton’s mind, would
some day fall down dead through drinking his
10s. all at once, and then his pension would
be saved for ever afterwards. Does Panton
want the aged popr to get iEto such a dread-
fully hygienic state that they will go on draw-
ing their pensions for ever ? The State has to
consider economy in these hard times, and
vigorous drinking among the aged poor is a
thing not wholly unpraise worthy

Athenabum Hall Carl Hertz.
The footprints of many other plays and
playwrights are conspicuous all over “A Night
Out” —the comedy-shriek which opened the Paul-
ton season at Her Majesty’s, Sydney. There is no
mistaking these footprints; they are as conspicuous
as the marks where the megatherium has been
standing after rain. The people who go out late
at night and gaily dine with other people’s hus-
bands and wives are reminiscent of old “ Pink
Dominoes,” and many other things besides. The
police who raid the hotel and bowl out these
people because of the discrepancy in their names
are the same police who rush the other hotel
in the “Magistrate.” “A Night Out” is the
good old tale of how a hen-pecked husband broke
loose in his wife’s absence and went on a fearsome
razzle-dazzle with the wife of s unebody else, and
how they painted the city red, and turned it up-
side-down and put a brick on top of it. The first
act is long and rather slow ; it is the gradual
building up of the chain of circumstances which
leads to the razzle. The third act is also long,
and in it all the people lie about their black eyes
and torn frocks, and bruises and swelled heads,
and, when the curtain goes down, they are stdl
lying beautifully. All the complication is crowded
into Act 11,, which is confused beyond descrip-
tion. No one could give a coherent account of the
nightmare of Act 11., in which four girls are going
to bed on one side of the stage, and an intoxicated
architect is already in bed unknown to them, and
the architect’s wife is trying unsuccessfully to be
a dissipated character in the next room, and her
fellow conspirator is losing her hat over the
balcony where he retired to be very ill after smok-
ing a strong cigar ; while an Italian who is behind
with his rent is assaulting a waiter in the corridor,
and the police are bursting in, and the place is
more or less pervaded with ghosts in addition to
its other troubles.
* * * *
“ A Night Out ” would be a really excel-
lent farce if it were played differently. There is
too little youth in it, and too much dreary middle
age. The giddy, frivolous, bad man is got up as a
short, hard person of bricklaying aspect and about
50 years of age. His wife is a stout personage orga-
nised in a style of premeditated ugliness, and in-
stead of regarding her with mere indifference he
views her with a loathing that is almost tragic.
The heroine he frivols with is made to represent
plain, undisguised SO or more, and is the wife of a
common-place individual whose years are uncer-
tain. The only other person who takes a very
direct part in the story is a white-haired in-
dividual of 60 or so. tut for a certain cheerful
waiter, and a perky maid who only appears
for a few minutes, and the four girls who want to
go to bed, arid who also don t stay long, A
Night Out ” is played almost entirely as a comedy
of grumpy middle age, and the spectacle of the
middle-aged people trying to frisk around in youth-
ful dissipation is a melancholy discord all through.
If half-a-century were taken off the combined
years of the five prominent characters it would be
a ton weight removed from the back of the show.
* * * *
The brightest and best individual in the
cast is Albert Whelan as the frisky casual waiter,
Oecil Ward also does his part well, but it is a part
with few opportunities, except a large black eye
in Act 111. Fred Cainbourne, as another waiter,
Oily Deering, a casual Italian who is behind
with his rent and who explodes violently in the
passage, and O. M. Berkeley, deserve great credit.
Of the new people Ernest Shand is far the best, so
far as the present show goes, and his white hair,
and his smile, and his dazzling white teeth light
up some dark places very effectively. So far as
Miss Alma Stanley is concerned, this paper would
prefer to drop that lady, and her acting, and her
dress, and her atrocious hat right out of the sub-
ject ; the things it feels inclined to say about her
are all things that are better left unsaid. Paul-
ton’s acting on the first night, at all events,
was unenchanting, and his loud, hard monotone
was painful to listen to; also, Mrs. Bryer was
only a moderate success. Miss Stafford and the
four girls who want to go to bed are pleasant frag-
ments of youth and brightness, but “ A Night
Out,” as a whole, is a good comedy whioh gets
less than justice.
# * * *
A spell of “ GiroflAG-irofla ” is in progress
this week at Sydney Opera House, which has now
been open so long that it is almost beginning to
regard itself as a live establishment again.
Lecocq’s tale of the prince who lived in the land
of nowhere in particular, and was sioklied o’er by
the pale cast of twins, is a breezy thing at all
times, and the upheaval of Knight Aston as
Marasquin makes it still more so. That tuneful
tenor never gets any older personally, and his
voice doesn’t get much older either. Also, he
never takes himself seriously ; he has a tendency
for the cork to fly out of him constantly, and
he dances through his part with the cheerful-
recklessness of a person who might, under
brighter auspices, have taken to the church
and been a bishop doing a skirt - dance
in a cathedral. Miss Glendinning is a pretty,
nicely-dressed and rather successful pair of twins ;
Miss Lambert's Pedro is one of the best The
Bulletin can remember, and Maud Lita’s Paquita
calls for some kindly observations. Farley’s Moor
is an old-established bass Moslem, who is too well-
known to require commendation. The scenery is
a trifle gone to seed, and the chorus gets
out of step now and then, and Don Bolero
ia a rather inefficient and straggly parent who
only seems to keep up at all because the
breezy Marasquin blows him along, ana the
pirates capture Girofla in a rather weak and
unconvincing fashion, and other loose ends of
detail hang out here and there, but, on the whole,
the jury returns a three-quarter verdict m favor o
the defendant. “ Girofle-Girofla is a very good
production in the main, and if a friend wou
take the management aside in a forbearing
and point out with an axe the few details which
want amendment, it might be made even better.
The orchestra goes well, and at least halt the co.
is good enough to satisfy any possible demands at
the price. Probably the saddest thing in the
show is the constant effort of seven Moors to
divide themselves into two lines with an equal
number of Moors in each. The Bulletin pre-
sumes that an eighth Moor is absent through
scarletina.
* * * *
Last Monday a tearful remark in the daily
papers announced that Sydney Criterion had
closed up owing to the serious illness of Miss
Elsie Adair, who, it is reported, will probably
never do her butterfly dance again, by reason or a
severe heart-affection. The illness of the principal
lady often covers a multitude of things in the
way of bad business and indignant revolt on the
part of the orchestra, but in the present case the
trouble was real enough. Miss Adair fainted on
the stage during Saturday night s performance,
and on Monday she was still in such a bad state
that the show had either to go on without her or
not at all. As the bright little woman was more
than half of it in her own attractive person, the
uselessness of any attempt to go on was apparent,
and the Cri. shut up abruptly. It was a very
pleasant entertainment on the whole, and the
sudden closure is a thing to be regretted.

The Flying J ordans commence a farewell
season of ten nights at Sydney Lyceum on Satur-
day. Virginia Aragon, the lady who dances on a
hair in space, and the other members of the
original co., are on the programme as before, and
Miss Lily Post, described as the American Queen
of Song, is another attraction.. Melville Stoltz’s
co. left a reputation behind it on the occasion
when it tried to break its neck in tights at the
Royal, which should ensure a good reception.
* * * *
“Mrs. Quinn’s Twins,” a new Irish
musical comedy, is to be staged at Sydney Royal
on Saturday. “Johanna” has done a tolerably
successful three weeks’ season in its own confused
way, and if Mrs. Quinn’s reckless addition to the
population lasts as long Miss Moore will have no
special cause for complaint. In view of the
rapidly - decreasing Australian birth-rate this
paper considers Mrs. Quinn’s conduct in having
twins as highly commendable and a noble example
to the female population.
* * * *
The Avolos turned up in due course at
Sydney Tivoli, where they do harrowing feats
on the bars nightly. It is a. difficult thing to
put up any records in this line in view of the
doings of some recent performers, but even under
these conditions the new people are remarkable.
A palatial “first part” with a vast amount of
expensive wardrobe in it has been inserted in the
programme, and most of the people that The
Bulletin hasn’t time to mention just now are
giving general satisfaction as usual.
* * * *
“ The Sign of the Cross” was duly hung
out, at Melb. Princess’s, last Saturday, before an
audience that had mostly booked seats in advance.
Towards the end of the melodrama there were
evidences that the congregation felt bored. The
dress-circle started to cough, in the interested tone
of people who find the sermon palling upon them,
and would like to get away without putting three-
pence in the plate. The app al to their religious
convictions touched them e.th r on the wrong
spot or nowhere at all. But a first-night effect is
not a fair indication of popular thought concern-
ing Early Christianity ; moreover sassiety in this
case had dropped some money at the races, and
was unusually weak in the Faith essential to
solemn enjoyment of church dogmas. Wilson
Barrett’s play was intended to tickle ignorance
and capture the pulpit vote, and doubtless it will
achieve its purpose in Melbourne as elsewhere.
The stage setting, anyway, is quite impressive,
and the dialogue sometimes rises about half-way to
the level of the spectacle.
* * * •
“ The Sign of the Cross ” has been already
much discussed by The Bulletin. Everybody
knows it is a gorgeous representation of Christian
virtue contrasted with Pagan vice in Nero’s time,
when the dominant Faith of nowadays amounted
to high treason and was punished accordingly.
Nero, in Anno Domini 64, put Christians to flame
with rather less brutality than their sister Mary, the
other Christian, displayed 1500 years later. He
soaked them in oil and pitch, that they might burn
merrily; whereas Queen Mary, having heard of a
Saviour’s love, used to frizzle ’em in their own
grease. Christianity in A.D. 64 was a danger to
the profligate Roman monarch, hence the outbreak
of martyrdom. Republicanism was a dangerous
religion in 1820, so two men were hanged and be-
headed at Stirling castle for speaking disrespect-
fully of George the Fourth. Perhaps they said Our
Gracious Queen’s royal uncle wasn’t much better
than Nero. These rude comments are chipped in
by way of suggesting that “ The Sign of the
Cross ” would be a grimly humorous play but for
its lack of realism in the religious scenes. If
Miss Ferrar, for instance, were to hug her charac-
ter with enthusiasm, one might picture her as an
Early Protestant marching to the stake at Smith-
field. But she and the other full-sized martyrs
are obviously acting their colorless parts, and the
small boy who shrieks from the torture-chamber
is an unmistakeable fraud. The leading male
Pagans appear genuine in many cases, especially
Nero, although the physical condition of
that notorious fiddler as depicted by Caleb
Porter is doubtless a fascinating libel on
the original. The real Nero was only 27 at the
period of his set against the Christians, and his
dissipations left him sufficient nerve for a suicide
some time afterwards. Julius Knight makes a
manly Marcus, whilst Gaston Mervale is merely
the same Black Elphberg as before, disguised in a
toga, and chopping his lines into uncertain musical
lengths, like a barrel-organ. Miss Elliott Page
is a conspicuous success among the Pagan
ladies, most of whom are unnaturally giddy j
also J. B. Atholwood shines in the capacity
of a Roman spy, and comes to exactly the same
sort of grief as Harvey Duff and other modern
identities. “The Sign of the Cross,” constructed
by a veteran actor, abounds in reminders of con-
temporary work. Marcus, coming on to save his
girl and form a tableau, is as inevitable as the
timely arrival of Walter Baker in a Bland Holt
melodrama. The tortured-ohristian incident sug-
gests “La Tosca ; ” saintly Mercia, praying
amidst the Pagan deini-mondaines, is a similar
person to Miss Ross in the Panther’s Den; the
terror-stricken informer, as aforesaid, is an old
theatrical acquaintance. Familiar stage devices
in the situations and business crop up at every
turn of the play, and give it away to hardened
theatregoers. Yet it is almost certain to boom in
Melbourne, and the present writer expects to have
Nero under observation for weeks to come. The
first matinee will be given on Saturday.
Bland Holt, at the Royal, recognising
powerful rivalry in a spectacular drama which
smells of mangled Christian, has decided to change
his bill on Saturday, for the last time this season.
“The Cotton King” is the piece chosen from
B.H’s collection, and it will be introduced to
Australia on a vast scale.
** * *
“Rip Yan Winkle” is now working
smoothly and melodiously at the Alexandra, to
the general satisfaction of such as can induce
themselves to give old friend Howard Vernon a
turn. The management hasn’t yet restored the
proper name of the theatre, but, despite its unwise
alias (“ The Queen’s ”), and notwithstanding two
or three weak spots in the “Rip ” cast, the front
of the house grows somewhat less desolate every
week. A revival of “Madame Angot”will fol-
low “Rip,” but meanwhile the indomitable
Clifford Ferley appears to be bringing special
theatre parties to the rescue, so Planquette’s opera
may possibly hold the floor until the 17th.
** * *
The Selbinis arrived at Melb. Opera House
on Saturday, and the success of those whirling
marvels came right up to the advertisement’s pre-
diction. The Rickards show is a mighty power
at present. Phil. Newbury’s warble excites the
multitude, and the list of popular “turns” is so
long that the multitude has, to a great extent,
departed, full of joy, ere Will Whitburn brings
up the rear. An entertainment which can keep
wag Whitburn in the background is a thing to be
spoken of with awe.
** * *
“ The Hermit’s Bell,” a rather comic opera
of romantic tendencies, will appear at Melb. Bijou
on Thursday (Bth) and two following nights. The
show is in aid of the fever hospital, and a nice
new chorus of 60 voices will sing under the patron-
age of the Governor.
