Next Saturday (10th) Carl Hertz opens a
short season at the Athenaeum Hall. The wizard
in question has come back triumphant from
Maoriland with a bloated banking-account and an
up-to-date Cinematography, which promises to
make an impression in a city where the Cine, is
almost a forgotten novelty.
** * *
The Caledonian Society of Melbourne
issued invitations, the other day, for “those who
take an interest in musical matters ” to come along
and hear “ Mr. Campbell’s Remarkable Tenor
Voice.” Mr. Campbell was described as a 25-
year-old native of Sale (Vic.), who had been a
blacksmith until the Caledonians “ discovered ”
his marvellous natural gift—and turned his head.
The young man’s appearance was not less dis-
appointing than his vocal revelation. He is a
cherubic-looking aspirant, with plenty of platform
assurance, and he struck people at once as the
typical up-country concert-singer, confirmed in
his own vocal conceit by the adulation of
bucolic audiences. As far as could be judged
from his assault on the “ The Holy City ” and
other numbers, his voice is not more promising
than 50 other organs that might be discovered by
any casual explorer. In its raw, untrained state
it is baritone, rather than tenor, and not particu-
larly powerful, or sweet, in either case. The fact
that the young Gippslander already seems quite
self-satisfied with his singing, having cultivated a
gentle smirk, an upward turn of the eye, and con-
siderable calm repose, suggests that he is not over-
endowed with the temperament and brains indis-
pensable to the production of an artist.
At Poverty Point.
Dear Bulletin, —The influence of a certain “ red-
covered rag,” given to attacking J. Bull, must be held
accountable for the blind fury with which many young
Australians butt at Harry Paulton, an old-established
Hinglish actor, as though he were a red rag, and they—
bulls. By these plunging critics Paulton’s reputation and
acting method have been likened to Johnny Toole’s, not-
withstanding that the newcomer is palpably the original,
or half original, of Elton, and he, unlike Toole, was an
over-rated Australian idol in days of yore. Now, as re-
gards J.L.T.’s “ reputation,” I can speak as a Londonet
who was weary—we were all weary —of that old-fashioned
joker fully twenty years ago. He hadn’t moved with the
times in farcical comedy acting, neither was he much of a
natural humorist at any time, and although he subse-
quently built his own theatre and lived on self-advt.
(assisted by Irving’s friendly puffs), the theatre was only
a very little one, and his followers were mostly country
folk, ready to laugh at anything that professes to be
funny. Touching the alleged resemblance between Toole
and Paulton, as comedians, I can see no cause for the
allegation except anti-Hinglish prejudice. Toole was a
mummer who jerked his head, elevated his eyebrows, and
“pulled mugs” at the audience. His voice inclined to
huskiness, he often spoke rapidly and chopped his words.
Paulton doesn’t jerk his head, or grimace, or attempt any
sort of extravagance. His voice is dry and metallic in its
ring, and his humor the deliberate expression of a
humor that is born in the actor s soul. He doesn’t try to
force it upon the audience because it doesn’t lend itself to
the forcing process, and therein he differs from Elton.
The nervous, assertive portion of Elton’s method was
copied from Johnny Toole. Elton, to my way of thinking,
lacked natural humor, but he was an experienced, agde
mummer, who played fine, fat parts— n n ged to get
dyspeptic Young Australia by the wool. n ther point,
dear Bulletin. You will find Paulton's oiow, solemn
method objected to particularly by the very men who
admired Nat Goodwin in “In Mizzoura,” a performance
which consisted largely of leaning against a door or a
verandah-post, and drawling out sentences at long inter-
vals. Goodwin came from ’Murka. I have observed that
the young Australians, who (never having been outside
their own country) feel unkindly disposed towards Hing-
lish institutions, endeavor to square their prejudices with
an intense admiration for aught that comes from America.
—Melbourne.
Frank Thornton had to pay £5OO into N.Q. Supreme
Court in connection with suits for damages for alleged
“ defamation,” in order to avoid the issue of a ca. re. The
miracle is that “ The Private Secretary ” paid it How
unprofessional !
George Rignold has been fined for conducting c lottery
in M.L., because he gave everyone who visited his theatre
a chance of winning a bicycle. What about the news-
papers which offer “ competitions” to all who buy a copy
and cut out a “ coupon ? ”
Miss Florence Schmidt travelled to Melbourne the other
week with the id a of introducing her voice to local notice
at a private reception. An unlucky cold, caught during
the train-journey, put the young artist hoarse de combat.
The Australian Amateur Opera Co. (Melb.) wants to give
its performance of “The Gondoliers” at the Princesss,
by way of challenging comparison with the Firm’s pro-
duction of the same opera.
The long monologue, “ How it Happened,” which. Alma
Stanley recites in “My Friend from India,” and in the
course of which she executes a couple of kick-upy i an :es,
used to be performed beautifully by Kate Vaughan at
drawing-room entertainments in London years ago. The
writer saw her do it years before “My Friend from
India” was thought of; and as a dancer, the elongated
and bulky Stanley is not to be mentioned in the same
breath with Kate Vaughan. But why does Miss Stanley
do the monologue as though it were part of the play ?
Why is there no mention on the programme of the fact
that it is an interpolation? Why is not the author given
credit for it ?

Daggerwood : “ George Herbert Rogers was a genius.
Originally a soldier of William IV. in Tasmania,, he got his
discharge through the unparalleled talent of his amateur

always his specialty, and he was very much better than
Lambert. Coppin and Neild will tell you that Rogers,
called the Farren of the Australias, was even better than
the greatest Farren.’

care of by the dr matic critics.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Treasurer Holder reckons, so far as is known
.pet, that South Australia for the year ended 30th June,
1897, will have a surplus of £5353 ; revenue, £2,628,352 ;
expenditure, £2,622,999. On the very last day of the
year over £60,000 was paid as probate duty on Thomas
Elder’s huge estate, otherwise there would have been a
deficit of £55,000, and a new accumulated shortage would
have been started in lieu of the one which has just been
unobtrusively dropped into the general debt. This shows
the unspeakable foolishness of the income-tax reduction
made 12 months ago. Thomas Elder won’t die again next
year to keep the finances square, and if the Kingston-
Holder Government cuts things so fine, the new deficit
will be bound to come shortly.
* * * *
The late South Australian loan has a sinking
fund of ss. per cent, per annum attached to provide for
its redemption. The foresight and velocity of a Govern-
ment which dodges the payment of £664,000 of revenue
shortage by converting it into permaneut debt, and at
the same time puts away £2500 a year in reduction of the
new debt, is remarkable. Unless someone ties a ton
weight to it to keep it from going too fast the blessed
deficit will be actually paid off by the year 2200, if nothing
else happens in the interim.
* * * *
Queensland revenue for the year was .£3,613,150,
and the expenditure £3,604,264, surplus £BBB6. But the
province also got through £1,148,341 of loan money—
about equal to the expenditure of the previous three
years put together, and presumably this has something
to do with the surplus. In Australia, so long as there is
loan money going about, the temptation to pay just
enough items out of it to make the revenue account
appear level is practically irresistible, especially as there
is practically no limit on the kip'* of items which a
Government may charge to loans.
* * * *
According to the figures in the Government
Gazette, N.S.W. revenue for the year ended 30th June,
1897, amounted to £9,309,269, and the expenditure is set
down as £9,480,214, so Premier Reid winds up with another
deficit of £170,945. Last year his deficit, according to the
Statistical Reoister’s figures, was £464,736, and for the
preceding half-year it was £365,784, so the aggregate Reid
deficit for 2J4 years reaches the handsome total of
£1,001,465. The best that can be said for it is that the
new shortage seems to be £58,000 less than was expected
a month or two ago. That Premier Reid will describe it
as a surplus may be assumed in advance ; that amazing
person called his last shortage of £464,000 a surplus of
£333,000, and when he intimated before going to England
that the year’s expenditure would probably be £229,000
more than the revenue be cheerfully described that as a
probable surplus of £104,000.
Premier Reid reckoned that the land-tax would
pan out for £330,000, but it only yielded £141,022. By
collecting two years’ income-tax in one year, however,
this source of revenue was made to yield £305,317. Next
year’s accounts, however, will only include one year’s in-
come-tax, and there seems to be no definite hope that the
land-tax will improve greatly, and some more customs
duties come off this month, so the Reid Govt, will re-
quire to hustle for more economies, or more revenue, or
both, if it doesn’t want things to be even worse in ’9B
than they are in ’97. Meanwhile the prospects of the
accumulated overdraft are not bright. It is eight years
since N.S.W. Govt, first began to pay off the old deficit
with one hand and to accumulate a new one with the
other, and still the new shortage keeps growing a great
deal faster than the old one is liquidated.
The expenditure out of revenue for ’97 was
apparently about £44,000 less than for ’96 (though
it is £300,000 more than in 1894), but as the expendi-
ture out of loans (£1,548,606) was £260,000 more,
and is the biggest since ’93, that probably accounts for it.
In nine cases out of ten the economical Treasurer who
cuts down ordinary expenditure simply does it by charg-
ing a lot of the items against loans, and when ordinary
expenditure goes down and loan expenditure goes up at
the same time, one usually explains the other. There-
fore, until these provinces either stopborrowingaltogether,
or else enact that only permanent works which yield direct
revenue shall be paid out of loan moneys, and that roads,
bridges, museums, statues to dead politicians, wood-
blocking streets, repairs to old bridges, wharves, dredges,
and the like, patches on the roofs of dilapidated public
buildings, and all other non-revenue-producing works
shall come out of revenue, there will never be honest or
solvent finance in Australia. The present system whereby
the Premier can charge to loans practically anything that
it isn’t convenient to pay out of revenue, and is limited
by no law whatever, is the chief reason why the finance
of the Seven Provinces comprises seven permanent stinks
of the worst description. Now and then a pro\ ince swears
off borrowing for a time, as in the recent case of Maori-
land and Victoria, and then its finance is moderately
honest for a year or two through want of loan moneys to
fake the accounts with, but otherwise the stink is chronic.
* * *
The Vic. Govt, has floated locally within the
last few months £2,200,000 of 3 per cent, stock at par in
place of the 4 per cent, stock previously existing. This
saves £22,000 per annum interest in one operation
* • * *
The amalgamation of the National Mutual Life
Assurance Society with the Mutual of Victoria took effect
on Ist June, and the fact that there was thus one office less
competing for business in an overcrowded market was
viewed with satisfaction. The same month, however, a
British institution, the Scottish Metropolitan Co., came
in to fill the gap, so the numbers are about as they were.
This makes five foreign life-assurance concerns (two
British and three American) doing business in Australia,
and it seems quite probable that a time will come when
they will rush Australia as the foreign fire-companies
have done, with very similar results. The Scottish
Metropolitan is one of the baby insurance concerns which
swarm in Britain—a country whose insurance institutions
range from first-class down to awful. It is a proprietary
concern founded in 1876, and at the end of 1895, after 19
years’ work, it had accumulated a life assurance fund of
barely £272,000, so it may fairly claim to be one of the
slowest concerns now doing business in Australasia. What
it chiefly, prides itself upon is that it charges very nearly
the lowest premium rates on record ; in fact, they are so
low that it has alv ays been somewhat of a mystery how
the co. contrives to make them pay. One method by
which it seems to make ends meet is by reckoning future
interest on its accumulated funds at 4 per cent., whioh is
tolerably steep in a country where interest rates are so
low as they are in Britain. The best British offices esti-
mate future interest as low as 2*4 |per cent., and very few
indeed go above Even in Australia, where interest is
much higher than in England, the best offices are now
reducing their future estimates to 3J4.
* * * *
So far as The Bulletin oan ascertain, the
Scottish Metropolitan has paid no bonus on its life policies
for at least a dozen years, and its surplus seems to have
faded from £4030 in 1885 to £2342 in ’93. A surplus like
that leaves no margin worth speaking of for bonuses.
The Scottish Metropolitan spent in 1895 over 18 per
cent, cf its total income or about 21£ per cent, of its
premiums in working expenses and dividends to share-
holders, .yet at its last valuation it appears to have only
estimated the future expenses at 121 per cent., and even
then it pulled througli with a very small margin of
solvency. Altogether the new co. looks very small and
unprogressive, very expensively worked, very reckless in
the matter of charging sensationally low premiums, and
even though it reckons future interest at a dangerously
high rate and future expenses very low, it appears not
only unable to pay bonuses but to have a very narrow and
decreasing margin between its assurance fund and actual
insolvency.
• J here are many weird life assurance institutions
in Britain. The Yorkshire Provident, which in 24 years
has accumulated a fund of £3552, and apparently lost
most of the shareholders’ capital in doing it, is a fine
sample. The Victoria, 35 years old, and with £76,000
accumulated, is another. The Pioneer, five years old,
which spends 60 per cent, of its receipts in ex-
penses and has £936 saved up, is also an in-
teresting concern ; ditto the Law Property Co. whose
total assets are £69,000, the biggest being “ Accumulated
Deficiency, £61,000.” The absence of any State supervi-
sion makes the British insurance co. a thing’ which is by
no means to be taken for granted, and the worst British
co. is T a good many times worse than even the worst Aus-
traiian one. The very worst J. Bull assurance office could
drink all its assets and prospects in one respectable orgy.
The 1896 balance-sheet of the Mutual Life In-
surance Co. of New York is to hand. The premium
income for the year is £8,288,340, and the total income
£10,421,359. Out of this £3,051,450 is added to the accu-
mulated funds, making them £47,890,454—the biggest life
?. si ™ee fund in the world. The surplus of assets over
liabilities is now £6,105,444, being about half-a million
more than it was a year ago. This is arrived at by assum-
ing future interest at 4 per cent. ; if the rate was reduced
to the same level as that assumed by the A.M.P. Society
and Maoriland Government Department the surplus
would probably be between £4,000,000 and £5,000,000, but
even this is a very solid margin of security.
The worst feature about the Mutual of New York
is the very heavy expense rate, equal to nearly 25t0 per
cent, on premiums, or a little over 20 per cent, on total
assets. This is an exorbitant rate accordingto Australian
ideas. An item among the assets is “ Interest accrued
and due, £468,140” ; the Mutual, like several Australian
offices, follows the bad practice of lumping these together,
and the amount of arrears is not stated. As it is guaran-
teed by a State auditor, however, as absolutely sound
and above suspicion as regards its assets and its surplus
and its other figures, the omission in its case is of less
importance.

Say well, merchant, Sydney ; W. M. Arnold, of the Stock

was M. J. Brown, of 44 Elizabeth-street, Sydney, and

E. Viles, accountant, of 8 Bridge-street, Sydney. If
“ Reader ” will circularise all the persons mentioned above
he may get the information he desires.

A Q. “ Order-in-Council ” has been gazetted,
conferring on a syndicate the monopoly (outside the
Municipal Council) of electrically lighting the central
area of the City of Brisbane. However, the City Council
may, if it chooses, compete with the syndicate, and that’s
one possible lever ihat can be used against extortion.
•** " *
Apropos a soap trade-mark action in Mel-
bourne Supreme Court, the law has laid down the sen-
sible rule that a mere adjective of common usage cannot
be employed as a trade-mark, even though it may get on
the register through an oversight. For instance, a manu-
facturer can’t have protection against the trade for his
“Brilliant” candles, or “Matchless” matches, whereas
“ Samaritan ” ointment would be a safe thing to register.
The world, by the way, is probably waiting for a Samari-
tan Ointment, and a Lazarus Liver Pill.

unexpected places underground. What a dreary farce

have become! “ No. 5 level : driven 13ft. No. 6 : driven
Bft. No. 2 crosscut extended 7ft.” And so on. Never a
word as to the character of the ore or country in the
faces. Nor do the monthly bullion statements help much-
they are “ arranged ”to produce uniformity. But, after
all, you can t judge a mine like the B.H. Prop, from week-
to-week or month-to month changes in the ordinary
working faces exposed. J

B.H. Co’s complain that they are not getting,
or, at least, have not been getting, fair prices for their
concentrates ; but little more than professional jealousy
prevents a combination to push on metallurgical works
for the further treatment of the concentrates. The B.H.
Prop, used to be just as copiously bled through its
bullion until it flew in the face of ali the Home experts
and put up and gradually extended its refinery.

So much gold has lately been stolen at the
Wentworth mine, Lucknow, N.S.W., that the manager
has notified a complete suspension of work, adding that
the date of resumption will altogether depend on what
aid he gets from the hands lately employed in tracing
out the thieves. The Wentworth proprietors are so un-
fortunate as to possess a specimen-mine. Specimen -mines
lend themselves to larceny ; whereas in a Mt. Morgan the
gold is comparatively safe.

Tuesday, when the inflated market cracked and the scrip
came back. The rapid rise took place after the publica-
tion, not of Mr. Randolph Adams’ report, but of extracts
from it, which may or may not be a very different thing.

B. H. Goodman, the London dentist who pro-
moted the W.A. Gold Districts Trading Corporation, and
boomed the Ore Automatic Reduction process, has reached
bankruptcy—debts, £61,548. Before this he had the
pleasure of attending a meeting of the G.D.T. Co. share-
holders, and hearing himself described as the “ new Jabez
Balfour,” and his bod}' and blood asked for. He managed
to escape from the rear of the building. One of the co’s
“ trading ” operations consisted of sending cables to its
representative in W.A. to cable back as follows, etc., etc.
It was an awful business, but it would be an easy matter
to give a long list of newspapers who boomed the G.D.T.
Co., also the“ process,” which the Mining Standard sa.vs
“ created quite a sensation in Australia” But it never
did. It only created a lot of ridicule, and caused some
fun. The only thing that could possibly be taken in with
it was perhaps a mining paper !
• * * *
In refusing exemption to the Easter Gift mine,
W.A., Warden Finnerty remarked that the fields were
being ruined through the working capital of companies
not being expended. “Shareholders who did not pay
calls were not deserving of consideration.” Rubbish.
The curse of the limited liability mining law of England
is that it enables directors, who are usually paid-up
holders, to call up every shilling to sink in mines after
they have been proved duffers. The blessing of the Aus-
tralian no-liability law is that it enables contributing
shareholders to retire when they think they have had
enough.
* « * *
After hogging the whole business for years the
Hampton Plains estate, W.A., has been thrown open to
prospectors. “As the ground is practically virgin, gold-
finds are expected.” But much bigger areas have already
been prospected in W.A. without success.
* * * »
The directors of the Glasgow, W.A., gold-
mines have recommended the abandonment of their pro-
perty, they having been utterly misled as to its value.
The vendors, who took £85,000 in cash and shares, offered
another mine as a solatium. It is always another mine,
never the money back !
• * * •
The public now hears that want of water is the
only thing the matter with the General Gordon mine,
W.A. It is expected that it will get enough shortly to
run the battery eight hours a day. Several hundreds of
tons are stacked and another 1000 can quickly be raised
from the open cut, and the mine is looking promising.
Then we are told that the Gordon North shaft is full of
water, that 10,000 gallons a day are being pumped from the
Gordon South, and that the Gordon Main Lode is dis-
charging 30,000 gallons a day. Then why in Heaven’s
name can’t they arrange to use some of it ? What has
become of the G.G. Extended ? Is the money still being
sunk in the mine, or are the directors, as stated some time
ago, returning what is left to the victims who put it up?
The winding-up of the Gordon Main Lode has been
peremptorily ordered by shareholders who described it as
“a rank swindle from its inception.”

Kaufman, boss Westralian mining schemer, has'
got a bond on the Ivanhoe mine. It is now wired from
the camp that the rumor is, the Corporation represented
there by Kaufman intends also to buy the Great Boulder
and the Boulder Perseverance mines, which, with the
Lake View and the Ivanhoe would be formed into one big
co. of £20,000,000, and thus control the greatest gold-mine
in the world. This sounds thundery, but the chances
are against it. If Kaufman’s crowd make as much
money out of the Ivanhoe deal as by that of the Lake
View, not only must Kaufman be a genius in finance, but
the public must even be a bigger ass than The Bulletin
has hitherto imagined. In the Lake View, half the mine
was given away for £30,000 working capital, which by
the way, was never wanted. Big deals were also made in
b ,. ef< l re th . e _, bargain was fixed. In the Ivanhoe
is to be paid for three-fourths of the mine and
£40,000 to be provided as working capital. Of the two,
so far, the Lake View has proved the richer property As
the Ivanhoe shares have averaged about £9 for a year
they could not have been got very cheap in anticipation.’
Ihe yarn that London management is better, is all moon-
shine—much better, perhaps, for the management.
“The Boulder Perseverance is bagging telluride
ore rapidly, but has not yet commenced shipping. It is
intended to send the ore away on a large scale.” Perhaps
san ' e was sal ’d of the Associated Mines!

One Westralian co. which recently applied for
assi «'! le< ! four reasons for its request : (1)
Work in hand finished ; (2) money all spent*; (3) short of
funds ; (4) English 00. failed to complete purchase as

possession of the property 15 months, and the warden
replied that according to the books it had already had
18 months exemption. A co. which wants a-year-and-a-
half exemption in a-year-and-a-quarter is a fine example
of persistent hard work. But the application was granted
all the same.

Dear Buli.etin, —The Jubilee illuminations in
Melbourne, conducted mainly by the Government and
city corporation., strike one as having been the jobbiest of
jobs. Other people’s money was poured into the coffers
of the gas and electric-lighting companies by these des-
potic wasters, some of whom themselves hold shares in
the aforesaid companies, and all of whom are favorably
inclined towards shareholders in general. Another pro-
tected concern that coined money from the illuminations
was the Tramways Co. The trams had the run of the
thronged streets whilst poor old cabby was driven out of
the city by special regulations.—Paul.
* * * »
The cheapening of the cyanide process and the
mipiovement in stamp mills notwithstanding, the syndi-
cates that put up deposits for the working of Bendigo
taihngs-heaps have forfeited. The writer mentioned,
years ago, that these dumps were probably the poorest in
the world, and could not be worked at a profit by any
known method. If a man could easilv get at the bottom
of some of the heaps, such as that of" Koch's Pioneer, he
would do well, but the trouble of shifting the top is too
great.
* * » *
Gippsland miners are howling about the Tambo
Valley race, a Govt, spec., to cost £lOOO, that promises to
be a fiasco. The race is to run 20 miles, and was intended
as a boon and a blessing- to the alluvial men, but the
course has been mapped through runs and cockies’lots
the whole of the way, and it looks as if a big- sop were
being-thrown to the “ landed gentry” under a specious
pretence of fostering the mining industry.

besides carrying £5OO to reserve. North Smithfield, 197
tons, 6400 z.

At Clermont (Q.) rush, washdirt is puddled on

returned 540 z. gold. Sunday work at Clermont is some-
times as profitable as a collection-plate.

A Cooktown (N.Q.) public meeting has “re-
solved that it cannot meet the strain on its resources
demanded by the destitute “ return ” diggers from New
Guinea. Boss Tozer has been appealed to, and it is also
considered that, since the Southern provinces furnished a
large proportion of the men who rushed to Macgregor-
land, those countries should contribute to the funds
necessary to support the deadbeats.
* « * *
The New Guinea infatuation is considerably
cooled down in Northern Queensland. The spectacle of
so many haggard, shaking, penniless wrecks coming down
the coast brings many- a man’s ardour down to freezing-
point. And the recollection of those who don’t return at
all is more edifying still.

New Guinea gold is poor stuff, and often only
fetchers about £2 16s. per oz. at Sydney Mint.

SOCIETY
“ The Council of the Churches” put that
absurd person, N.S.W. Colonial Secretary
Brunker, on the Protestant ticket at Federal
election time and so secured his return as a
Federal delegate. Now, at the instigation of the
Council aforesaid, Brunker sools the police on the
little shopkeeper who commits the crime of selling
cakes, fruit and hop-beer to the workman who
takes his wife and children to Manly on his only
day of rest, Sunday. “You may sell meals to
people who eat them in your place,” says the
policeman to the shopkeeper, “but you must not
sell a cake or an orange to be taken away from
your shop. A purchaser may take away a bottle
of milk but not a roll of bread —that is the law.”
A MAORI BELLE.
(One of the most charming of many Maorilanders whom Mr. Arthur Hes, now of the Tasma
Studio, Hobart, has so skilfully photographed to the glory of a perishing race.)
And such are the laws championed by the so-
called “ free ” Protestant Church ! How the Pope
must smile!
* * * *
Viator : “ Odd are Australia’s sumptuary
laws. Eating must perforce be divorced from
drinking, in ruling the common people, as though
alcohol was only for drunkards. Why should not
cheap restaurateurs be allowed to furnish light
lager, on licensing terms similar to those for native
wine?”
• * * *
Sydney Morning Herald raises (in a
leading article) a regretful wail over what it calls
the “ increasing delicacy ” of courts of justice in
dealing with offenders. “ There was a time,” it is
sorrowfully added, “when the proceedings of the
courts were marked by a strain of ferocity.”
Granny weeping for her Windeyer ! “ But oh ! for
the touch of a vanished hand, and the sound of a
voice that is still! ”
• • • *
Gov. Buxton (S.A.), who is the most
twaddlesome speaker in the Seven Australias, told
the 20,000 children assembled in Adelaide on
Jubilee Day that if they were really good between
eight and 12 years of age they might all confi-
dently hope to be kings and queens at 18. At
least, if he didn’t mean that, his address meant
nothing, which, after all, would be quite charac-
teristic. One K.C.M.G., forgetting his Imperial
servitude for the moment, even went s(f far as to
describe His Ex. as a “ damned old ass ” when
commenting on his public Jubilee utterances.
* * * *
Mayor Rogers, of Croydon (N.Q.), gave
the local niggers an ante-jubilee tuck-in, a few
weeks back. The blacks gorged themselves
into a comatose state. Every Jacky refused
all offers of manual labor for three days,
and the gins banjed the banje of the full-and-
plenty. In vain might “White Mary” cooey
and coax her colored sistah. The routine reply
was: “Baal me washum to-day, missus; too
plenty big fellow sit down longa binjie.”

Atkins : “The climax of Jubilee bathos
was reached in Gov. Brassey’s pretending to all

Ye Gards ! Grand Duchess Liddiard asks Fritz
Vernon : ‘ How many campaigns, how many
wounds?’ ‘Your Highness, no campaigns, no
wounds. Oh, I forgot. Getting over a spiky
orchard fence, after some apples, I tore my—but
never you mind where.’ ”
• • * *
From a cablegram anent the free distribu-
tion of grub among the London poor, with the
Prince and Princess of Wales wearing haloes and
tasting said grub :
Many touching scenes were witnessed, and many women
were so overcome as to burst into tears.
An involuntary testimony to the satanic side of
the Glorious Reign ! Bejewelled patriciandom
dangling sugar-plums above the maw of ravenous
famine. Satin-skinned plumpness in purple and
soft raiment holding the spoon to the wolfish
fangs of skeletonism in rags. Hell itself too
weak to do more than “ burst into tears.” In the
back-ground, well-fed Insanity bellowing itself
hoarse with idolatry of the divine beneficence
of the pampered drones born with a legal right to
rob the bees of their all. And the angels looked
down and sorrowed, while the fiends leered up and
grinned. Yet, where gristle (and not exhaustion)
had the place of fat, there was Hate bred.

A Brisbane (Q.) bootblack, at 6, one after-
noon : “Yus, bizness is orful bad. I sat at me
stand all day yesterday, from 6 in the mornin’ till
7at night, and took nix. Never got one job. To-
day, since 6 a.m., I’ve taken ninepenee for three
shines.” And his stand is in the busiest part of
Queen-street, the main business thoroughfare.
A Melb. jour-
nalist was shocked
the other night on
receiving a whole
raft of telegrams
informing him
that a brother
quill - jerker was
seriously ill, and
begging him to
hasten to the sick-
bed without delay.
The wires had
been sent to dif-
ferent points of
the city in the
hope of catching
the friend, and
they found him,
one after another.
The recipient
made a bolt for
the home of the
suffering man,
hoping at least to
be in at the death,
but the invalid’s
relations had not
heard of him for
many days. Then
began a toilsome
hunt for the pa-
tient or his corpse,
and at length jour-
nalist No. 2 ran
No. 1 to earth in
a dim, secluded
pub., where the
dying man was
seated at a table
composedly drink-
ing rum from a
tumbler. Recri-
minations and
bad language fol-
lowed, and eventually the stricken man was heard
in tearful explanation: “You see, dear boy, I
was so lonely—so desolate and lonely.”

Last week the Anglican Diocese of Mel-
bourne had a Jubilee service at St. Paul s Cathe-
dral. But for his very sudden indisposition, the
sermon would have been preached by old Canon
Handfield, who came from ’Ome with Melbourne’s
first bishop (Perry) in 1848.
* * * *
On the Saturday preceding the orthodox
week of rejoicing the Hobart Civil Service (in
response to a circular from heads of departments)
assembled in Franklin Square, and, after Treas.
Fysh had orated, shamefacedly howled “God
Save the Queen ” in a heart-breakingly discordant
howl. Nobody sings “ Gorsave ” with any appear-
ance of dignity unless drunk. It was interesting
(says a Bulletin correspondent) to watch the
faces of the servants in the crowd prior to the
unmusical proceedings. Thpse of many depicted
a struggle between a desire to flee from the whole
ridiculous show, and a mortal fear of offending
their bosses. It was indeed a pitiful exhibition.
* * * *
All the large Melbourne fac-
tories and works are adopting the
European system of salarying a
handy doctor to undertake the
medical care of all their employes
for a good round sum per annum.
One big East Melb. brewery, for
example, pays its regular doctor £BO
a year to attend its hands.
• * *
Tom Price (S.A.), while
Bpeaking in the Assembly recently
on the subject of “bench packing,”
stated that a Conservative who
stood for a city constituency at last
general election forced himself into
a house to canvass for the support
of the occupier and his wife. The
man picked up the politician by
the scruff of the collar and dropped
him on the footpath. Just after-
wards the Conservative sat as a
justice when the voter was defend-
ant, and joined in deciding the
case against him.
• * *
Law has liver-disease badly
just now in Melb. Scanty cause-
lists, and mostly rotten at that, are
trying enough, but there are no
pleadings either, which means scan-
tier lists for months to come. There
is some talk of trying Sydney, but
the drought and the admission
rules frighten them unless they
have, in the latter case, an English
qualification ; while to others the
far West, with rumors of other fel-
lows’ luck, appears the Lexdorado.
The rest are anxious to wish them
“ God speed, and don’t come back again ! ”
• * * *
In view of the recent hysterical shivoo it
would be a consistent and graceful act for the
united sects to celebrate the “Phosphorous
Jubilee ” of the Devil. According to orthodox
beliefs, Beelzebub is a personage most necessary
to the welfare of mankind, and a big celebration
is really due to him for his unflinching and self-
sacrificing assiduity to duty during his long-
regime. “An Eternity of a Blameless Life : ”
“God Bless our Devil.” In its mind’s eye does
The Bulletin see the illumination.
AUSTRALIAN DRY GIN.
Some years ago a young barrister, since
highly distinguished in law, politics, and litera-
ture, arrived in Sydney. He didn't then know as
much as he knows now, and so it happened that
in an interval of loud silence at a fashionable
dinner-party everyone heard him say that “ The
Bulletin is a most enjoyable paper—by far the
most interesting published here, 1 think.” At once
he perceived his mistake ; host and guests looked
—well, they looked as an execution crowd looks
when it is waiting for the body to fall through the
trap. “ You will pardon me, sir,” at length said
a venerable M.L.C., who was of the company,
“ but as a new-comer you probably do not know
the paper mentioned quite so well as those who
have lived longer here. You will find, on further
acquaintance with it, that it is a most disreputable
print. Why, do you know ’’—continued the
ancient, his voice sinking to an awed and reveren-
tial whisper —“ that it.actually makes disparaging
remarks about the Prince of Wales ! ” Yet
around that table there wasn’t even the ghos 4 - of a
smile.

A well-known Sydney doc-
tor says, re consumptives who
wouldn t voluntarily enter a
special phthisis - hospital, that
he d be “ sorry to see the
statute-book stained with a law
to force people into hospital.”
Hut peopleare practically forced
into hospital, as it is, in the
cases of small-pox and leprosy ;
and, as the need for equal
stringency with phthisis seems
to be close upon recognition,
such a course will either be
proper in this instance or wrong
in two others.

A man who was well-dig-
ging near Deniliquin recently
stated that he struck water
which “had a distinctly spirit-
uous taste and exhaled intoxi-
cating fumes.” That man,
though he is probably unaware
of the fact, has also struck the
site of the future Australian
capital. It might have been
better had he found an artesian
well spouting beer regularly all
the year round ; but one can’t
have everything.
In a certain Australian province, where a
hanging job seems to be now pending, the authori-
ties recently had a week’s search to find the hang-
man. He was eventually run to earth in a back
alley, where his wife and three other “ladies”
were engaged pounding him with a spade and
other articles. As the job probably comes off in
two or three weeks time, he has been sentenced to
one month for “ riotous behaviour and indecent
language ” so as to guarantee that the execution
wm be performed satisfactorilv and with credit to
the department. Further, both the gentlemen’s
eyes ar e at present closed, and unless Nature is
j j W ? j am P^ e lrie to effect repairs there is the
added danger that the sheriff or a reporter might
be scragged in mistake.

Kapunda (S.A.) Racing Club announces that it has removed the disqualification of a racehorse owner, in celebration
of Queen Victoria s Diamond Jubilee. A splendid idea, which will appeal to the hearts of all “stiff 'uns." When the

casualness of it all, decided that the defendant
married Maud Eva simply to prevent the church
getting mouldy. Quite a lot of marriages appear
to arise through one of the parties seeing a church
that isn’t in use at the moment, and feeling a
suaden desire either to get married in it or to
break the front window with a brick, just as he
would want to prod a sleeping dog to see how long
the animal is when it stands up.

A big railway arbitration case is a fine
corpse for legal vultures to batten on. The his-
toric Robb case, in Kanakaland, cost something
like .£20,000 ; and the M‘Sharry case in N.S
Wales promises to eclipse it. Twenty-six wit-
nesses were called for plaintiff ; and defendants
have called 41 (one of whom was in the witness-
box for 31 days !) and are not through yet. The
arbitrator gets something like 30 guineas a day.

* * * *
. Samarai (New Guinea) is such a small
island that a capable “lepper” can traverse its
diameter in a hop-step-and-a-jump. And what
troubles the unconventional boys there now is that
—there are 14 white women on it! There is one
hotel, built originally for a small family. But
it has at its rear a “native house.” where all-
comers must camp-along with the fowls, which
roost aloft. The nominal accommodation capa-
dty of the “native house ” is up to 10; the real
limit is as many as come ! At the morning
toilet, each male who has stripped gets a blush-
big consciousness that his hairy legs are visi-
hie to everybody in “ the kitchen.” Scoot-
ing round the corner in search of
privacy, he cannons against several ladies
puddling at washtubs. Fleeing round the
hotel, he debouches into the main street
an s* meets the reproachful gaze of more
aghast females. So he rushes inside the “ hotel,”
and finishes trousering himself under a table, and
also under the gaze of native women, who stare at
ns movements as if he were a prize lunatic. It
takes the hardening mill of time to make a modest
male (and Northern diggers are nothing if not
modest before beyvchus woman) used to the public
performance of his toilet in Samarai.
.** * ♦
St Vincent's Hospice, Sydney, an appeal
on behalf of the funds whereof is made by Mr.
Justice Simpson, has its doors “always open for
those who are beyond hope of recovery, and for
whose last moments no provision is made by other
charitable institutions.” Grimly reminiscent of
poor Mannington Caffyn’s Bulletin story, in
which an outcast looks up an old friend, and asks
to be allowed the use of his sofa to die on !

A.n illustration of the overwhelming suc-
cess of the great Q. International Exhibition
On its opening day. May 5, and for several days
afterwards, suburban trains were run to and from
the show. When the day’s receipts of those trains
dropped down to ss. 10d., the cold-blooded com-
mercial commissioner abolished the service. And
now “Disgusted” is writing to the Brisbane
daily papers and asking why such trains are not
put on.

At Cairns (where the whites take off their
hats to the Chows) recently, an application was
read from the sanitary contractor requesting that
his contract be transferred to one Archie Lock.
Now it happened that Archie Lock was a pure-
blooded Caucasian, but Cairns is so accustomed to
find the Chow an all-pervading element that
aldermen interpreted the name as Ah Chee Lok.
Hence ensued a furious discussion on the ad-
visableness of letting contracts to Chows, and
trouble seemed likely to arise until the aldermen
were fully seized of the astounding fact that the
party in question was not a Chow, and that all
European contractors had not died out, &c., &c.

VICTOR J. DALEY.
This is a long-ago photograph of Victor
Daley, by the publication of whose collected Bul-
letin verses in a month or three Australian litera-
ture will be permanently enriched. As Paterson
Lawson, Boake, Ogilvie, and others—the bards of
the bush—represent one side of. recent Australian
poetry, its open-air vigour and “ joyous animal
motion, so Daley, a bard of the cities, represents
its refinement and grace. Since Kendall died
and Brunton Stephens ceased to write, Victor
Daley in his sphere stands alone. Even Kendall
with perhaps a greater sense of natural beauty'
has no more natural magic than Daley at his best’
thought SIS Der pililosoph y aQ d the stronger

Messrs. E. Cusenier and Co., of Cognac,
France, have now put on the Australian market
their famous Grand Supreme brandy, a liquor
which has a splendid reputation in Europe and
America, but which has hitherto been in short
supply in this country. The Grand Supreme is
reputed, where it is best known, to be the finest
brandy in the world, and the Australian demand
for it will assuredly be large and constant.*
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, July 6, 1897.
Mr Deak Myee—•
A glance round St. John’s Church, Darling-
hurst, on Wednesday of last week, when the
handsome and popular Miss Nellie Hill became
the wife of Mr. E. P. Rundle, instantly assured
one that the floral decoration of the edifice had
been undertaken by the “girl friends of the
bride.” The professional and artistic touch in
arrangement was entirely missing. White moir6
antique, an exquisite fabric, but one which always
suggests the early Victorian era of drooping curls
and spreading crinolines, comprised the bridal
costume. The advantage this material has over
almost any other is that it imperatively does away
with trimmings and fixings of any sort. On this
occasion, the fascinating “waterings” in the
material clearly asserted themselves, for the well-
out skirt fell in unbroken lines ; some full puffings
of chiffon on the sleeves, and some sprays of
orange-flowers on the bodice, constituted the whole
garniture. Among the guests, not more than
half-a-dozen were arrayed in wedding garments,
and winter gowns that had seen half a season’s
hard wear, and were beginning to show it, were
mostly sported. A recently-travelled blonde,
however, in an unmistakably French gown and
toque, furnished a refreshing contrast, and seemed
to bring to the sombre-looking assemblage a whiff
of gay Paree.
Persons who possess that quaint fad, a desire to
search out the influence of bodily coloring on
character and temperament, should not overlook
the fact that three of the best singers Australia
has produced up-to-date—Crossley, Florence
Schmidt, and Eva Mylott—have auburn tresses, and
are, by the way, all three pretty well of the same
build. Miss Mylott certainly takes her place now
in the front rank of warblers. On Saturday night,
at the Jules Simonsen benefit concert, she sang
“ Out on the Rocks,” the only song apparently
ever written for a contralto, and managed artisti-
cally to insert a soul under the bare ribs of that
worn-out dirge. Jules Simonsen, who seemed far
more at ease in faultless dress clothes and
smoothly-plastered hair than he did in the
mysterious grey small clothes and the wild and
woolly wig oif II Trovatore, was in fine voice. Jules
holds a world’s record for being the only male
Warbler to whose cheek applause brings the shy
and modest blush. It would be interesting to
observe how long he will be able to retain this
fascinating failing on the wicked continent.
The wedding of Miss May Wedderburn and Mr.
Lawrence Campbell took place on Wednesday,
30th inst. The church was thronged with specta-
tors whose, chattering spoiled much of the effect
of the beautifully-rendered choral service. The
back seats were principally filled with nurse-girls
with their arms full of large, fat baby, who
punctuated the prayer and praise with emphatic
howls. The bride, attended by two prettily-
dressed little girls, wore her travelling gown of
steel-grey bengaline and carried a large and
spreading posy of bridal flowers. After the cere-
mony, afternoon tea and speeches were served at
“ Heathfield,” the residence of the bride’s sister,
where a glittering array of highly-polished wed-
ding gifts were displayed.
The omission of the word ‘‘obey” from the
bride’s oath at a recent smart Sydney wedding
was the most conspicuous and most widely-dis-
cussed feature of the ceremony. The fair bride
raised her eyes and nervously watched the face of
the “ joiner ’’ when he began the “ say after me,”
and when he at last came to the important word,
balked at it, and finally “cleared it without
touching,” something which on a face less fair
would have been called a broad grin pervaded the
bride’s features. The bridegroom, who seemed
rather painfully conscious all the time of his bright
tight boots, generously returned the grin as if he
had no further use for it, and became preternatur-
ally grave.
As Madame Pailliard, in “ A Night Out,” Miss
Alma Stanley presents an object-lesson to all
beholders in the matter of what colors and styles
to avoid when choosing raiment for the merry
spring-time. On her towering six foot of figure
she manages to combine such a vast aggregation
of smart, fashionable hideousness that one’s eye-
balls are seared, and it is a positive relief to gaze
at the red-and-green check gown and scarlet bon-
net of Madame Pinglet, which in comparison
seems quite sombre and, colorless. On a mar-
vellous headpiece, worn in ; the third act, vivid!
greens and pinks and purples and heliotropes are
wildly flung together like an upset paint-box.
This nightmare of millinery is worn with a
gown which suggests an undried tea-cloth, and
looks as if it had been hastily pinned round the
wearer by someone in the wings. The “ pro-
perty ” black-and-steel gown of the second; act—
the bodice of which, with net sleeves attached,
seems a mere afterthought—is the smartest-looking
garment in the play, if the really artistic frocks of
Mathieu’s four daughters are excepted ; and Miss
Stanley goes to some trouble to emphasise the fact
that with it she wears black silken petticoats and
stockings. It grieves Cecil Ward’s numerous ad-
mirers to notice that his favorite action of rubbing
the top of his head when he is in doubt has
scratched away a little bald spot, which is be-
coming rapidly more visible.
On Thursday evening, at the Govt. Hons®
shivoo given in honor of visiting sheep-breeders,
it was noted with disgust by many members of
our “first families ” that the visiting-book wasn’t
as freely expurgated as of yore. The hard-and-
fast rules ignoring “ trade ” as a social factor were'
set aside, and haughty squatteresses and; bank-
manageresses rubbed shoulders with plebeians whc
retail tea and do other disgusting things. By the
way, there has been much talk, concerning the
dinner-table exploit of a sheep-breeders’ official
who, publicly taking up a collection for-next year’s'
show-prizes, asked Gov, Hampden, in presence of
the assembled company, whether he (the official)
understood that H.E. wished to rise with a sub-
scription. The Governor gave ten guineas, and!
was amiable enough not to resent the unprece-
dentedly familiar style in which.he was “hustled.”'
The trouble at St. Mark’s is over by the appoint-
ment of the Rev. Willoughby Flower, Archdeacon.
of Grafton, a clergyman of the popular variety.
He has a reputation for brains, and is “just right ”
in his views—knows exactly the proper width of a
chasuble’s hem and the proper time for placing
red and purple on the altar vases. After all their
tempestuous discussions, the congregation will
now rise and gratefully sing the hymn ending with
the lines
The bud may have a bitter taste
But sweet will be the Flower.
Gas struck work erratically on .Jubilee night
and with patchy effect. On one building, flaring
with the usual device and star, the words of fire
blazed forth as “ Australian Widows Fun ”
(Fund). Visitors from the back-blocks thought
the idea rather a good one that. while_ one English
widow was jubilating, Australian widows should
be taking the taste of bereavement out of their
mouths by having a little fun of their own.
This week (Thursday, Bth) the first Pleyel
Chamber Music Concert will be given at Pleyel
House. Violinist Staell leads the. “ strings,”
Sydney Moss plays the piano; Miss Blanche
Thomas, an effective contralto —yet another ruddy-
haired vocalist —will also assist. There has been
good booking for the opening' of this series, which
is attracting much interest among musical people.
A “ Record Reign ” March, by Eliza Longfield,
beginning with joy-bell effects, and meandering
into “Home, Sweet Home,” somehow reminds me
of the cruise of the “ Fiery Dragon ” on Jubilee
night.
The bestowal of a P.C.-ship on her untitled
husband is said to be extremely vexing to one
Premier’s wife, because the wives of P.O.’s don't
come into the table of precedence at all unless
hubby has already a handle to his name. She
would rather he had been dubbed “ Sir,” and then
she would be “ Lady,” and entitled to sail into
swell dinner-parties ahead of other women who
had previously assumed a far-distant expression
whenever she was around. The cablegram last
week deprived all the knightly-husbanded pre-
mieresses now at ’Ome of their handle, and they
all figured without distinction as plain “ Missis.”
At a R.C. Church, the other day, a bridal
party arrived at 7.30 a.m., and had the knot tied
at that early hour. What the bride and bride-
groom will have to do during the honeymoon is to
keep away from' remarking that “the earlybird
catches the worm.” For who is bird and who is
worm ?
A thanksgiving Jubilee demonstration was given
by the “ working girls of Sydney,” at Sydney
Town Hall, the other evening. Guvment ’Ouse
sent its contingent to preside, and no alleged
males, except the harmless and reliable clergy,
were admitted. Miss Lilian Frost took charge of
the organ, and sounded deep pedal notes to keep
feminine tongues in order occasionally : and when
a scrimmage at one end of the hall took place, the
Town Hall officials (men) were sent for ; then the
row subsided.
Customers of a certain solid bank, at an im-
portant mining centre, are accustomed to transact
biz. through clouds of smoke. It is a common
thing to see a lady waiting at the counter for
•change of a cheque and the teller counting it
cigar-in-mouth, while the accountant, sitting above
him, is also puffing vigorously.
Attending cooking-class is a prevalent winter
■craze in Melb. just now. The “professor ” is
■eloquent and competent, adopts the Socratic
method, and teaches by question and answer the
group of three or four score pupils whom he
attracts in different suburbs. “ Ladies,” he
queries appealingly, “ do you boil your ’am ? ” A
novice asks, blushingly, “What else should we do
with it?” The oracle smiles pityingly, “Oh,
never boil your ’am; bake your ’am.” Then he
explains the process while note-books are busy.
Again a question comes : “ Ladies, ’ow do you try
potatoes ? ” “A knife ? ” suggests a murmur from
the back row. “ No, not a knife ” —the teacher’s
manner is bland, but intense, with expressive
throwing up of hands—“ Never a knife, ladies;
take a ’at-pin.”
The children’s fancy-dress ball, to be held on
15th July in the Town Hall, is expected to raise
substantially the fund for the Consumptive Home,
which, despite the efforts of many energetic
workers, still languishes on the wrong side of
,£5OOO. Melb. has raised more than several times
that sum for its little jubilee charity flutter in the
same space of time, and Melb. considers itself
still in low water financially. But when a fund
has to be raised locally it is invariably, though un-
intentionally, bungled from the start bycliqueism.
The Nicest run the affair, and run it exclusively,
■or else the committee falls to pieces* before it is
fairly in working order through the intense
horror which one of the Nicest feels at seeing.her
name on the same list as that of some unmention-
able and unknowable woman who runs a sausage-
shop or a bakery. Too much Darling Point
swamps every charitable appeal. It is rough on
the exclusive appealers, because on them falls the
brunt of subscribing to every new fund which they
initiate. On them does it hail collecting-cards and
ball-tickets, and often are the boxes rattled at
street corners.
Meanwhile the public, out of earshot of the con-
fidential murmurs of high-toned committee meet-
ings, goes its own way, and the shillings and half-
crowns which the fund urgently requires re-
main still in the pockets of many who would
give the money but for a vague notion that a
fund engineered by the “toffs’’will be attended
to entirely by the “ toffs.” The churches know a
much better way of raising money. Were they to
depend on high pew-rents and large donations
from the wealthy and would-be wealthy they
would soon cease building altogether. It is the
steady influx of sixpences and shillings from
Bridget Maloney, the cook, and her class, that
pays the piper. What all local fashionable
■ charities need is to get at Bridget Maloney.
I hear that at a recent race-meeting Tocumwaj
'(N.S.W.) way, some aristocratic young bloods
amused themselves by hacking each other’s clothes
to pieces; and, while waiting for slop-made gar-
ments to come from the nearest store, arrayed
themselves *in potato sacks and danced a wild,
hilarious, and high-kicking can-can, winding up
this interesting performance by pouring cham-
pagne over eaqh other.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent: —
“ ‘ My friend from India,’Miss Alma Stanley,
in her drawing-room dance, upheld the theory
■current in every ball-room, that the larger the
person the lighter the foot. In these days of stage
magnificence, of silk linings, lace petticoats, and
superlative slippers, it was -surprising to detect
that the dancer’s shoe-soles \ had lost their first
freshness, and that a non-dpeorative selvage or
rough edge on its inner side marred the whirl of
her black skirt. \
“ Quite a number of Australian husbands went
’Ome ‘on business’ last year, and managed to
shilly-shally in London until over the Jubilee.
The wives, of course, saw through the move at the
time, and fought tooth-and-nail for a joint trip.
Some gained their point, others were less lucky.
In consequence, there are ip |Melb. at the present
i moment a host of disappointed matrons, hitherto
dutiful wives, who now breathe naught but
vengeance against their absent lords, protesting
how cheaply it could have been managed if only
Z had had the spirit of a mouse. Some
women, not such very big guns either, took court-
trains from Melb. in case of emergency. You. can
get one at a certain big cheap firm on this side of
the world for £2O. In London, especially in the
thick of a “ tail ” boom, you would give <£loo for
practically the same garment.
“ The awful system of royalising his Excel-
lency and party when supper-time arrives—a sys-
tem, by the way, which their exalted Excellencies,
emulating Greater People, inflict upon their own
guests at their own Government ’Oases —was
bound to be dragged into ridicule by inferior poten-
tates. The mayoress of a remote Melbourne
suburb gave a reception in the local ’all, the other
week. Her bounding ambition didn’t bound quite
as high as an invite to Lord. 8., but the worthy
matron produced two dazzling trump-cards in the
Mayor of Melbourne and a Cabinet minister.
These were not less great in her circle than the
Governor is great in his circle, and accordingly she
invited a ‘ select few ’ to join them in a special re-
fresh-room, shut out from the common herd, who
were left to refresh themselves at a buffet. The
common herd, professing to feel much hurt by this
social distinction, promptly withdrew from the
building in single file. They marched out snorting.
“ But, why ? The same sort of thing is done at
Government House, where the visitors are Lady
Brassey’s guests to more than the same degree
that ordinary visitors at a reception are guests of
the Mayoress who holds it. If it be right for the
vice-regals to grovel to their own greatness, in
addition to accepting grovel from other hosts, why
can’t a suburban councillor’s wife be allowed to
bow down before her municipal Sovereign—the
Mayor of Melbourne? A society that tolerates
court etiquette at second-hand has no right to
kick against any display of snobbery. If his Ex-
cellency at one end of the town is entitled to erect
himself and family on a platform, the boss Mayor
starring at the other end of the town seems by no
means incompatible with refreshments for a Select
Few.
“The first of the subscription concerts by the
Howard Conservatoire of Music (Melb.) was a
good show of students’ ability rather than a popu-
lar entertainment. During the interval, Gov.
Brassey unburdened himself of some laudatory
comments, after which the ancient mariner and
party toddled off without waiting for part 11.
The second half of the programme, as it happened,
was better suited to a rather sassiety audience
than the first half, wherein a Beethoven sonata
for the pianoforte had outstayed its welcome by
several minutes, for Beethoven, in his severe
moods, presses heavily upon the common or gar-
den kind of Philistine. One or two other numbers
were long enough, yet not too long to be likeable.
A string quartette performance of Schubert’s
‘ Death and the Maiden ’ was pleasant, in spite of
many imperfections, and the violin soli by clever
Miss May Nash (who was painfully nervous) and
Miss Guenett gave promise of laurels in store for
both young artists.
“ Apropos of feminine excitement as manifested
in the Jubilee crushes, sufferers, who felt them-
selves deliberately and viciously kicked on the
shins by flustered females, are still wearing the
bruises. One spitfire broke her umbrella over the
shoulders of a stout patriarch, remarking explana-
torily that fat people were best at home. In the
human whirlpool which surged nightly at the junc-
tion of Collins and Swauston streets two well-
dressed women looked for a moment like tearing
one another’s eyes out. One had accused the other
of ‘ digging ’ her with her ‘ razor elbows.’
“ The wife of the Premier of another province
was lionised in a mild way during her recent stay
in Melbourne. One of her hostesses, by way of
accentuating her guest’s importance, rustled about
lamenting in gushful whispers to the rest of the
company that ‘ Mrs. Z. was a trifle difficile, but
one must excuse it in the idol of “X.”’ (“X.”
standing for the Australian capital usually in-
habited by the alleged “ idol.”)
“ The recent ball at Govt. House was like its
forerunners —a suffocating crush. Two thousand
guests would have been a tight fit at any time,
but on this occasion ever so much of the available
space was railed off for the vice-regal party, which
made matters even worse than usual.
“ Proud parents no longer keep back the young
and tender debutante until a vice-regal dance
affords a suitable opportunity for her to blossom
forth. She and her frock get more battered under
gubernatorial auspices than anywhere else, and,
as a matter of fact, experienced ball-goers now
reserve the ugly ducklings of their wardrobe for
Govt. House festivities.
“ Women are notoriously bad shots. In W.A.,
the other day, a mine - manager’s wife was
christening an engine and had to throw a cham-
pagne-bottle at it. It was somewhat larger than
the proverbial haystack, and she was only a couple
of yards from it, yet she cleverly managed to miss
it and hit her hubby—-a portly person—below the
belt, knocking him into a cistern ten feet deep and
badly hurting him.
“ A few weeks ago a relay of relic/ieuses inau-
gurated as a new convent what was lately the
palatial residence of a boomster. The Mother
Superior, when going over the mansion, dis-
covered certain pagan and unholy designs in the
fresco of the front room, which she required the
agent to have painted over that very day. Later
on the good nun was horrified, when raising her
heavenwards in her mid-day devotions, to
find little naked oupids in flesh tints disporting on
the Arcadian ceiling. Sending at once for the
agent, she locked the door, and guarded the cham-
ber of horrors till the painter and factotum had
arrived on the scene. Holy water was then used
to rid the air of all impurities.
“A stranger in Kalgoorlie (W.A.) was puzzled
to hear strains of ‘ On the Benches in the Park ’
and ‘ Her Golden Hair ’ proceeding from the
Church of England building. On investigating
the matter he learnt that a social club hires the
sacred edifice for its fortnightly dances and sup-
pers. Things ecclesiastical do not boom in the
Golden West—tho Australian left to his own
devices is not a church-going animal. One of the
most money-making concerns in Kalgoorlie at
present is undertaking. The local ‘ planter ’
makes a clear profit of £lOO per month. He had
quite 50 funerals during the month of May.
“ One of the queerest figures seen on the Block
for some time is a golden-haired charmer who
sports a marvellously-long, woolly black cape in
which she looks for all the world like a prize collie.
Her gold nob just shows at the back, between a
drooping feathered hat and the great collar, and
the front view acquaints you with a sharp-featured,
powdered face through a black, trellis-work veil.
The effect of this figure in locomotion, with its
skirt-tail wagging and its head pointing from side
to side, is too funny. Old-stagers remember no-
thing so startling since Kate Foote, the tall
Gaiety girl, some four years ago, used to patrol
Collins-street in a plain, grey, cowboy felt hat
and a huge overcoat, in the pockets of which she
was wont to thrust her ungloved hands.
“ In a recent local housebreaking case a second-
hand dealer gave evidence that he ran what
practically amounted to a circulating ‘ library ’
in clothes. In his suburb, he said, ‘no leddies in
serciety liked ter wear the same things alius.
And consequently they paid him ss. a month for
the privilege of varying the pink blouse in which
they appeared at the local football club’s hop, with
a glorious confection of ostrich feathers and ribbons
for Saturday night wear, and a pea-green dolman
with big pearl buttons in which to scintillate when
they take their seats on the lorry booked for the
racecourse. The principle is excellent and capable
of almost illimitable extension.
Yours affectionately, Cleo.
In reference to a story by Henry Lawson,
in last issue of The Bulletin, it seems desirable
to make it clear that no reference was made, or
could possibly have been made, to Mr. Felix
Sehmellitscheck, the proprietor of the Foreign
Agency Co., at 82 Pitt-street, Sydney, and a gen-
tleman whose kindness in giving his services as a
musician in the cause of charity has made him
widely known and respected.
In the special Anglican Jubilee church
service the British are boldly spoken of as the
chosen race. Moses’ nose is right out of joint.
When murderer Butler was caught, there
was much nervousness among the sects, notone of
which desired him to claim it as his own. In a
word, none of the sects, which are supposed to
rejoice in the repentance of sinners, wanted the
Glenbrook butcher to pray in its yard. Mr.
Butler seems to have at last selected a ghostly
comforter. He says he is a Wesleyan.
Melb. : “ Coining home from the theatre,
last night, I was astounded to find in a gravestone-
yard, corner of Lonsdale and Exhibition streets, a
replica of the Ormond statue, which adorns the
front of Ormond College. I was as ‘ took-a-back ’
as Don Giovanni encountering the Commandant.
This replica is on a nice granite plinth, or pedestal,
which has borne an inscription, now rubbed out,
so that, returning by daylight, I could faintly
distinguish ‘ Ormond,’ with some undecipherable
catalogue of virtues. Then I made haste to assure
myself that the real Maginnis, at the college, had
not walked down off his perch.”
Church religion was revealed in a new and
nteresting light at a Melbourne inquest the other
day. Hear the seven-year-old son of deceased :
“Mrs. Blank told her husband there was poison
in the box and she intended to take it. He said,
‘ Well, take it.’ She emptied the contents of the
box into her mouth, and then threw the box itself
into the fire. Next, she drank some milk from a
jug, and became sick. During this time Blank
was sitting in the kitchen. He saw Mrs. Blank
take the poison, but did not see her vomiting. He
put on his hat and coat, and, having kissed wit-
ness and the second child, went to church.”
Quite a scene occurred in Melb. City Court,
the other day, when two “ respectable ” women
were charged with shoplifting—on five separate
charges, each from different shopkeepers—the
value of goods stolen being nearly <£lB. Lawyer
M‘Kean pleaded most ardently for his clients,
and finally his voice became broken and he
burst into tears. Then magistrate Panton
arose to remark that the bench was sworn to
“ administer justice without fear or favor,” and
that it was necessary to “make an example,” and
“ to pass over a succession of crimes was impos-
sible,” and so forth ; and then he sentenced them
to one week’s imprisonment—which, as they had
been under remand for more than that time,
meant immediate release. Great onions !
The jubilee served various Governments as
an excuse to get rid of some awkward problems.
The M.L. Ministry has been troubled for seven
years with the body of Louis Chemis, sentenced
in 1889 for a murder which almost everybody is
convinced he didn’t commit. It lately established
a court of appeal, and the act was intended to
allow Chemis a new trial and dispose of him that
way, but, by a stupid mistake, it was so drafted
that he didn’t come within its provisions. After
that the Government, lacking the moral courage
to give Chemis a free discharge, let things slide,
till the Jubilee came around. It then ejected him
from prison suddenly as an expression of loyalty,
and got rid of the difficulty that way. Still, it
might have done worse. The N.S.W. Govern-
ment, for instance, refused to reconsider Suffield’s
case even at Jubilee time, and Suffield was con-
victed upon evidence which almost proved his
innocence.
The painfully platitudinous E. J. H. Knapp
is at it again in the Sydney dailies, with a weirdly
incoherent assertion by an “ eminent barrister ”
that the cursed drink is at the bottom of nearly
all the divorces. But marital incompatibility is,
as often as not, in turn, at the bottom of the in-
temperance, and Knapp picks up the chain of
causation just where it comes handy for the silly
press screed beloved of Knapp and all his vvooden-
witted kind. Knapp isn’t calculated to illuminate
any subject, even if he knew what he was talking
about, but this is a case in which outsiders don’t
see most of the game, and Knapp, as a celibate
and teetotaller, is as gratuitous a circumstance as
the prurient old tabby who vexes her vinegar soul
about an “ age of consent ” which could never
have concerned her personally.
Dear Bulletin,— ln giving judgment in
favor of Mr. Norton, claimant in the interpleader
case of Allen v. Willis, N.S.W. Justice Simpson,
while correctly deciding the main point at issue,
said :
Parliament never intended that if a verdict was ob-
tained against one person or company, execution could
be made upon somebody else.
I would submit that in certain cases this is just
what Parliament did intend to do, and what Par-
liament has done. If A prints a libel with B’s
plant, then plaintiff can levy on B’s plant— as long
as B owns it. The words of the Act are that the
damages may be levied out of the plant “belong-
ing to the person whose types have been used . . .
as well as out of the property of the defendant on
the record.” The last phrase clearly points to
some person other than the one against whom
judgment has been obtained. —Rus. Roberts.

Joshua Bearn, the Streaky Bay (S.A.)
Butler, shortly to be hanged, was arrested when
in company with a half-caste mate, one “Buck-
akin,” who now tremblingly recalls that one night
he awoke to find someone walking about the
camp. He looked around and found Beard com-
ing towards him with uplifted axe. “ What you
doing, Mr. Beard ? ” Beard dropped the axe.
“ It’s all right, Buckskin—I’ve been cutting
sheoak,” said Joshua. Buckskin now says he is
afraid Beard was going to mistake him for a
sheoak-tree and thus add him to his little list of
accidents.
Said that C. G. Tegetmeier
will succeed Henry M'Kenzie as
general manager of the B.N.Z.
Tegetmeier is a native of Lon-
don, and graduated in the Lon-
don office. It is chronicled that
he is related to Walter Bagehot.
Prophet S. L. Rimmer,
speaking at Auckland on June
9, fixed the date of Christ’s
second coming for March of ’9B
—he was not particular to a
•day. If Christ should come
then, the first business to be
done would be to effectually
wipe out the prophet Rimmer
for disclosing State secrets. If
the Advent does not come off,
then the prophet should be torn
to pieces by the faithful. But
when next March comes Rimmer
will be all right he’ll have
“skipped,” either to America,
where apostle Worthington came
from, or to the nether regions.
Nankivell, the Vic. artist
who has had such a brilliant
career in Yankeeland, is a Prah-
ran native, and about five years
ago was a prominent figure in
local cricket circles, being cap-
tain of the Menindie Club.
Starved out of Australia, Nan-
kivell went to America, and in
a few weeks had the public at
his feet with his clever imita-
tions of prominent black-and-
white artists. With all his ex-
cellent draughtsmanship, Nan-
kivell is destitute of any style of
his own, but he can hit off an
imitation of Phil May, Beards-
ley, or Gibson, three men of
utterly distinct methods, with
such skill as to baffle the closest
observers.
The Ean of Hardwicke, who would have
been made Governor of Queensland had not the
Ministry heard that he was a broken-down turf
plunger, is dead. He was chiefly remarkable for
the splendor of his hats ; no other mortal man
ever succeeded in getting his tile to shine like the
Earl of Hardwicke’s. To whom he has imparted
the secret, or whether it has been buried with him,
the cable sayeth not. When Fauntleroy, the
London banker,, was about to be hanged for
forgery, two fashionables of his set visited him in
the condemned cell. “To morrow,” said one of
them, “you will appear before your God. Now
that the secret can no longer avail you aught, tell
us, we implore you, where you used to buy that
matchless curaejoa ? ”
Two eminent Tasmanians celebrate their
birthdays this month. Mrs. Humphrey Ward
will be 46 on July 12, and Federal Conventionist
W. A. Trenwith will be 50 on July 16.

duty on the last day of the S.A. financial year.
Which justifies Solomon’s remark that Treas.
Holder has “ the luck of a Chinaman’s horse.”

Lyons, who was crippled for life when bravely
trying to arrest the (since hanged) burglar Mont-
gomery three years ago, is starving with his wife
and family on a pension of 3s. 6d. a day !

Judge Moles worth appeared as witness in
a police-court case in Melbourne the other day, and
was so discursive in the box that he was several
times sternly commanded to “ keep to the point ”
by the perky counsel for the defence. Which goes
to show that it is easier to be a Judge than a
witness.
Died recently, a Mr. Edward Baly, one of
the pioneer teachers of Sydney. His own early
tutor was Robert Lowe. His early pupils included
John Kinloch, Consett Stephen, W. C. Windeyer,
and John Lackey. Through Lowe’s recommenda-
tion he obtained the head-mastership of Sydney
Grammar School.
Poor Ninian Melville, who died the other
day, was usually badly treated by reporters. Com-
ing out of Parliament one night, he ran against a
particularly obnoxious member of that craft.
“ Could you tell me,” said Melville, vindictively,
“ how to get an ounce of lead into a reporter
without breaking the law ? ”
The officers of the department, says N.S.W.
Just-ice Minister Ghould, are quite free from culp-
able negligence in re the severance, per rope, of
hanged murderer Moore’s neck. “ There seems,”
says the Minister, “to have been an unavoidable
error of judgment.” Probably the frozen one
means an error of execution.
London Spectator hails Henry Lawson as
“ the greatest Australian writer.” There is some
Spectator reviewer who keeps an eye on Aus-
tralian books. When Dr. Pearson was alive, he
used to do Australian subjects for the paper, being
a close personal friend of Meredith Townsend,
editor Hutton’s right-hand man.
Mrs. Jacob (wife of Mr. A. H. Jacob,
Chairman of Committees in N.S.W. Leg. Council)
died last week after a very long illness. Her
father, Col. Snodgrass, ruled as Acting-Governor
of both N.S.W. and Tasmania. He was reputed
in each Government to be a soldier of the old
type—a fighter first and reasoner after.
Premier Reid furnishes another proof of
the fact that the late Mr. J. Caesar knew what he
was talking about when he said, “ I would have
men about me that are Fat! ” Fat is Force —
fuel—reserve-energy. All Georgy-Porgy ever did
by way of exercise was—like Oscar Wilde —“a
little talking, now and then,” and he has more go
in him, at the right time, than any dozen of your
“lean and hungry-looking” Cassiuses. Wilde,
by-the-way, nearly died when most of his adipose
was treadmilled away, and got back fat and health
pari-passd.

During the fearful hurricane encountered
by the s.s. Aberdeen en voyage to London, a re-
markable incident occurred. A big sea caught the
captain and washed him overboard and out of
sight. Then another sea swept him back again on
to the deck almost as good as new.

Lee Steere, Speaker of W.A. Assembly,
was 67 years old on July 4. He is a stately old
gentleman and belongs to a family which has
shown its love for its native land by continuously
retaining in its possession the estate in Surrey
granted to the original Lee Steere by William the
Norman more than 800 years ago.
It is possible that Gov. Brassey will not go
’Ome in the Sunbeam in November, as he had at
first intended. He wants to take a trip to the
South Seas —possibly to look at Louis Becke’s
brown girls. He has not made up his mind yet,
but anyhow says that he has no sort of intention
of giving up the Yic. Governorship before his time
expires.
The Colonial Office intimated in a despatch
to one of the Australian viceroys recently, that
they could not consider the claims of Austra-
lian newspaper proprietors, as such, to Imperial
distinctions, but that there must be some outside
service to entitle them to special recognition.
Well, isn’t Mister James president of the
Y.M.C.A ?

An alleged centenarian lady who died at
Brunswick (Melb.), the other day, left two
daughters and 30 great-grandchildren, but no
g.g.-grandchildren. Interesting to know at what
age she was married and became a mother. Queen
Victoria, should she live, will probably be photo-
graphed with a g.g.g.-child on her lap long before
she reaches the nineties.
“ Alfred F. Culvert,” says M.L. Graphic,
“ was reputed to be worth 4500,000 in cash a year
ago. At that time a British and French syndicate
offered him 41,000,000 for his gold-mine holdings.
The offer was refused, and a course of reckless ex-
travagance was entered upon. Lately Mr. Calvert
has sold for 470,000 the properties that he refused
41,000,000 for a year ago.”
It would appear that, in any case, Railway-
Commissioner Eddy had not long to live. “He
had unmistakable symptoms of Bright’s disease, he
had had a sharp attack of pneumonia, and he was
beginning to be troubled with rheumatism and
gout. He was in the prime of life, being only 44
years old, and ought to have been in the prime of
strength, but he had worn himself out too fast.”
For solid, unadorned self-conceit it would
be hard to beat the British war-scribe who expects
people to take his own word for it that he and his
companion deliberately placed themselves under
fire to do their writing, in order to encourage the
Greek troops with whom they were. The story
would have come a lot more convincingly from
somebody else; and, even if it’s true, there’s no
heroism in mere gratuitous foolishness.

Romance of Life.
The story of Enoch Arden is a very common out
Husbands are continually turning up at incon veniea*
times, when they ought, as they are supposed u®
be, in another sphere. The whole history of loa»
heirs and defunct claimants shows that people who
are supposed to be dead and buried unexpectedly
appear upon the scene. But when a man has oetn
attended by doctors and subjected to every treat-
ment, and has been told that his case is hopeless
coming from such sources, it is generally accepted
as the inevitable.
However, experience proves conclusively tru.-,
many have been saved from the grave, d<snh»
such gloomy prognosis. Robert Wilson, Master
Schooner Saucy Jane, writing from New Heb -ine b
says: “About eighteen months ago I had oct isios
to visit Sydney, and while there was taken ill. I
went to the best doctors, but they could do nooning
for me, and I rapidly got worse and worse. I
then came on to New Caledonia and went into tint
hospital there. My spleen and liver were swelled
to an enormous size, and the pains in and unde*
my shoulders were unbearable : it being an* im-
possibility to lay down, I was obliged to sit up all
the time. After three months’ treatment I decided
to return to the islands and give Warner’s ‘ Safe '
Cure a trial, as I would sooner die than keep o»
suffering the way I was. After using seven
bottles of ‘ Safe ’ Cure and two phials of pills ttk«
pains left me, and I continued using the medicine
with the result that to-day I have not a pain or
ache in me. I thank the day that I used Warners
‘ Safe ’ Cure ; without it, I should certainly have
been in my grave long ago. It. is well worth it*
weight in gold. I say to anyone who is a suffer®?•
Go for Warner’s ‘Safe’ Cure with full confidence ”*
