POLITICAL
POINTS
The Q. Government consists of five lawyers
and three nominees.
Opinion of a visitor : “ Victoria is governed
chiefly by Presbyterians and policemen.”
Australian loyalty is as profound as Prin-
cess May’s attachment to the Duke of Cla ’ence.
Knighthood is like marriage and rat-traps.
Those who are out want to get in, and those who
are in want to get out.
The speech of one of the Q. Labor-mem-
bers on the female Japanese was so “ blue ” that
a moral Hansard baulked.
Northerners begin to wonder which side of
the fence John Plumper Foolin’ is on. So many
true words are said in joke.
The policy of Q. Lands Dept, hitherto has
been, apparently, to cut up all the inferior lands
into small areas and to lease or sell all the good
lands in large areas.
Said that Benjamin Hoare, of the Age, will
be run by boss Syme against little Dr. Maloney,
for West Melbourne, at the coming general elec-
tions. Both are R.C.
The very transparent attempts of the Q.
Govt, whip to indicate that labor-leader Glassey is
especially obnoxious to Tozerites are arousing, not
lulling, Labor-party suspicion.
Had the order of precedence in the Jubilee
procession of colonial Pretniers, in London, been
according to the numerical population of piebalds
and skewbalds, Queensland would have been easily
first.
“In N.S.W. Assembly,” says a member,
“ the underlying feeling is right against Federa-
tion, as nine-tenths of the members feel that,
under Federation, they would sink into insig-
nificance.” “ Let us reign in Hell ! ”
The amount of lobbying done over the
Sydney city railway business was so suggestive as
to be disgraceful. Nor did the whispering all
come from the property-owners concerned : a lot
of it came from members of the Public Works
Committee itself ! JStink 1
“SOMETIMES A LIGHT SURPRISES.”
Dr. Watts-his-Name.
THE DRUNK : “ Shay, old sport, thought you, said
you — hi c— weren’t going to have any Jubilee
’lluminations? ”
During May 1251 people left Victoria for
Westralia and 1253 arrived in Victoria from the
Western province. This looks like the first sign
of the turning of the tide. Hitherto the surplus
has been always going westward, but apparently
the West is full for the time being.
Q. Govt, has despatched hired liars to Eng-
land, Ireland, Europe and America to try and
induce people to swarm to the Northern Mottle-
land to “ settle upon (or under) the soil ” while it
actually has £250,000 in hand as the paid fees of
applicants for land who can’t get land.
Rad. : “ Not a word about Princess Louise
of Wales and the Duke of Fife. Here is a prime
mischief of regal government, the gunpowder
material of conspiracies, lying about. A year or
two ago Labouchere pointed out how close this
Princess Louise might be to the throne. Surely,
in the mere course of human nature, the Duke of
York’s children must be irritants to her.”
The vagaries of Queensland politics have
brought Labor-inember Macdonald into line with
J. V. Chataway, one of the Tozeraith supporters
in the Assembly, on the question of sugar-bounties
to enable sugar-growers successfully to oppose the
“ unfair competition of beet-sugar-bounty coun-
tries. ”
There are certain islands (Lady ELiott,
Bunker Group) off the Q. coast covered with rich
deposits of guano. The Q. Govt., with its cus-
tomary recklessness, has sold the right of removing
aforesaid manure to a British syndicate for .£BO
per annum. And just within hail of Lady Elliot
Island are impoverished sugar-plantations starved
for want of this very material.
In March, 1897, the total population of
Thursday Island was 1344. Europeans numbered
571; Malays and Manilla men, 150 ; Chinese, 73 ;
aborigines, S. Sea Islanders, and mixed races,
213 ; and Japanese, 331! Aliens, 773 to 571 Euro-
peans. By the way, Thursday Island is called the
Gibraltar of Australia. Is it because, like that
famous rock fortress, it bids fair to be the posses-
sion of a power alien to its rightful owners ?

“ Owdodo ” to The Bulletin
You state that Parliament has no authority at this
stage either to accept or reject the Convention Bill, and
that members’ opinions on Federation are as useless and
irrelevant just now as their opinions about the lost grave
of Moses. It is, however, just because P. has no authority
either to accept or reject the Bill that it becomes neces-
sary for those who see in it a dangerous invasion of the
rights of the people to pound unceasingly at it. The
objective is entirely different from that of a debate on
an ordinary Bill which, in theory at least, is conducted
with a view to influencing votes. In the present discus-
sion the objective is the information of the people who,
seized with a vague sentiment in favor of Federation,
may unthinkingly disregard details. The conspiracy of
silence on the part of the dailies renders still more neces-
sary the individual utterance of members’ opinions. Nor
cari it be fairly stated that the scholarly though, perhaps,
too didactic speech of Piddington, the spirited and bril-
liant utterance of Hughes, and the passionless logical
eloquence of Ashton, are so much wasted jaw and per-
functory howl.
“Nevermind” Nelson : “The mission of
Queensland is to form part, of one of the pillars
which support the Empire.” More whisky, please,
while the bibber rises to state that the mission of
Queensland, at present helped along by Nelson,
is to carry Bull, “the Old Man of the Sea,’ on its
shoulders, and its own piccaninny, the Slave
Trade, under one arm, with the Chow and the
Jap. fastened, cat-like, on to its hip. The other
arm is mercifully left free to enable Mongrelia
to wipe the £7O-per-head debt-drops of per-
spiration off its brow, and to indicate
when its throat is getting too near to
being strangled.

Young Rupert
Clarke’s speech on be-
ing pitched into Vic.
Leg. Council Chamber
by a doting constituency
is a precious effort, and
should be printed large, glazed,
and framed, and hanged in that
high hall. A small, but elegant, specimen:—
This is the proudest moment of my life. It is a great
compliment to me as one of the youngest men elected to
the 1 pp r House, not on account of my own qualifications
for ti e position, but merely because of my father’s good
actions.
Why was S’rupert allowed to give the show
away in this bold and banal manner? Doesn’t
everybody know that he has inherited the title
and most of the cash, and were not these his
father’s “ qualifications ” ?
Apparently fearful lest the “God-fearing
Ministry ” should, by its anti-gambling and
Scotch - Sunday posturings, monopolise clerical
support, N.S.W. Opposition leader Lyne gives
notice of a resolution that God shall be recognised
in the preamble to the Constitution Bill.
Argus wails about the weakness of the
Opposition in the Vic. Assembly. Practically the
Argus is the Vic. Opposition and is snivelling
over its own ineptitude. The Leg. Council is the
real Vic. Opposition, though, behind which Fat-
man hugs his privileges in peace. Whilst the
Council remains, a feeble Conservative Opposition
in the Assembly counts for little either way.
Premier Braddon is reported as proudly
stating in England that “ Tasmania will soon be
able to reduce its land and income taxes and to
remit the duties on certain products.” Tasmania
has had eight deficits in the last twelve years, and,
in proportion to its revenue, has the biggest accu-
mulated shortage in the seven provinces to wipe
out, so if the Braddon Government is found
reducing anything at any time within the next
ten years it should get three months’ hard labor
for dishonesty.
In Kingston’s absence the S.A. Parlia-
ment has begun its sittings amid violent storms.
The direct Opposition led by Downer has been
reasonably quiet, but Grainger, who is practically
a free lance, has been hurling wild charges of
fraud at Acting-Premier Holder, and only retract-
ing under extreme pressure. Some Ministerialists,
on the other hand, charge Grainger and Solomon
with “conspiracy,” and Kingston’s presence with
the famous gun that he was going to fire at
Chaffey Baker is much needed to keep order
The Braddon Ministry will have put in
years of office this month, and things are so calm
in Tasmania now that it should have a fair to
break Fysh’s record of 5 years and 41 months. The
other Tas. Premiers with a longer record than Brad-
don’s are Francis Smith years), James Whyte
(3 years, 10 months), James Wilson years), and
Giblin (four years, 10 months). Barring Queens-
land and Westralia, Tasmania’s Ministries, on an
average, are the longe t-lived in Australia, Wes-
ton, who lasted only 17 days, being almost the
only Tas. Premier who was cut off suddenly in his
political youth.
Not long ago, Q. “Lands” Foxton told
the Rockhamptonians that the sale of the Corowa
and Katandra lands in the Central division was
made for the purpose of effecting settlement in
small holdings. How ? The Govt, resumed
114,000 acres, and. immediately let the owner of an
adjoining block buy —(1) 10,000 acres ; (2) 22,000
acres; and (3) 19,000 acres. Small holdings!
And now Foxton has confessed to Q. Assembly
that the land was sold to rake in revenue, much to
his personal regret. “ I had to give up my most
cherished convictions,” says he. What’s the lump
in his cheek? His tongue ? Yea, verily !
Queensland Minister Dickson, neatly
balanced on a rail :
Whilst not on the one hand a Protectionist, nor on the
other a Freetrader, yet I believe in such a readjustment
of the tariff which, while not unduly increasing the
burdens upon the people, will produce such an increment
to the exchequer as will result in an equilibrium between
the public revenue and expenditure.
And the audience, deeply impressed by the im-
posing words “readjustment,” and “ increment,”
and “ exchequer,” and “equilibrium,’ let-it pass.
If Dickson had added that he firmly believed in
the interna ionalisation of pelargoniums in Meso-
potamia without creating any unexpected ther-
mometer in Ramoth-Gilead, lie would have been
even more impressive and just as definite.

AT THE PICTURE SHOW.
“And these are works of art!”
“Some of them others are works of imagination !”
The Imperial ennobblement of Cuthbert,
M.L.C., Vic., is as great a political mystery aa
was the “elevation” of MTntyre. Both are so
palpably gone in their democratic knees as to
render the hobbles a wasteful and ridiculous
excess, and, as Conservative powers, they are
mere ear wigs.
Brisbane (Q.) Observer was surprised that
Acting-Premier Tozer accepted a title, because he
“ has become associated in our minds with men
like Mr. Gladstone—or Mr. Glassey.” Satirical?
Not a bit. The Observer merely hedged with
Glassey, according to office tradition, and then
proceeded to butter the Tozer bread seriously.
Vic. Commissioner Mathieson flatly denies
the charge of employing an undue proportion of
bov-labor on the railways. The percentage of
apprentices to mechanics, he says, was 21 per
cent, in '94 and is now only 1 per cent., and even
with the proposed increases he claims that the
Vic. lines will have the smallest percentage of
boy-labor in Australia.
Vic. M’s.P. are hurt because the Turner
Govt, has put its boot down upon the free-pass
abuse in the matter of deadhead dockets handed
out to politicians’ relatives at the opening of
Parliament. Previously, these passes have enabled
the sisters, and the cousins, and the aunts of
members, also their brothers-in-law, and their boss
supporter, and his brothers-in-law, and the corner
publican, and Totty, the barmaid, to use the rail-
ways as their own for an indefinite period, and
rush around, first class, without limit or reason.
Now the member gets a ticket for two, his wife
and his mother probably, and they can only use
this for one return journey, so many M’s.L.A.
are choke-full of disgust, and want to know if
they are still living in a free country.
A recent Sydney death notice regarding a
late lamented Chinaman ended with the words:
“ Deeply regretted by his two daughters, aged 54.”
The grief which makes any lady advertise her age
in this reckless fashion must be indeed profound.

Dr. Gore Gillon, respondent in the recent
well-advertised and abortive Sydney matrimonial
case’ has now the pleasure of footing an enormous
bill for costs on joint account. Note this, ye
woman-shriekers at the injustice of that Monster,
Man ! Here, you see, a woman can drag her hus-
band into court, overhaul his character and her
own with much public scandal, and escape
pecuniarily scot-free from the whole proceeding by
charging the bill to him, with the approval of a
man-made law !
The name of Walter Bradley has its histori-
cal associations in Sydney ; and the man who now
answers to that name is a prominent Sydney
figure to-day as he stands upon his rostrum with
his hammer uplifted and his top-hat tilted some-
what on the back of his head. Walter Bradley,
senior (he died in ’93), was a progressive citizen ;
he was one of the chief founders of the N.S.W.
Royal Agri. Soc., and he founded the Zoological
Soo. all on his own. He whacked his hammer on
the works of art and general effeots of Henry Mort
when that first of Australian fathers was “leaving
for England by the Duncan Dunbar.” He auc-
tioneered Robert Lowe’s effects; Sir John Robert-
son’s effects ; Sir Edward Strickland’s and the
Hon. Thos. Cadell’s. He also watched a certain
beloved politician drink two bottles of whisky in
one afternoon. Bradley, father and son, auction-
eered the biggest art collections ever sold in Syd-
ney—viz., the Wallace and the Douglas collec-
tions. The firm also conducted the big sale for
the late Hon. Jas. White; while yesterday
it disposed of the late George Hill’s effects
WALTER BRADLEY, a well-known Sydney
man,
Photo., Kerry.
#—the biggest sale for a long time in Sydney.
The firm used to do its business in the building
where S.M. Herald machinery now groans under
the weight of the leaders ; and, at present, Walter
Bradley and Co. hang out in a thorough old
Sydney landmark—premises occupied by the
N.S.W. bank in the days when a garden grew in
front of the bank counter, and the barracks stood
over the way, and the bullock teams lugged and
swore for miles along old Parramatta Road, and
the local aristocracy also swore, and called each
other Jack and Jim, and filled each other up with
rum, and shot ducks down Hay-street, and went to
the races in carriages drawn by dogs, and fought out
there and got run in. These things ended when the
name of Bradley was painted on the doorway,
and the hat of Bradley was tilted on the head.
The present Walter Bradley is a straight-out Aus-
tralian, an old King’s School boy—good shot, good
cricketer, good all-round sportsman. Also, he is
a top man in his line of business, and intends
shortly to get top some more by stepping into
Parliament in the right way. He did a twelve-
months’ trip to Europe in ’94, and came back a
stauncher Protectionist than ever ; and since then
it has been his ambition to help stop this country
from being eaten up by the Foreign-trade party.
Even if there is but an oft’ chance of any
scientific gain from an examination of Butler’s
brain, there is absolutely no reason why that
chance shouldn’t be grasped at. At all events, the
affectation of any sentimental consideration for
a small portion of Butler defunct is sheer impudent
hypocrisy on the part of a community that doesn’t
value Butler, alive and complete, any more than
to strangle him in cold blood !
A Melbourne man was tried for bigamy
the other day. He had married a barmaid, or
the barmaid had married him, at a local matri-
monial agency, when he was too drunk to remem-
ber that he had one wife at home already. Case
dismissed. The officiating person was a duly-
qualified parson, too. “Do you take this woman
to be your wedded wife ? ” “ Yesh (hie!), I’ll
take either of ’em.”
Part of a Melbourne unemployed deputa-
tion the other day went so far as to shake his fist
at Acting-Premier Isaacs, and threatened to stoush
that eminent man in his own office. The visible
embodiment of the law was called in, but the
deputationist lay down in Isaacs’ office, and had
to be trailed along the carpet to get him out.
Apparently an Acting-Premier doesn’t inspire so
much respect as the real live-meat article.
Thus an exchange:—
Had his age permitted it, one of the most appropriate
spectators at the unveiling of Gov. Phillip’s statue in
Sydney would have been Mr. Thomas John, senr., of Com-
leroy Road. Mr. John is the son of one of Gov. Phillip’s
soldiers, who came out in the first fleet.
But there is some error here. No soldiers came
out in the first fleet.
Fitzgibbon, the sewer-bard of Melbourne,
did address a jubilee ode to Queen Victoria, after
all. The ode came out at the last moment, and
although it was but a small ode ’it was sufficiently
blatant, every verse beginning with a frenzied
“ hail 1 ” as per sample :
Hail to the Queen 1 Holy and homely,
Perfect in life,
Daughter and wife,
Mother and widow, loving and comely.
“Flat-iron” sends to The Bulletin this
incredible-looking story:—
Recently a youth was charged, at Wangaratta (Vic.)
court, with stealing 2s. 6d. from his employer. The
latter, a wealthy landowner, had engaged him to clear
and burn the timber off 200 acres of forest land at the
munificent rate of threepence per acre, and keep himself
in food, &c. £2 10s for a winter’s job—ye gods ! Do
you protect the sweater? Let Chief-Sec. Peacock make
the landowner a factory and fix a living-wage, and he will
have done something towards providing old-age pensions.
For midwinter rains the showers which
have lately fallen out back have been among the
best on record. Cold rains would inevitably have
killed more of the famishing sheep than the actual
drought has done ; but in the midst of a very cold
winter these rains, though not heavy enough in
most far-back parts to fill the tanks, came in
gently and with a delightfully mild change, under
which scrub revived and grass sprang “ like
magic.”
The evangelic howl of “ Where was Nan-
sen’s God?”--like the local shriek about the
Deity’s “omission” from the Federal Constitu-
tion—is based upon stubborn blindness to the fact
that it is never the praetically-religious man who
is continually turning God over in his mouth. If
Nansen believes in God, he probably believes also
that God doesn’t need reassurance on the point at
every second page ; if he doesn’t believe in God,
he wouldn’t be any the less an atheist for being
more of a hypocrite.
The London Dental Institute, of 98 King-
street, Sydney (now in charge of Dr. S. Lever, a
doubly-qualified dentist, and Mr. S. D. Asher,
both favorably known in the dental world), draws
attention to the fact that another operating room
has been added to the establishment. The new
department is reserved for conservative work in
general—-such as treatment of teeth, gold fillings,
pivot teeth, gold caps and gold and porcelain
crown and bridge work. All this is done at
reform fees, so as to bring such work within the
reach of all who may be desirous of saving their
teeth, instead of losing them. There is no amateur
work turned out of this establishment, whether
artificial teeth on plates, bridgework, or fillings.
Dental science has now reached such a stage that
all dentists should be able to guarantee a perfect
fit as far as artificial teeth a,re concerned, and no
toothache after filling has been inserted. Such,
at any rate, is the practice of the London Dental
Institute.
“But for this being Jubilee week we
should fine you -610,” was the remark made by
O’Connor, J.P., of Richmond (Vic.), to a man
who had used undressed language in the streets
and savagely battered a constable. It being
Jubilee week the prisoner got off with £3. It is
difficult to follow the ideas of tfie typical J.P.,
but he seems to hold that people should not object
so much to awful language at a Jubilee as at
other times —in other words, that it is more appro-
priate then’ than Usual. Also, that a policeman
should be kicked in the eye more freely and at
less cost when his Queen has reigned 60 years than
on ordinary occasions. Another individual who
let off fireworks in the crowd, and nearly blew up
sundry loyal citizens, was released upon the strong
protests of one J.P., who thought he should be
let off by reason of the Jubilee ; and in the case
of an individual who banged a cabman’s horse on
the head with a big stick, the Bench took the
trouble to explain at great length that even the
Jubilee couldn’t fully justify his conduct. If the
news gets about that man may batter and blow up
his fellowman, and maul his neighbor’s horse with
a club almost gratis at Jubilee times, there will
arise a general demand that future monarchs
should die young.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The great anti-betting crusade in N.S.W. ia
apparently flickering out. At the beginning the fine on
a first conviction was £lO, but when a recent batch of
cases came on the highest fim- for a third conviction was
£lO, and some first offenders were let off as low as 40s.
Also, though many bookies who were heavily fined haven’t
paid, nothing dreadful has happened. The tote and
“ double ” shops, meanwhile, are doing business as openly
as ever and quite unmolested.
* • * #
Aurum’s record weight—for a three-year-old—of
Bst. 61b. in the Melb. and Caulfield Cups, shows the handi-
cappers to be agreed as to the moderate quality of the
older horses engaged. A thumping weight for Aurum
was inevitable. In describing ihe probabilities of the’9s
Melb. Cup, The Bulletin argued that a good three-year-
old would whack the veterans, and that Auraria justified
the argument by beating Hova. Last year, with Hova
and the crack youngsters of ’95 all laid on the shelf,
The Bulletin again predicted that Melb. Cup would
fall to a three-year-old, and Newhaven, after winning
his Derby in a canter, “ walked home ” for
the big handicap. Now Newhaven has left the
country, and the only notable representative of classio
form included in the Cup entries is Wallace, whose racing
career was probably ended when he broke down last
Spring. The all-round quality of the older horses appears
so very moderate that Aurum, as the boss youngster of
his season, is weighted 71b. higher than the burden which
Newhaven made light of last year, not because thehandi-
capper reckons Aurum a half-stone better three-year-old
than the phenomenal chestnut, but because the p.c. won
his Melb. Cup with more than that amount in hand.
Merry Boy, on the bare fact that he won the
V.R.C. Handicap (carrying 7st. 121 b.) last Nov., looks
lightly handicapped for the Melb. Cup at Bst. 41b. At
any rate, the nuggety, common-looking horse should hold
O’Trigger safe, presuming that he didn’t suffer a serious
injury through his mishap in Sydney. In the case of
Bravo, years ago, a victory in the V.R.C. Handicap fore-
shadowed a Melb. Cup win, and The Merry Boy, with a
few pounds less than Bravo’s weight, will not have such
sterling animals as Carbine and Melos against him. Time
enough, however, to think about the “ Boy” after he has
been seen again in public.
* * * *
The Bulletin’s attempt to guess the winner of
the G.N. Hurdle Race, at Fleinington, proved a ghastly,
failure. Ten horses were mentioned as likely ones ; only
four of them started in a field of 18, one of those four fell
and broke his neck, and neither of the other three got
placed. Tipsters in general, and punters at large, re-
peated their last year's miscalculations. Buzzi won for
the second time, at an outside price, followed by Castillo
(25 to 1), Deposed (40 to 1), and Conventioner(2s to 1). A
cruel satire upon the intelligence of the betting-market.
The popular mistake this year was in backing
young and unseasoned jumpers to give a lot of weight to
a horse that had already proved himself a stayer. Half-a-
mile from home, Soult and So-and-So had command of
the situation, but, at the end of the 3-mile journey, they
dropped away, outpaced by three light-weights. Had
the going been at all heavy, the defeat of the favorites
would have been more decisive. Buzzi last year won a
muddy race with several pounds to spare.
Handicapper Dakin appears to have reck-
oned that Buzzi’s first win was more or less of a
fluke — i.e., a result aided by exceptional circumstances—
and the public followed the handicapner’s opinion. In
the light of last Saturday’s surprise Buzzi must be set
down as a good horse, specially dedicated to Grand
National hurdle-races. His victory in 1896 came after a
sequence of eight defeats over shorter courses. Then he
was laid aside for many months with a view to annexing
the same event a second time, and the handicapper gave
him a great chance to do the trick by letting him in once
again among the under-ten-stoners. Buzzi’s owner is a
lucky man.
For the G.N. Steeplechase next Saturday, it
goes without saying that Zouroff has an apparently great
chance. He proved his speed and staying power last year
by running second to Buzzi over the hurdles, and if he
can get over the bigger jumps without an accident,
Zouroff, with list. 31b. to carry, must be better worth
backing than most of them. The Bulletin will venture
another tip—Zouroff in preference to Romsdal, Walter,
Nipper and Hayseed.
When Buzzi was making his effort last Satur-
day, an excited sportsman roared persistently from the
upper grand stand : “Stick to him, Alf. 1” The nearer
the horse got to the winning-post the louder and faster
roared the voice from afar off “ Stick to him, Alf. ! ” It
was Mark Whitty talking to his boy, Alfred, on Buzzi’s
back, and forgetting in his excitement that he was wast-
ing words. But, anyway, he had the satisfaction of seeing
Alf. stick like a postage-stamp.
• • * *
Though unlucky with So-and-So, last Saturday,
the Ballarat stables are sure to have an innings shortly,
as not only have they a good chance of winning G.N.
Steeple with Walter, but Dreamland and Wait-a-Bit are
promising Caulfield and Melb. Cup candidates.

Owing to internal dissensions amongst the S.A.
bookies, they are laying the good odds of 2UUO—I the two
Cups. Victorian Ikeys only offer 500—1.
* * * •
Duddingston was a great public favorite
throughout last week for Canterbury Park Handicap,
N.S.W., but he was scratched, and the stable tried to win
with Wingello, who only ran second. Such tactics are so
common in Sydney that the disappointed backer does not
even raise a groan when the horse returns to weigh. In
England, the other day, Calvert's Clwyd, a winner under
similar circumstances, had to be escorted back by a guard
of prize-fighters, amidst the groans and execrations of a
furious mob.
• • • *
N.S.W. cast-off Response (Richmond —Bis Bis)
easily won V.R.C. Maiden Hurdles last Sat., and, as
jumping practice will likely instil confidence into this
brilliant though shifty mare, her chance for Caulfield
Cup must not be altogether despised.
• • • *
Key’s 7st. 101 b. is not the heaviest impost
allotted a three-year-old in the Melbourne Cup. Volley
was awarded a like burden in ’BB.

k petty fortress and a humble hand, so Larrikin, the

neok at the “small sticks” game.

. Re horse nomenclature, merely mixing up the
names of sire and dam, and taking half of each, doesn’t
necessarily make a suitable name. Originality is required.
Perhaps no two horses are better christened than Ice-
Cream, by Young Snowden-Mystery, and The Spud, by
The Rake-Miss Murphy

Two Year Old Handicap last Saturday, is in the same
stable as the Flying Key whose meeting with 'Aurum in
any race is anticipated with great interest. Rumour hath
it that there is a “ darkey ” in the same stable better than
either of the pair. If this is so the Derby won’t, after all,
be an exercise canter for Aurum.

(Martini-Henry— Vain Glory) has been sold at a good
figure for Singapore, whither he will be accompanied by
Ante (Antoeus—Venapa).

for his Two Thousand Guineas and Derby failures by land-
ing, for Lord Rosebery, the Princess of Wales’ Stakes of
£lO,OOO. Now that this colt has, seemingly, returned to
form he may be found capable of stretching the neck of the
popular idol, Galtee More, in the St. Leger.

for Melb. Cup is ridiculous, considering how uncertain it

starting his mini turn in p arvo champion under such a
weight as Bst. 61b.
* * * *
One Griffiths has put up a club-swinging record
at Newcastle (N.S.W.) with 36 hours and 220 combina-
tions. How can such a dreary exhibition be classed as
sport ? Yet we are told that a committee of idiots
attended all the time to feed him by means of an invalid
cup with beef-tea, port, &c. It’s as sickening as that other
record —Queen Victoria’s salary-drawing.
* * * *
Some 25,000 people rushed early to Sydney
Cricket Ground to howl and yell while 2£ tons of live
human meat mortified itself by assault and battery.
The 30 players all wore numbers upon their
bodies. As the Maorilanders came out they looked, in
their black jerseys, a harder and more square-shouldered
set than the N.S.W. light blues. And they are. All but
three or four of them are horny-handed—laborers, farmers,
bricklayers, carpenters, &c. Such are the footballers cul-
tivated in M.L.; when they get seriously to business, they
simply knock chips off their opponents. They played the
roughest game seen on Sydney Cricket Ground for many
a day ; they crippled a big man like Abbott so badly that
he had to be carried off the field ; they opened veteran
Warbrick’s scalp, and played the very devil with N.S.W.
shins in the scrum. And they cowed and smothered one
or two elegant N.S.W. civil servants, who had been put
into the home team by mistake.
Though they had never played as a combination
before, and had only been off the steamer a couple of
days, the Maorilanders pressed the play back into the
N.S.W. twenty-five the whole time, and when they were
not close to the N.S.W. line they were closer, and the
only time they ceased the offensive was when they
stopped altogether for the ambulance brigade to rush
upon the scene with splints and bandages, and to patch
up somebody who was more or less dead. They played
with more knowledge, more coolness, more pluck, and
more unselfishness than did the N.S.W. team, and the
only reason why they shouldn’t deal it out still hotter to
N.S.W. in the return match is that they are inclined to
eat too much, and to generally live too well. The only
possible things to beat M.L. next Saturday are the hotel
table and the little perfumed handkerchief.
This season’s football in M.L. has been re-
markable for evenness of form. No man of the present
visitors is distinct for individual brilliancy. Some of the
visitors, however, claim that Orchard, their back, is not
only the best back in M.L., but the best ever seen there.
He didn’t play up to his reputation last Saturday, other-
wise his team would have won by 13—5 instead of 13—8.
The most experienced man of the Maorilanders is Bailey
(the captain), a farmer, and one of the two or three
married men of his side. He has been playing first-class
football over 10 years, and was vice-captain with the ’93
M. team in N.S.W. Also, he is the oldest man of his
side —over 30 ; the youngest is Brooker (19 years), who
played so well as a junior last season that he finds him-
self picked for the representative tour. The rest of the
side average about five years at first-class football.
The M.L. side that played last Saturday could
■only have been improved by substituting Mills for Hard-
castle. Yet Hardcastle’s play was Al, he shone more
than any N.S.W. man did. Note his splendid run through
N. and his unselfish passing of the ball at the last
moment to Allen, who couldn’t help getting the try. Take
them as a side, the Maorilanders fed one another hand-
somely. Several of the Sydney players are stupidly selfish.
The only Maori in M.L. v. N.S.W. was veteran
“ Billy ” Warbrick, who played with N.S.W. No Maori
in M.L. just now is considered good enough by the M.L.
selectors. They say, indeed, that the Maoris of the
Hawke’s Bay Union team are the only ones that reach
■even first-class club form this season. And the Hawke’s
Bay district is notably rich in Maoris.
The M.L. team comprises 14 North Islanders
■and onlj' 6 South. The South is very sore about it. The
match North v. South, just before leaving for N.S.W.,
was consequently very willing. Millsand Armit had their
shoulders seriously hurt. Taranaki, top province for two
years, has 5 representatives and Wellington 4.
To sum up, the chief points of the Maoriland-
N.S Wales football-match were : brutally rough play in
the first half by Maoriland, and ditto by both teams in
the second ; unselfish play on the part of the Maoris; and
selfish play by N.S.W. M.L. started a rough game, played
a deal of offside, and kicked freely in the scrums. Their
unselfish play and combination no doubt won the match
for them, as they scored a couple of very soft tries owing
to want of combination by N.S.W. Abbott’s uniform
selfishness had one bright exception—when he fed Wick-
ham, the result being a score.
* * • *
The football craze : Abbott leaves his solicitor’s
business, in Wyalong, to come to Sydney to get laid out
by the M.L. sons of toil. Football and the lawyer’s pro-
fession can never work together.
• * * *
Mick Dooley should have a very soft thing with
Felix. If Dooley retains anything of his old form Felix
would not hit him in a month of Sundays.
*
Think of the number of men who would have
■done comfortably in life but for their sporting bias.
■C. T. B. Turner, “ the Terror,” says “ cricket loses a man
his chance in life.” Here and there a successful athlete
acts sensibly. A. G. Steel, who one season was best bat,
best bowler, best fieldsman in England, slung the whole
baby-show when he started his law-business. How often
his case used to be cited by Percy M‘Donnell! No man
ever spoke more bitterly about the cricketer s bubble re-
putation than did M’Donnell. H. Moses, by the way, is
one Australian who stuck hard down to business and
would have none of the trip to England.
* * * *
Cycle road-racing seems a trifle worse than
steeplechasing. The Vic. season just starting and already
two fatal falls. Young Robinson’s death last Saturday
was through exceptional hard luck—he was on level road
with nobody near him when the crown of his machine
cracked and threw him head-first on the metal. Against
a faulty machine the best rider is powerless to contend.
In cycling, so much depends on the way a race
is ridden that 20 men of equal merit might he started in
20 different races and a different competitor win every
time. The man who gets home under all conditions is
the real champion, and that is the sort Bilmartin was at
the end of last year, and the sort Bananalander Walne is
to-day. In the big race of the Melb. Jubilee meeting, the
spectators quite forgot it was a handicap to see cracks
like Walne, Body and Relph battling home for places, and
in that final run of his the form proved once more that he
is unquestionably the best Australian who ever crossed a
wheel.
Where has the much-advertised Parsons in any
way approached the record of Walne, about whom there
is little fuss ? Parsons now and again rode brilliant races,
hut not his most ardent worshipper can accuse him of
being constantly good. Yet day after day, week in and
week out, Walne beats the cracks of Australia repeatedly,
and those outside swoops of his at the finish are really as
brilliant as the best of Parsons’ work.
Walne rode a bicycle for the first time in May,
1896, and started to race four months later — i.e., he has
not yet completed his first year’s racing, and yet he has
over £7OO to his credit in prize-money alone. Where is
there a first year’s rider with a better record ?
• * * *
A Canadian visitor who witnessed a lacrosse
match in Adelaide, recently, between the two leading
teams, N. Adelaide and University, described the game as
being equal to anything he had seen in Canada or Eng-
land. The S.A. team which played Victoria last Saturday
(3rd) was, with the exception of two players, the strongest
S.A. could place in the field. The game has become im-
mensely popular in Adelaide, and has completely under-
mined football.
• » * *
Lacrosse needs even more endurance than foot-
ball, as there is none of the delay which accompanies that
game, the ball never being out of bounds. The S.A. team
which played in Melbourne consisted almost entirely of
noted runners, particularly Fotheringham, R >sman,
Drew, and Downer. Much skill is also required, but the
great drawback in the game is that the spectators cannot
see the little rubber bail which is used, and the excite-
ment is not wrought up to the extent that it is with two
evenly-matched football teams. This is the chief reason
why lacrosse will probably never supplant football as a
gate-money game.
'** * *
Jack Donahoo, Melb.’s champion heavy-weight
lawyer (20 stone) runs Premier Turner’s law biz. during
his jubilation pilgrimage.

The Sydney Cycle Co. Ltd. is doing big busi-
ness with the Cleveland wheel at its show-room and
riding-school, basement of Equitable Buildings, George-
street, Sydney. Cleveland racers won the programme at
Wellington, N.S.W., June 21 ; won the Narrabri Mayor’s
Cup ; five firsts from scratch at Dubbo ; while, at the
N.S.W. Wheelmen’s V.R. meeting, they scored two firsts
and several seconds and thirds. The above co. hires out
first-class ladies’ and gents’ wheels at.reasonable charges,
and does all cycle repairing with the latest approved
machinery at rock-bottom rates.*
* * » »
Dunlop tyres secured first three places in the
2-mile handicap of the Cyclists’ Union, on Sydney Cricket
Ground, July 3.*
* * * *
“Phiz.” and the “Phiz” cycle are among the
best-known of cycling names in N.S.W. Phizackerley
started his Sydney cycle business in ’B3, and has done an
ever-increasing trade ever since. Being progressive, he
went to England a very short time back, and secured all
the best of latest appliances for building bicycles. At the
Electric and Engineering Exhibition, now running in
Sydney, Phizackerley has a fine show in the bicycle-
building business. He sends his trustworthy wheels
straight from the Sydney depot, Druitt-st., to any part of
N.S.W.*

Bicycle Co. in Australia is what every prospective bicycle-
buyer should wear next his heart during the day, and
wear on his pillow during the night. There are plenty
good wheels in it, and nicely-colored men and girls.*
“ Order the main brace to be spliced !”
The princely signal’s rather rummy ;
And yet the occasion it sufficed—
A pageant admiraled by Tummy.
Legal question whisperingly discussed just
now in a certain province : Is it bribery or em-
bracery or anything in particular for a defendant
to transfer his printing work to the office of a
juryman in a successfully-defended libel-action,
immediately after the case is finished ?
Miss Flora Shaw's meddling and muddling
in Transvaal affairs is not encouraging to those who
claim for women a larger share of political power.
By the way, in his novel skit on the South African
trouble, published some months ago, St. Stead
makes Rhodes, thinly veiled under another name,
and a woman journalist play principal parts. His
Holy Nastiness once again had obtained a peep
behind the scenes.
The saddest spectacle on Jubilee night, in
Melbourne, was a “push ” of young men, 400 or
500 strong perhaps, charging through the dense
crowd, bearing a soldier on their shoulders. The
man aloft was a glittering hero who brandished
his helmet and whooped, whilst the trotting fools
below howled like fiends, and gesticulated in a
frenzy of what may have been “ loyalty,” or mere,
unreasoning excitement, but was certainly not
beer. The writer saw nothing in the whole
Jubilee idiocy so disgusting as this apotheosis of
Tommy Atkins by youths who couldn't plead
liquor as an excuse.
The Eye-sight measure per post by the in
ventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough. the
well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney, enables
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection—
long sight, short sight, old sight. Full particulars
from Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough.*
It is urged that because the N.S.W. rail-
ways, thanks to the late Mr. Eddy, are now in a
comparatively efficient condition, there is no need
to engage a railway-man of wide managerial and
up-to-date experience in succession to the dead
commissioner. In a word, it is proposed that one
of the two remaining commissioners shall take
Mr. Eddy’s place. Only one of the present com-
missioners is a railway-man at all. The late chief
did, as it were, all the actual shaving; his col-
leagues merely lathered, but, being only human,
they now reach out for the razor.
At Puysegur Point lighthouse (M.L.)
23.79 inches of rain fell in April. Near Hastings
—scene of the recent Hawkes Bay flood—the score
was 21.93 in., and the same station carried off the
record rain for a single day, Good Friday weeping
at the rate of 12.49 in.


Private raffling is becoming a nuisance in
at least one branch of N.S.W. Civil Service.
Scarcely a week passes but a dismissed or impecu-
nious civil servant raffles some article nobody
wants ; and charitable friends, who take tickets,
(ind the system as bad as another income-tax.

N.S.W.—Not Sufficient Water.
Cheerful lie from a Northern Ananias.
“I was feeding up a cow for the local show. She
was the fattest beast I ever saw, but the ticks
killed her. She died near the head of a gully, and
the fat melted and ran down the gully nearly a
quarter of a mile.”
In New York lately people thought that an
elegant Jack the Ripper had settled down to work.
The victims—in every instance women of the town
who wore jewellery—were followed to their rooms,
choked to death with short pieces of rope, in
which a peculiar slipnot was tied, and were then
robbed. But a couple of ordinary murderous
burglar ruffians were caught red-handed.
Dear Bulletin, —I think I can hail, con-
ceal, and never reveal the abracadabra of Masonry,
and still let you know that I agree with you in
your remarks on “the Masonic sham.” The
brethren are never glad to see their older brothers
with Matilda up, and I once experienced the
greatest difficulty in obtaining admission to the
installation of a Provincial Grand Master, not
because I was not known to be a Master-Mason,
which I proved myself, but because I was only
decently, not funereally, dressed. The want of an
apron was then used as a bar, but I got one out of
a pawnshop in five minutes, and saw the stamped
gilt name on the case—the name of one of the'
very men (a tyler) who proved me. But I got in,
and sat among the Grand Imperial Holy Panjan-
drums, and I am only,—S. le W.
Love really is blind. He hadn’t any
friends, was sightless, and used to sun-toast him-
self all day on a bench in the park. She sat down
beside him one day and began to talk. Then she
came every day and took to leading him home to
his lodgings when the sun waned. The touch of
her hand was pleasant, her voice was gentle and
fresh, and she diffused an air of youth and sym-
pathy around her. . . . She said “ Yes ” when he
asked her. Then one day, as she was sitting
beside him as usual, it occurred to him that people
who passed giggled, even laughed outright. It
struck him that they had been doing this for days.
Irritated, he at last asked somebody the reason
while she went to buy him an orange. And when
he heard that they grinned at seeing a negress
, sitting beside a blind white man, he got up quickly
and hobbled away. She never saw him again.

Mr. Biht n writes later: “The Pills I had from you
were of great benefit to me and others. In one case,
when three hunters and trappers were travelling about
the ranges near my place on the Rose River, one was taken
very ill ; his mates intended taking him in a waggon to
Wangarat ta (about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor.

Miserable Women.
HOW WOMEN LOSE INTEREST IN THEIR
HOUSEHOLDS.
KINDRED AILMENTS—ILLS TO WHICH
WOMEN ARE HEIR, CAUSE MUCH
MISERY—THE EXPERIENCE OF
WOMEN WHO SUFFERED, AND HOW
THEY REGAINED THEIR HEALTH.
From the Republican, Princeton, 111.
Mr. F. W. Stevens is well-known in Tiskilwa,
111. For the past six years he has conducted the
cleanest and most attractive meat market in the
city. His business is done on a large scale, and is
both retail and wholesale. He is recognised as
one of the city’s most progressive business men,
being always prominent as a leader.
Mr. Stevens says that some months ago his wife,
whose health has never been of the best, began to
show signs of an early decline, which he feared
would result seriously unless something could be
done for her. She appeared to lose the greater
part of her usual interest in the affairs of the
family and household. Mr. Stevens says he had
repeatedly cautioned her about going too long
without proper medical attendance, but she would
keep putting it off until he took the matter into
his own hands and summoned the family physician
who diagnosed her complaint as chlorosis or green
sickness. She was also troubled with coldness of
the hands and feet resulting from a very im-
poverished condition of the blood. The general
debility of the system was particularly well-
marked, and the above, coupled with tardy and
irregular periods, made Mrs. Stevens a very sick
and miserable woman,
For a space of two weeks she followed strictly
the rules laid down by her physician. At times
she felt somewhat more at ease, but there was
evidence that no improvement had commenced.
The patient, who had faithfully expected im-
mediate results from the treatment, began to fall
into fits of despondency which would sometimes
last a week or more.
Mr. Stevens says that one day he picked up a
piece of paper in his store, which, on reading,
proved to be a circular about, Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills for Pale People. “ I noticed the pills were
said to be used as a specific for all the ills woman
is heir to, and this set me to thinking. I figured
that they couldn’t do her much more harm than
the various decoctions and compounds the doctors
had prescribed for her, and if they did her no good
why there was no harm done, and no great amount
of money squandered. I sent my clerk out at once
to get a box of the Pink Pills, and that day at
supper time my wife commenced taking them.
“ At this time she was in a very enfeebled con-
dition and her stomach very weak. She would
sometimes be unable to retain even a glass of
water. The first few doses seemed to have a good
effect in soothing her at night. She slept better,
and felt and looked fresher in the morning than
she had for months. Her mental condition ap-
peared to receive fully as much beneft as her
physical, for she was more inclined to look at the
: brighter side of matters, instead of constantly
hunting out the dark features.
“ She kept right on taking the pills, and the
favorable symptoms became more marked. We
all felt as if she were on a very fair road to re-
covery. Time proved that she was, indeed, for I
do not believe you will find in the city a healthier
woman than my wife. She has no bad symptoms
whatever, she has gained in weight, and we all
know that she owes it to Dr. Williams Pink Pills
for Pale People.
“ Wait a moment, I’m not through yet, I want
to do as much for Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills as I
feel they have done for us,” said Mr. Stevens, as
the reporter, after thanking him for the courteous
attention shown, was about to take his leave.
Mr. Stevens said : “My wife had no more than
recovered when my son Verne began to look thin
and puny. He had plenty of good food, exercise
and in general, as healthy surroundings as most
of us, but that didn’t seem to help much. He had
fever and ague, and everything pointed to a
malarious condition of the system. The doctor
said that there was a poor assimilation, and did
all he could to help him.
“ We noticed no favorable results, and finally I
made up my mind that if Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
for Pale People had increased his mother’s weight
and enriched her blood, it might do the same for
him. He began taking them, and almost from
the first day, began to feel better. He always
awoke in the morning with a foul-tasting mouth.
He remarked the second or third morning after he
began taking the pills, that this had left him, and
he felt like eating his breakfast. You never saw
anyone’s recovery so rapid as that boy’s, and he
hasn’t had any return either.
“ There is nothing visionary or that looks like
guesswork about what I have told you, we know
what the remedy is, for we have, I believe, given
it as thorough a test as it ever had.
“Yes, sir, I can truthfully say that I believe
these pills, if given a fair chance, will prove a per-
manent cure for lots of diseases that people are
suffering from.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have cured numerous
cases of paralysis, locomotor ataxia, spinal disease,
rheumatism, and sciatica ; also of diseases arising
from impoverished and vitiated humors of the
blood, which cause scrofula, rickets, chronic ery-
sipelas, consumption of the bowels and lungs,
ansemia, pale and sallow complexion, general
muscular weakness, loss of appetite, palpitations,
pains in the back, nervous headache, early decay,
all forms of female weakness, and hysteria. These
Pills are not a purgative. They are genuine only
with the full name, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Palo. People, and are sold by chemists and by Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Company, Sydney, who will
forward (post paid) On receipt of stamps or post
order, one box for 2s. 9d,, or half-dozen for 14s. 9d.
They are unrivalled as a tonic for both sexes.*

! If anything can kill tight-lacing it will be
the radiograph. The Queen of Portugal, who is an
enthusiast about the new photography, has been
horrifying her court-ladies with a sight of the
effects of corseting upon their internal arrange-
ments. They begin to wonder, with Abernethy,
‘ where they’ve been stowing their livers.”
Commerce depends on two governing fac-
tors : the sale of commodities, and the receipt of
the cash (or other commodities) which they repre-
sent. In times of depression like the present, both
processes are difficult of accomplishment—esp'-
cially the latter ! But merchants who can sell
their goods need not be apprehensive o* getting
paid for them if they will hand their accounts to
the Victorian Debt - Collecting Agency (Holds-
worth & Co.), 101 Swanston-st., Melbourne.*
John M'Kenzie, the huge, blundering
Highlander whom Sedd _,n left in charge of Maori-
land affairs when he went away to jubilate,
promises to be the Nemesis of the Ministry.
M'Kenzie is a cumbersome, ill-tempered person,
who has a yearning to make speeches on all occa-
sions. Then the judicious National Ass man
prods him with irritating questions, and John
plunges about like an angry whale, and says things
that it will take Seddon all the time between now
and next general election to explain away. No
politician ever had a greater capacity than
M'Kenzie for putting both his boots in his mouth
at once, and his statements about the jobs he has
done, and his wild threats about the jobs he will
do if somebody doesn’t behave himself better,
bring joy to the bosom of the National Mule.

A rumor has arisen that there will be a
“ surplus ” from the N.S.W. railways and tram-
ways for the present financial year. It probably
only means that there will be a surplus over last
year’s earnings, not over interest and expenses,
but that is a side issue. The interesting point
is that, as soon as the word “surplus ” was men-
tioned, the Assembly rose almost as one man to
suggest ways of spending it, and the scramble
was like that of a dozen dogs on top of one bone.
The N.S.W. railways had £374,000 deficit last
year ; they have an accumulated deficit of
.£7,700,000} the Treasurer has a shortage of over
millions to pay off and is praying for any little
windfall that may come in his way to help him;
and yet as soon as one department promises to
have a small surplus, or not quite so big a deficit
as usual, there goes up a wild demand that it
should shout seed-wheat for everybody’s consti-
tuents, and generally take the whole country out
to have a drink.
Two “ Jacks ashore,” from H.M.S. Kara-
katta, were before the Cairns (N.Q.) court the
other day. One had been “dished” for
“language,” assaulting the police, and “ tear in’
me uniform,” the other was presented for “ob-
structing ” the constable “ in the execution of his
duty.” And this was his jovial patter :
Well, sir, we comes ashore an’ gets too much whisky,
an’ my mate here gets took, an’ when he gets took, bein’,
as it were, a chum o’ mine, I goes to help him. You’d
help a chum o’ yours, sir.
The court roared, and—let the pleader off. But,
when told that he might go, while his mate was
apparently to be kept until fines were met, the
briny one blurted out—“ Go ? No, not me ! I
goes along o’ my chum. If you takes him to
quod, you takes me ! ” Both tars were returned
on board a little later on. “But, sir,” said the
chumophile, “no matter what you gives us
here, we’ll get punished enough when we gets
aboard.”
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The.firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
From the “other side,” this: “Oh, the
Govt. (Q.) is all right. Ministerialists may growl,
but we know just how they’ll vote, my boy. We
know how to bring each man round. Politics is
only a matter of voting, after all. If your men
are a good lot, so much the better. If they are a
bad lot, it doesn’t matter a damn; their votes tell,
and other things happen fast enough to make
everyone forget it. There will be no change of
Ministry just yet.”

The Gold Slug .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
With sundown it became chilly, and the
door was closed. A fire in the wide fireplace and
a milk-pan of dough set to rise before it; the
father, seated under the chimney, mending har-
ness by the light of the blazing logs. Two children
doing their home lessons —Frank chanting his
four-times like a refrain ; Maggie writing her
exercise at one side of the candle; on the other
sat the mother reading the newspaper, knitting
socks, and talking at intervals. After reading
the column of “Births, Deaths and Marriages '
with the unction of an epicure she gave a sigh, for
all the names were strange to her, and turned to
the less important portion of the paper. Pre-
sently she put it down with an air of decision.
“ Really, George ! You ought to go—everyone
is going. There seems to be gold all over the
country.”
“ Why are you always harping on that, Mary ?
That’s only a yarn. We have heard it all before
any time these last 20 years.”
“ But here it is in print! Don’t you believe the
paper ? Oh, if I were only a man I’d go. Why,
if we only had half, or quarter, of what others are
getting the mortgage could be paid. Now, how
do you expect to ever pay it stopping here ? ”
The wife paused, the man was silent and looked
sceptical as he continued to bore holes for his
thread.
“ And, supposing it is only part true, there
must be a fortune for everyone who has the heart
to go. Hundreds of miles of country full of
nuggets and reefs. Think of that! And. is it
likely the papers would invent all that if it was
not true ? ”
“ Always the same song, my dear. Remember,
it is not all gold that glitters. We can make a
living here—a hard one, it’s true; still:, we can live.
And, as my old dad used to say, ‘A crust at
home is better than a cake abroad.’ We hear of
the few who succeed, not of the many who fail;
and, besides, it would want all the little money
we’ve got to take me there.” :
“ Well, I declare, George, you’ve got no pluck !”
and the mother resumed her reading with an air
of disgust, but she was interrupted by Maggie.
“Do you know what, Mum ! All the children
were telling at school to-day about Alf. Austin.
He’s come back from Coolgardie with I don’t
know how much money, and he’s going to buy the
best farm in the bottoms —Johnson’s place, you
know ; and Fknery says his father and two uncles
are going, and he is going to have a horse of his
own when they get their fortune, and he’ll let me
have a ride on it.”
“Do you hear that, George?” exclaimed the
wife; triumphantly. “ What did I tell you ? If a
ne’er-do-well like that Austin can make a fortune,
why not you ? Really I’ve no patience.”
The man’s resistance was broken, his objections
fruitless and exhausted. He felt it better to face
the evils he dreaded rather than maintain this
nightly contest.
“ As you’re so set on it, Mary, and you think
you can manage here by yourselves for a spell, I’ll
go with the Emerys when the corn’s shelled.”
* » • •
Ten months passed. The children were
playing touch round the house, when Maggie
called out: “ Here comes a swagman ! ” They all
stopped in their game to stare. A man, bent in
the shoulders and dragging his feet wearily, was
coming towards the house.
“ I believe it’s Dad,” said Frank.
“ You silly! Why, it’s an old man.”
Ernie (the baby), catching the word, toddled off
along the track, singing out “ Dada ! Dada ! ” so
that the others came racing along the path and
passed him ; then, not to be outdone, he screamed
out in his piping voice : “Dada ! Beauty’s dot a
white talf with a red tail! ”
The man smiled, almost cheerfully, as the
children surrounded him, and, taking his swag,
disputed as to who should carry it to the house.
The shouting brought the wife to the door wiping
her hands from soapsuds. She gave one long,
astonished look at the man.
“ Well, Mary ! ”
“Oh, George, is that you?” There was a
choking in her voice as she kissed him on the
cheek. It was little Ernie who broke the silence :
“ Oh, Mums ! Dada’s dot flour on his head.”
“Yes, Sonny,” said the father with a grim
smile, “ I’ve got flour on my head right enough,
and what’s more, it will never brush off.”
The wife bustled about, and soon had him in a
chair. Kneeling down she took off the boots from
his tired feet.
“ Well, I hardly knew you, you look 20 years
older.”
“So I am, my dear; not 20 but a hundred.”
“We have not heard from you for four months.”
“ Been in the hospital; only left it to come
back.”
“ And the Emeries ? ”
“ Beth buried there,” and the man pointed with
his hand towards the west. “ I’m the only one of
us the fever did not kill. But I’ve got gold,” and
he took a slug the size of a bean from his pocket
and placed it on the table. “ There is what I
have got for my health and a hundred pounds. I
thought I’d bring it home. It might make you
another wedding-ring,” he added, trying to joke,
“if it was beaten out very thin.”
The woman did not regard the gold ; her eyes
dwelt on the man’s face with a wondering agony,
noting the lines and wrinkles strange to her. The
baby’s eyes were more active. Unnoticed, Ernie
took the slug off the table, and running with it to
the garden began to play with it.
The wife’s eyes were full of tears, her voice full
of chiding.
“ Oh, why did you go? If you had only listened
to me. I told you how it would be. To think of
a married man at your time of life leaving his
home to go to that horrid place. I knew those
papers were no better than a pack of lies. Better
a orust at home than a cake abroad.” She moved
about the room briskly getting a : meal ready, the
back of her hand going to her eyes every now and
then; “It’s not all gold that glitters—if you’d
only, listened to me and not been led away by all
those foolish yarns. ” .
He stood up to draw his chair to the table, and
she noticed now, more than at first, how the once
strong man was bent and emaciated. Sobs choked
her voice. “My God I How you must have suf-
fered. Why ever did you go ? ”
The baby gave a cry from the verandah, “ I
tan’t find it.”
“What have you lost, Ernie?” asked Maggie.
“ Dada’s ikkee dold marble’s dome, dome away.
I tan’t find it.”
N.S.W.
HENRY FLETCHER.
A witness, who was once a manufacturer
in England, told Vic. Commission on Old-Age
Pensions that his experience is that workmen who
drink too much are by no means worthless crea-
tures. On the contrary, he says, the drinking
man is usually the best workman, and earns the
best wages when he works at all.
Westralian Govt. * longs to tackle the
scheme of making Fremantle the first Australian
port of call for English mail steamers. The large
shipping companies are hard to convince of the
safety of the new harbor, and their objections
must be overcome before the agitation can be com-
menced in real earnest. Albany and the southern
districts will offer lively objection.
“ I wasgoingup St. James-st. about4a.m.,
the other day,” writes a Bulletin correspondent
from London, “and passing a church saw a
placard suspended from one of the pillars—“ This
church is now open for rest, meditation, and
prayer.” Leaning against the door, with his head
sunk upon his heart, was a well-dressed Johnnie,
dead-drunk. He had been deposited there to
“rest ” by some convivial companions.
Thus Alfred Walker on colony :
“ The social life is pleasant and natural, and
numbers are increasing every month. The work
is hard —hard labor to grind corn, pit-saw to cut
timber, wooden rollers and horse to crush sugar-
cane. Neither wheat-flour nor beef more than two
or three times a year; pork once a month,
fruit and vegetables abundant.” Goes on to
testify that New Australia is a dead failure. By
the way, Alfred owns a little coffee - plantation
there, run on purely individualistic principles.
The public school authorities of Australia
should agree to a uniform age for the admission of
children to State schools. The statutory school
age for admission in four provinces is six years,
and in three (taking Maoriland in the group) seven.
Circumstances, domestic and industrial, warrant
uniformity in this matter. The statutory age
for quitting school is 14 in some provinces where
the entrance is fixed at six, and 13 in others
where the entrance is at seven. Thus the statu-
tory age in N.S.W. is from 6 to 14, and in S. Aus-
tralia from 7 to 13.
“ Derx,” from Kalgoorlie, to The Bulle-
tin :
Be that stupid acrostio in Perth Table Talk. It is
ancient, so very ancient that I wonder its very odor
didn’t betray its initial offence. Its first acquaintance I
made in a Vic. mining township in ’74, when, as a boy, I
was shown the lines by a sort of professional eccentric
(bellman, bill-poster, and what not), who knew (or in-
vented) a history of it something like this (time and
place lost in the mists of oblivion) : —Rival editors —un-
dying hatred —Ha ! ha 1 R-r-revenge !! —This piece of dirt
concocted—sent to rival editor—printed—law proceedings
—ruin ! Villainy triumphant ! ! The second (and last)
time I saw it—or, rather, the first few lines of it —was in
a Coolgardie paper, with some comments, quite in Dead
Bird vein, which were simply an aggravation of the
original offence. Strange, a thing like that living for
nearly a quarter of a century, don’t you think?
Jim and Bill: A Moral .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Jim and Bill were mates —at least, they travelled
together, and, when possible, worked together in
the season ; but they were opposite in character,
as most friends are.
Jim, the sober, steady man, invariably
“camped” out the slack, handy to some small
western town, and so made his season’s earnings
keep him in bare necessaries—bread, meat, and
tea—vegetables were unattainable luxuries to the
man who had to make about three months’ earn-
ings pay for nine months’ compulsory idleness.
Bill, the reckless, improvident one, as regularly
“ blued ” his cheque in the handiest coast-town,
and as regularly tramped back to the hospitality
of the provident and thrifty Jim, who kept him
till they resumed work in the season.
Well, Bill still “ carries on,” and when sober, is
just an ordinary live man. . . Jim? Oh!
Jim’s in the lunatic asylum—lost his reason
through incessant and unrelieved contemplation
of the exhilarating monotony of invariable same-
ness.
Westerners are mainly Jims and Bills —mostly
Bills.
C.A.J.T.
A church - bazaar is selling pill - boxes
labelled “ Bachelors’ pills—one to be taken in a
life-time.” When opened the “pill” is found
to be a miniature doll dressed as a bride. These
boxes sell like iced lager in the dog-days to simple
maidens anxious to convey a delicate hint to a
bashful lover.
Writes “ W.W.”
In lieu of the old rationing-out system, so objection-
able to station-hands, the ever-oblitring squatters in this
(Gilgunnia) district have substituted the following: —
The is charged (at station rates, of course) for all
stores supplied, and at the end of each quarter is credited
with £2 15s. —called “ ration allowance.” If the store-
account exceeds that amount, it is deducted from the
magnificent screw of 15s. per week. No wonder pas-
toralists need assistance in time of drought.
A Melb. wine merchant recklessly adver-
tised that he would give a glass of wine to every
caller during Jubilee week, and a sportive police-
man carefully cut out the notice and pasted it in
the prisoners’ “waiting-room” at Melb. City
Court. The result was—ye gods ! “I never had
any idea before that such disreputable men as
that read the papers,” said the broken-spirited
wine-seller to his clerk at the close of the first
day.
From the cablegram describing the presence
of “ all that was left ” of the famous Light Brigade,
at the Jubilee procession in London, it would ap-
pear that they who fought so well for Queen and
country have, at last, “ got their reward.”
Together stood that grey-haired band,
To watch the Queen pass by,
And. soon the Lady of the Land
Had “ caught them in her eye ; ”
She bowed with gracious queenly style,
With feelings truly deep,
And smiled a “Gracious Sovereign” smile
Because—to smile is cheap !

The Two Keys.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There was a Boy, long years ago,
Who hour by hour awake would lie,
And watch the white moon gliding slow
Along her pathway in the sky.
And ev’ry night as thus he lay
Entranced in lonely phantasy,
Borne swiftly on a bright moon-ray
There came to him a Golden Key.
And with that Golden Key the Boy
Oped ev’ry night a magic door,
That to a melody of Joy
Turned on its hinges evermore.
Then, trembling with delight and awe,
When he the charmed threshold crossed,
A radiant corridor he saw—
Its end in dazzling distance lost.
Great windows shining in a row
Lit up the wondrous corridor,
And each its own rich light did throw
In stream resplendent on the floor.
One window showed the Boy a scene
Within a forest old and dim,
Where Fairies danced upon the greeM
And kissed their little hands to him.
Sweet strains of elfin harp and horn
He heard so clearly sounding there,
And he to Wonderland was borne
And breathed its soft enchanted air.
Then, passing onward with the years,
He turned his back on Elf and Fay,
And, sadly sweet, as if in tears,
The Fairy Music died away.
The second window held him long:
It looked upon a field of fight
Whereon the countless hordes of Wrong
Fought fiercely with the Friends of Right.
And, 10, upon that fateful field
Where cannon thundered, banners streauud
And rushing squadrons rocked and reeled,
His sword a star of battle gleamed.
And when the hordes of Wrong lay still,
And that great fight was fought and won,
He stood, bright-eyed, upon a hill,
His white plume shining in the sun.
A glorious vision ! Yet behind
He left it with its scarlet glow,
And faint and far upon the wind
He heard the martial trumpets blow.
For to his list’ning ear was borne
A music more entrancing far
Than strains of elfin harp or horn,
More thrilling than the tramp of war.
No longer as a dreamy boy
He trod the radiant corridor:
His young man’s heart presaged a joy
More dear than all the joys of yore.
To that third window, half in awe,
He moved, and slowly raised his eyes—
And was it Earth grown young he saw ?
Or was it man’s lost Paradise ?
For all the flowers that ever bloomed
Upon the earth, and all the rare
Sweet Loveliness by Time entombed
Seemed blushing, blooming, glowing there.
And every mellow-thmated bird
That ever sang the trees among
Seemed singing there, with one sweet word
“ Love ! Love ! ” on every little tongue.
Then he by turns grew rosy-red,
And he by turns grew passion-pale.
“Sweet Love ! ” the lark sang overhead,
“ Sweet Love ! ” sang Love’s own nightingale.
In mid-heart of the hawthorn-tree
The thrush sang all its buds to bloom ;
“ Love ! Love ! Love ! Love ! Sweet Love,” sang
he
Amidst the soft green sun-flecked gloom.
She stood upon a lilied lawn,
With dreamful eyes that gazed afar :
A maiden tender as the Dawn,
And lovely as the Morning Star.
She stooped and kissed him on the brow,
And in a low sweet voice said she :
“ I am this country's queen—and thou ? ”
“I am thy vassal,” murmured he.
She hid him with her hair gold-red,
That flowed like sunshine to her knee ;
She kissed him on the lips, and said :
“Dear heart ! I’ve waited long for thee.”
And, oh, she was so fair, so fair,
So gracious was her beauty bright,
Around her the enamoured air
Pulsed tremulously with delight.
All sounds did then in music melt,
Bird-voices, scent of flower and tree,
And he within his bosom felt
The piercing thorn of ecstasy.
The years passed by in dark and light,
In storm and shine ; the man grew old,
Yet never more by day or night
There came to him the Key of Gold.
But ever ere the great sun-flowers
In gold above the sky’s blue rim,
All in the dark and lonely hours,
There comes an Iron Key to him.
And with that key he opes a wide
And gloomy door—the Door of Fate—
That makes whene’er it swings aside
A music sad and desolate.
A music sad from saddest source :
He sees beside the doorway set
The chill grey figure of Remorse,
The cold dead image of Regret.
For all the glory and the glow
Of Life are passed, and dead, and gone :
The Light and Life of Long Ago
Are only memories, moonlight-wan.
There is no man of woman born
So good, so great, so brave but he
Must in the middle night forlorn
Take up and use the Iron Key.
VICTOR DALEY.
“ Pomare’s Death.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Of Hongi ika, the fiery ravager; of Hongi katan-
gata, the eater of men, many songs have been sung,
for he was the greatest chief in Maoriland when
the pakehas first came here to hunt whales : but
ot Pomare, Hongi’s fighting captain, little is
Known, and only the manner of his death is told
in song. Yet Pomare was a great warrior; so
huge of bulk that the ground shook under his
trea-d ; so skilful in war that no man could stand
against him, and he it was who fought by Hongi’s
side and trod the path of victory from Whan-
gs™l to the region of the Hot Lakes; from the
Kaiapara to the last stronghold of the Waikatos
at the head of the Waipa. Men feared Pomare •
women hated him ; and Panui cursed him night
and day, for Pomare had destroyed his pah and
slam Apia, of the terrible kiss, and it is of Panui
as much as of Pomare that the song is sung
When Hongi died Pomare was made chief, and
in his cruelty he resolved to march over the
country that Hongi had ravaged, for some of the
tribes had gathered together once more, and had
rebuilt the pahs. Now, among the tribes none
had recovered so quickly as Panui’s, and none
were so flourishing; and when Pomare heard of
this he remembered how once he had crushed
them to the earth, and he led his warriors South-
ward, sending messengers ahead to warn Panui of
his coming, and commanding him to offer himself
and his people as slaves. When Panui received
this command he held council with his tribe and
urged them to meet Pomare and fight the Nga-
puhis saying that it was better to die fighting
than live as slaves, and the men of the tribe cried
one and all, “ We will fight to the end.”
Then Panui left the chiefs in the council-house
and went to the whare of his son, a mere youth.
Ireta, ho said, “thou hast heard the song of
tny mother Apia s kiss, and how the Ngapuhi
who held her captive was slain. Thou knowest
how the Ngapuhis, under Hongi, destroyed our
pah, and how Pomare burned alive Apia, my wife
because she fought with the spear of that Ngapuhi
to whom she gave the kiss of death. Listen, then,
the Ngapuhis come once more against us, and
Pomare is at their head, and no man can stand
against Pomare; our only hope of victory lies in
Pomare s death. Heed now—l am an old man, I
am the chief of the tribe, I will engage Pomare in
battle, I will take his spear into my heart, but be
thou near, and while Pomare is slaying me do
thou thrust over my shoulder and slay him.”
So Ireta sharpened his spear and practised
thrusting over a man’s shoulder daily until the
Ngapuhis drew near and the battle began.
The Ngapuhis, in their pride, treated their foes
with disdain, and Pomare stood before his warriors
and shouted “Who will fight with me?” and
Panui rushed forward and cried: “I will fight
thee, Pomare, burner of Apia.” Pomare laughed
and replied : “ Thou art already dead 1 ”
* ‘ Nay ; but thou ! big and cruel, ” and he struck
at Pomare with his taiaha, and at this signal all
Panui’s warriors rushed on to battle.
Pomare played with his foe, and Panui defended
himself until he heard Ireta cry behind him: “I
am ready, father ! ” Then the old man lifted his
guard and received Pomare’s spear in his chest.
He seized it with his hands and stooped for-
ward, and as he stooped Ireta thrust over his
father’s shoulder and struck Pomare a fatal blow;
and when Pomare fell the Ngapuhis gave way and
fled. Thus a father’s sacrifice won a victory, and
revenged the death of Apia.
Maoriland.
F. ROLLETT.
A Westralian Newspaper Report.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A brilliant coterie last evening assembled at the
recently-erected handsome edifice known as the
Hungry-quartz Hotel, at the invitation of Mr. Mc-
Umty, the genial landlord. No expense has been
spared in the erection, which compares favorably
with any of our first-class city hotels —in fact, to
ensure satisfaction, Mr. McGinty was his own
contractor, and built it himself during his spare
hours.
The picturesque corrugated-iron walls of the
bar are decorated with artistic and costly frames,
C °Tor^ )aSS ' ll^- o^ eo^ra P^ 8 excee ding rich coloring
Mr. McGinty has ever been a patron of the fine-
arts. The room is brilliantly lighted throughout
with kerosene lamps. The two large 6xB rooms
at the rear are to be devoted solely to the use of
the popular landlord and family. It shows a
great confidence in our resources when capitalists
are willing to invest in such a truly regal manner.
Several gentlemen mostly engaged in under-
ground pursuits comprised the house-warming
Party Mr. McGinty, after a happy welcoming
speech, asked the company to drink success to the
house, which. request was acceded to with perfect
bon hommie in beakers of “ shypoo.”
“Two bob in and the winner shouts” was the
next item on the programme. The winner—Mr.
Stumpy Grumps, manager of the Slime Pits De-
partment of the Call Due G.M. Co., Ltd. —grace-
fully asked Mr. McGinty to now join his guests.
That gentleman smilingly bowed his acknowledg-
ment to the compliment paid him.
Mr. Scratchpen, the talented representative of
the Hoolgarlie Bustler, proposed the toast of
Mr. McGinty, coupled with the “ Queen ” and
the mining industry.
Mr. McGinty, in responding, said he was
pleased to have his name coupled with the Queen
on such an occasion as the Jubilee celebration
year. It was a time when all differences should
be sunk. From that moment he would sink all
resentment against Her Majesty, with whom he
had been on bitter terms for many years past. He
was content to let bygones be bygones. lie would
ask the guests to again fill their glasses.
Mr. Scratchpen was courteously conducted to
his cab by several of the company, and carefully
packed away in the bottom of the vehicle. The
act of vandalism of stealing the front-door, smash-
ing the window, and wrecking the rear of the
building, which ocourred later on in the evening,
is attributed to a low gang of ruffians from the
Other Side who have been frequenting the neigh-
borhood lately.
